Foreword 
For the last two decades the loss of, in particular, tropical rainforest has alarmed the public in 
the developed parts of the world. The debate has been characterised by a lack of understand-
ing of the causes and effects of the process, leading to the prevailing reaction being unquali-
fied condemnation. Such attitude has even been observed among scientists, claiming suprem-
acy to biodiversity conservation. Many scientific analyses are available, but the basis for so-
ber debates and appropriate actions is still highly insufficient. Two recent international initia-
tives! will hopefully lead to improved knowledge of deforestation and forest degradation as 
they recognise the need for studies to critically investigate those issues. This book will pro-
vide useful input to the initiatives. 
In my opinion, the scientific analyses have not sufficiently promoted the understanding that 
the fate of tropical forests is first and foremost a concern of the governments of the countries 
in which the forests are situated. Tropical forests may be important to the global environment 
and their rich biodiversity may be a human heritage. But their main importance is their poten-
tial contribution to improving livelihood in the countries in question. 
Most scientific analyses do not immediately contribute to such understanding because their 
scope is narrow. They often deal with specific technical questions, e.g. how to carry out re-
duced impact logging, or investigate ecological or silvicultural issues, e.g. dynamics of a cer-
tain forest type or the sustainable felling intensity of certain tree species. More recently, social 
questions have entered the scene, e.g. the participation of a local community in the manage-
ment of its forest resources. I do not try to devalue such studies. They are necessary, but sel-
dom thrown in a national context relief. 
The issue of deforestation or forest degradation in the tropics is extremely complex. Many 
different forces are simultaneously at work, e.g. of cultural, economic and political nature. 
Some recent scientific analyses acknowledge this and try to elucidate the intricate pattern of 
causes and effects2. However, most of these analyses are general in the sense that they deal 
with the issue in an abstract or overall way, even though some restrict themselves to a certain 
region. There is a conspicuous lack of in-depth and comprehensive case studies, i.e. studies 
that deal with a specific country. To the best of my knowledge, only two such studies have 
recently been published: Wunder (2000)3 and the present study. 
This study provides an in-depth analysis of the national-level forest policy and economic 
forces behind the development of high forest management in Ghana in the period 1991-1999, 
tracing the development back to 1986 or even further. Ghana is one of the most advanced 
countries in terms of natural high forest utilisation, but has not reached a state of sustainable 
forest and timber management. 
The study is based on literature, semi-structured interviews and direct field observations. It is 
impressive how much unpublished literature the author was provided access to - key persons 
have shown him an uncommon confidence that he does not abuse even if making many frank 
statements. Similar confidence is demonstrated in interviews on sensitive subjects. It goes 
I Inter-governmental Forum on Forests 1999: Valuation of Forest Goods and Services: Report of the Secretary 
General. United Nations Commission on Sustainable Development, New York, Report E/CN.l7IIFFI1999.12. 
World Commission on Forests and Sustainable Development (WCFSD) 1999: Our Forests Our Future. Cam-
bridge University Press, Cambridge. 
2 For example, Brown, K. and D. Pearce (eds.) 1994: The Causes of Tropical Deforestation. The Economic and 
Statistical Analysis of Factors Giving Rise to the Loss of Tropical Forests. University of London Press, London. 
o Wunder, S. 2000: The Economics of Deforestation. The Example of Ecuador, Macmillan Press Ltd., London.

--- Page 10 ---

without saying that the study benefits significantly from that - in this respect the study has 
similarities to that carried out by Judge Barnett in Papua New Guinea4, however on a volun-
tary basis. Also impressive is the comprehensive data material, much of which has been far 
from easily available. Further, the author demonstrates great acquaintance with not only Gha-
naian forestry but also with society and culture in general. 
The subject of the study is complex. Technical aspects, e.g. forest inventory, must be analysed 
for interpreting economic data - which again must be investigated from a policy point of 
view. Competent analyses are made of the national forest inventories, demonstrating design 
errors and unclear objectives that for a long time produced data about the standing volume 
and growth of timber species which was either over-optimistic or of limited value in a forest 
policy context. Among the economic analyses made, two should be emphasised: (i) Ghana 
appears to be foregoing substantial export revenue earnings by promoting the export of value 
added products, and (ii) Economic incentives in the forest sector have kept domestic log 
prices artificially low and opened the opportunity for log processing companies to earn wind-
fall profits, cover high informal costs, and operate profitably at very low recovery rates. 
Among the main findings of the study is that in Ghana trees outside forest reserves are the key 
to sustainable forest and timber resource management in the future. However, despite recent 
legislative reforms emphasis remains on control of the off-reserve timber exploitation rather 
than on creating incentives for increasing the off-reserve timber production. It is questioned 
whether this approach, in combination with an attempt to control the domestic timber market, 
will actually work when in the foreseeable future the supply of raw logs cannot meet the 
combined export and domestic demand. In the author's opinion, "the obvious alternative, 
over-exploitation of the remaining resource, will definitely be a risk." 
Many of Ghana's past and present forestry related problems reflect rather general issues of 
managing tropical high forests, and by investigating these problems the study may prove use-
ful in national forest management planning in other tropical countries. And being a thorough 
and well-documented analysis should make the study interesting to politicians and forest sec-
tor stakeholders in and outside Ghana, to the international development assistance commu-
nity, and to students and scholars in the field of tropical forestry. 
FINNHELLES 
Professor of Forest Economics and Policy 
Royal Veterinary and Agricultural University, Denmark

Guinea Timber Industry. The Ecologist 20(5): 174-181.

Preface 
My sincere thanks for providing funding for this study go to the Danish Research Academy and 
Danida. I am pleased to return these institutions' belief in what was then a PhD research 
proposal with this publication of the results. In this respect I am also indebted to the two 
anonymous peer reviewers ofKluwer Academic Publishers. 
During my collection of data in Ghana, I was at all levels met by friendly open-minded people 
who from the outset gave me access to a wealth of information and took time (and pains) to 
answer my endless line of questions. For this remarkable support, without which the study 
would probably not have been possible, I would like to warmly thank:

Executive Summary 
The present study investigates developments of high forest management in Ghana during the 
period 1991-99. Focus is on national level forest policy and economics. Overall, the study 
findings may be summarised by three main points: 
• 
The re-vitalisation of an export oriented timber industry during the early 1980s, has led to 
an undermining of its own resource base. However, design errors and unclear (political) 
objectives of national forest inventories, which were carried out during the period 1985-97, 
for a long time produced data about the standing volume and growth of timber species, 
which was either over-optimistic or of limited value in a forest policy context. This, in 
combination with strong financial interests to maximise timber export revenues, gave the 
timber industry the benefit of the doubt when sustainability of the recorded timber harvest 
was questioned. 
• 
The package of (i) restrictions on log exports, (ii) forest taxes and fees and (iii) the 
modalities of granting rights to standing timber has, until 1999 , produced rather unfortunate 
incentives. It has (i) created an over-capacity in the timber industry, (ii) resulted in massive 
official revenues foregone and (iii) promoted a notorious over-harvest of the most valuable 
timber species. Poor felling control has failed to counteract the latter effect. 
• 
Trees outside forest reserves are the key to sustainable forest and timber resources 
management in the future. Yet, recent legislative reforms offorest taxation and modalities 
of granting rights to standing trees indicate that decision-makers and the official 
administration maintain an anachronistic (colonial) attitude to the management of 
renewable natural resources. An unfortunate official and constitutional preoccupation with 
extraction of revenue from commercial harvest of naturally occurring trees off-reserves is 
undermining financial incentives for off-reserve timber production by farmers and land 
owning communities who are the real managers of such trees. 
The Timber Resource and Harvest 
Commercial utilisation of timber from the high forest zone was strongly promoted by the World 
Bank and the IMF during the Economic Recovery and Export Rehabilitation Programmes in the 
mid 1980s. In reality, the timber export was rehabilitated with little technical knowledge about 
the resource base although the then Chief Conservator of Forests, J. Francois often tried to raise 
the issue of matching the capacity of the fast growing timber industry with the production 
capacity of the country's forest resource. 
A first attempt to estimate the annual allowable cut (AAC) from forest reserves was made by 
Silviconsult (1985). Based on rather meagre growth and inventory data the sustainable on-reserve 
harvest was estimated to be around 1.1 million m3 per year. Later this proved too optimistic. In 
fairness, it should be mentioned that Silviconsult (1985; v) at the time did emphasise that 
knowledge about the resource was insufficient and incomplete for efficient planning and 
utilisation. 
In 1985 the British Government decided to fund a forest inventory in Ghana's high forest zone, 
the Forest Inventory Project (FIP), which ran from 1985 to 1989. The mechanisms of identifying 
this project are unclear, but it appears likely that the possible negative effects of rehabilitating the 
timber industry were increasingly becoming a matter of concern in ODA (now DFID). 
Unfortunately, FIP suffered from a lack of clearly and consistently defined objectives which 
ended up confusing the required level of sampling intensity. It was clear that estimation of a 
xiii

--- Page 13 ---

national AAC from forest reserves should be produced, but whether inventory results were 
supposed to be used at management unit level remained unclear. 
As a result, the static inventory design, which involved measurement of 1,332 one ha temporary 
sample plots, ended up yielding overly accurate estimates of the standing volume at the national 
level, but rather inaccurate estimates at forest management unit level. Acceptable national level 
estimates of standing volumes above 110 cm diameter at breast hight (1.3 m above ground level) 
for the 55 species that had been exported during the period 1973-88 (referred to as FIP class 1 
species) could have been produced by measuring only around 120 plots (Flint and Hardcastle, 
1993; 19). 
Although the national level static inventory results ofFIP appeared statistically very accurate, the 
volume available for harvest was actually over-estimated because the area of forest suitable for 
timber production was not sufficiently investigated. Accordingly, the area of production forest 
was over-estimated by at least 260,000 ha (see below). Indeed, it is surprising and criticisable that 
the logical sequence, of first identifying areas suitable for logging and then undertaking a timber 
inventory for these areas only, was not followed by the FIP. 
The static inventory under FIP also failed to take another politically rather obvious issue into 
account. It was never investigated how much afthe recorded timber harvest originated from areas 
outside forest reserves. Later TIED (1993; 15) could reveal that more than 50% of the annual 
harvest actually came from off-reserve areas. Had this been realised at an earlier stage, it is likely 
that an off-reserve timber inventory had formed part of the PIP design. 
An AAC of 1.5 million m3 from forest reserves was estimated and published by FIP. However, 
this estimate, like the one produced by Silviconsult (1985), also suffered from inadequate primary 
growth data. In reality, the 1.5 million m3 per year was mainly the result of a theory based 
computer simulation. In addition, this simulation assumed the area of production forest to be 1.3 
million ha, instead of the 1.16 million ha used to generate the static inventory results. Given the 
available growth data, it was clearly over-ambitious by FIP to assume it would be possible to 
produce a scientifically sound estimate of the AAC. 
Surprisingly, the computer simulated AAC of 1.5 million m3 was, under FIP, never related to the 
actual principles of selecting trees for harvest within forest reserves. During the FIP, the "Interim 
Yield Allocation Formula" was developed. This formula says Z = 0.2X + 0.5Y, where Z is the 
number of trees above felling limit allocated for felling, Y is the number of trees above felling 
limit and X is the number of trees in the diameter class (20 cm) immediately below felling limit. 
The formula was applied on each tree species within a 120 ha compartment, with the intension 
to ensure that, after a felling cycle of 40 years, the number of trees above felling limit would be 
re-established. Later, Z = 0.2X + 0.5Y was named the "Normal Formula" because the "Reduced 
Formula", Z = 0.2X + 0.25Y was eventually applied for over-harvested species in all vegetation 
zones and for all species in fire prone areas. 
Had the Normal Formula been applied on the national level static inventory data for FIP class 1 
species, an estimated allowed annual cut of 0.94 million m3, or only 62% of the computer 
simulated AAC, would have been the result. This was, however, never done. Instead, FIP' s rather 
ill informed policy analysis concluded that the then recorded annual harvest of 1.14 million m3 
stayed within the AAC. 
xiv

--- Page 14 ---

In 1988, the FIP was, in practice, continued under a new name, the Forest Inventory and 
Management Project (FIMP). This project "completed" the forest inventory such that, during the 
period 1988-94, a total of 3,101 one ha plots had been sampled from 128 forest reserves. In 
addition, the FIMP finally began to make use of collected information about site conditions within 
the temporary sample plots, such that the total on-reserve area suitable for protection and for 
timber production, respectively could be estimated. On this basis, the extent of possible hill 
sanctuaries, swamp sanctuaries, special biological protection sites, shelter belts, intact forest 
sanctuaries, provenance protection sites, fire protection zones, convalescence forest and 
conversion forest was estimated. It would have been better (and more logical) to define and make 
direct area measurements of such areas on the basis of maps and field visits. Yet, as a second best 
solution, the definition of forest categories and area estimates based on sampling data, must be 
considered sufficient for policy level considerations. 
Depending on how many of the above categories are set aside for protection and how strictly 
logging, for environmental reasons, is controlled, this realistically leaves a production forest area 
between 0.65 million ha (fine-grained forest protection) and 0.76 million ha (large-grained forest 
protection). Quite a reduction in comparison with Silviconsult's (1985) and FIP's production 
forest estimates of 1.18 and 1.16 million ha, respectively. 
According to rarity and harvest intensity, FIMP divided commercial timber species into three 
groups. Scarlet, Red and Pink Star species with Scarlet Stars being those under highest harvest 
pressure. Furthermore, FIMP finally abolished the rather fruitless attempts to estimate the on-
reserve AAC through analyses of the meagre primary data on forest growth. Instead, the Normal 
and the Reduced Yield Allocation Formulae were applied on the FIMP static inventory data. 
Table E.l summarises the FIMP estimated AACs from forest reserves.

It is seen from table E.l that the previously estimated on-reserve AACs of 1.1 and 1.5 million m3 
were much too optimistic. Had the FIP been designed more carefully, the figures in table E.l

The general message from table E.2 is clear. Scarlet Star species are being over-harvested, 
whereas the harvest of Red and Pink Stars could, in general, be increased substantially. In other 
words, the problem of sustainability is that market forces are creating an excess harvest of certain 
(the commercially most valuable) species. Luckily, other species are, however, "under-utilised". 
This represents an option for maintaining or even increasing the total on-reserve harvest, provided 
the present species composition of the harvest could be changed. Yet, when one looks at the off-
reserve harvest and inventory data, the picture becomes more gloomy. Table E.3 summarises an 
off-reserve resource lifetime scenario under a continuation of the 1996 recorded timber extraction 
off-reserves.

xvi

--- Page 16 ---

It is seen that Scarlet Star species make up the lion's share (63%) ofthe off-reserve harvest, while 
this group represents the smallest resource base. Furthermore, Scarlet Star species are, within the 
coming 40 years, likely to become economically extinct off-reserves, unless the harvesting pattern 
is radically changed. 
Tables E.2 and E.3 taken together show that the total recorded 1996 timber extraction was 
approximately 1.1 million m3 of which Scarlet Star species accounted for approximately 0.7 
million m3 or 63%. It is, in other words, clear that the timber industry is heavily dependent on 
Scarlet Star species. The FTh1P data has finally made it equally clear that the resulting harvesting 
pattern is distinctly unsustainable for this group of species. 
An analysis of the wood export reveals that the annual harvest for export during the period 1986-
96 was approximately 0.8 million m3. Of this, approximately 0.6 million m3 (75%) was Scarlet 
Star species with Red and Pink Star species making up approximately 0.14 and 0.06 million m3, 
respectively. The documented over-exploitation of Scarlet Star species is, therefore, to a large 
extent export driven, and a rather unfortunate (but foreseeable) result of the abovt; mentioned 
Export Rehabilitation Programme. Accordingly, the much praised increase in annual timber 
export revenue from approximately DM 110 million in 1986 to DM 220 million in 1996 has had 
a serious "cost-side" to it. Depletion of the stock of Scarlet Star species. 
A comparison between the estimated harvest for export and the recorded extraction of timber 
from on- and off-reserve areas indicates that the annual domestic timber consumption (excluding 
charcoal and firewood) is at least 0.7 million m3 roundwood equivalents. In the near future this 
figure might increase to approximately one million m3• Together with and an export-driven 
harvest demand of about 0.8 million m3 roundwood equivalents, this gives an annual timber 
demand of some 1.8 million m3 round wood equivalents. Since the on-reserve AAC is only 
between 0.8 and 0.9 million m3, this comprises a serious problem. Naturally, the domestic timber 
demand is likely to be satisfied either legally or illegally, and the timber industry will obviously 
try very hard to maintain its export earnings. Meanwhile, off-reserve timber resources are being 
steadily depleted. 
Accordingly, the national timber inventory results, the AAC estimates and analyses ofthe timber 
harvest, point to two core policy priorities: 
• 
Diversifying the timber harvest on-reserves 
• 
creating a sustained production of timber off-reserves. 
The Economics of Timber Exploitation 
Past regulatory mechanisms and financial incentives for harvesting different timber species 
obviously play an important role in understanding why the timber harvest has become 
unsustainable. Since 1962, the Government has, both on- and off-reserves, regulated access to 
standing timber through a system of concessions or concession-like arrangements (allocation of 
long- and short-term rights to standing timber). In this process rights to standing timber have 
never been allocated according to a system of competitive bidding. Largely, the official costs of 
acquiring rights to standing timber have been and still are confined to the costs of processing 
applications. Yet, the Government is officially influencing domestic timber prices through: 
• 
Timber royalties 
• 
stumpage fees (substituted royalties in December, 1998) 
xvii

--- Page 17 ---

• 
export levies 
• 
forest fees and other taxes 
• 
log export bans. 
Other factors, which, in addition to the official charges, influence the domestic log prices are: 
• 
FOB export prices on wood products 
• 
costs of logging and further processing 
• 
informal payments. 
The resource conserving effect of forest fees and taxes has been almost negligible. In general, 
they have been too low and differed too little among species to produce any significant financial 
incentive, for holders of timber rights, to harvest Pink or Red rather than Scarlet Star species. In 
theory, this could have been checked through strict felling control, but a glance at the official 
figures for timber extraction from forest reserves proves that this has not been the case. Direct 
inquirations into the practicalities of timber harvest in forest reserves also show that, especially 
for Scarlet Star species, under-sized trees and higher volumes than the yield selection formulae 
prescribe have been extracted. Furthermore, there is evidence that this has happened (and may 
still be going on) as a result of collusion between forestry officials and timber companies. 
In combination with a progressive restriction on log exports, which began in 1979 by the banning 
of log exports for 14 species and became complete in 1995, the low forest fees and taxes have 
kept domestic log prices at 20-50% of FOB log prices. In this way, domestic wood processing has 
been heavily subsidised, because export levies on processed wood have always been and still are 
quite low. Furthermore, it has created a significant over-capacity in the wood industry. Current 
estimates suggest that the processing capacity of the industry is around 3 million m3 annually, 
which compares rather unfavourably with the on-reserve AAC. 
An investigation of the actual willingness to pay for standing timber has show that, until 1999, 
this was significantly higher than forest taxes and fees. As a result, DM 20 million or more in 
official forest revenue was annually foregone during the period 1993-96. Accordingly, huge sums 
have been available for informal payments, windfall profits and keeping inefficient industries 
financially afloat. Considering the magnitude of funds being available for informal payments, it 
is no wonder that the procedures for allocating timber concessions completely messed up in the 
early 1990s. 
From the beginning of 1999, the system of forest fees and taxes was finally reformed. The main 
achievement of this reform has been that timber royalties were substituted by stumpage fees, 
which almost equalled the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber. This has significantly 
narrowed the financial room for windfall profits and informal payments. However, in 
combination with the log export ban, the new forest fees and taxes will still keep domestic log 
prices far below the world market level. 
Development of the domestic timber industry may, due to the Government controlled "prices" 
of standing timber, mainly be pursued through a gradual increase of forest taxes and fees. The 
industry cannot be considered fully "developed" before the difference between domestic and 
world market log prices is close to zero. Future adjustments (increases) of stumpage fees and 
other forest taxes should, therefore, be assessed against their effect on domestic log prices. In 
addition, the difference in forest fees and taxes between Scarlet, Red and Pink Star species should 
xviii

--- Page 18 ---

be maintained or increased in order to conserve over-utilised and increase the harvest of "under-
utilised" species. 
If the current harvest for export should be maintained and the expected domestic demand for 
timber should be satisfied on a sustainable basis, there is a rather urgent need to promote 
sustained yield off-reserve timber production. Yet, the subsidy provided to the timber industry 
through log export bans and low forest fees and taxes is a serious obstacle in this respect. An 
unfortunate, but rather important, effect of keeping domestic log prices artificially low, is that this 
completely undermines financial incentive for establishing plantations on a commercial basis. 
Attempts to attract foreign investments for establishing large-scale plantations have stranded 
specifically on this condition. 
Presumably, the low official forest fees and taxes have, during the period 1986-93, generated 
revenues which were below the actual costs of forest management. From 1994 onwards, forest 
fees and taxes have generated a surplus, but at an unsustainable harvest level. This is so, because 
most of the revenue came from harvest of timber outside forest reserves. 
Institutional studies indicate that the costs of sustainable forest management through a semi-
private Forest Service (to substitute the Forestry Department) may be estimated to DM 7.5 million 
per year. In this respect it is an interesting fact that, if the on-reserve harvest is reduced to the 
AAC for over-utilised species and kept at the 1996-level for other species, forest fees and taxes 
could, if they equalled the industry's willingness to pay, generate an annual revenue ofDM 10-12 
million. The revenue form a sustainable on-reserve harvest would, with the 1999 forest fees and 
taxes, be around DM 8-9 million annually. It should, in other words, be financially possible to 
finance a sustainable forest management through a sustainable harvest from forest reserves alone. 
Rights to Benefits from Timber Exploitation 
It is, however, not only the official forestry authorities that are entitled to a share of the forest 
revenue. When forest reserves were constituted, the ownership to land did not change. Only the 
management authority was shifted from the land owning local communities (stools) to the State 
(the Forestry Department). Off-reserves the situation is and has, since 1962, essentially been the 
same, because the 1962 concession legislation transferred the right to all naturally occurring 
timber trees from the land owning communities to the State. In short, local communities have lost 
the right to manage timber on their land, but they have maintained a legal right to a share of 
revenues from timber exploitation. 
Prior to 1962, land owning communities were legally entitled to no less than two thirds of the 
gross revenue from forest reserves. This rule was cancelled by the 1962 concession legislation, 
according to which all revenues from forest reserves were paid into the Forest Improvement Fund. 
This fund has, since then, been used to finance the Forestry Department. After covering the 
Forestry Department's running costs, any excess money in the fund should each year be 
transferred back to the land owning communities and local authorities from whose land timber 
had been extracted. In reality, very small amounts, if any, have been paid from the Forest 
Improvement Fund to the land owners and local authorities. 
Off-reserve timber revenue was collected by the Lands Department, and presumably local 
communities actually received some share of this. How big this share has been is, however, 
impossible to say, because the Lands Department has never been able (willing) to present any 
accounts for the collection and disbursement of off-reserve timber revenues. 
xix

--- Page 19 ---

Payment of on-reserve timber revenue into the Forest Improvement Fund probably became illegal 
with the 1992 Constitution, which stipulates that: 
"Ten per cent of the revenue accruing from stool lands shall be paid into the office 
of the Administrator of Stool Lands to cover administrative expenses; and the 
remaining revenue shall be disbursed in the following proportions: 
a) 
twenty-five per cent to the stool through the traditional authority for the 
maintenance of the stool in keeping with its status; 
b) 
twenty per cent to the traditional authorityl; 
c) 
fifty-five per cent to the District Assembly, within the area of authority 
of which the stool lands are situated." 
The 1992 Constitution does not include any provision for covering administrative expenses of 
the Forestry Department through (a share of) timber royalties. Accordingly, the Forestry 
Department has, de jure, been illegally financed from 1992 to December 1998. The Timber 
Resources Management Regulations (1998) provides for financing the Forestry Department out 
of revenue arising from timber. This is legally possible because the Constitution does not specify 
the term "revenue" as gross revenue. The Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998) do, 
however, not specify whether timber revenue used to finance the Forestry Department should 
stem from forest reserves alone, or whether it could accrue from stool land in general. Nor is there 
a specified maximum share of timber revenues, that may be used to finance the Forestry 
Department. 
Considering that the annual revenue from a sustainable on-reserve timber harvest is hardly going 
to be higher than DM 8-9 million (at least in the short-term) and that the running costs of 
sustainable forest management is estimated to DM 7.5 million, it is rather convenient for the 
Forestry Department that the legislative provision for covering its running costs are held in 
unspecific terms. Conversely, the legislation provides little guarantee that local communities will 
enjoy significant financial benefits from exploitation of naturally occurring trees on their land. 
This is hardly in the spirit of the 1992 Constitution, and it is weakening the incentives for land 
owning communities to protect and tend naturally occurring timber trees off-reserves, despite the 
fact that such trees would attain high commercial values within say 10-30 years. Consequently, 
off-reserve timber trees might end up as firewood rather than high value export commodities. 
Unfortunately, the problem of creating clear financial incentives for people to grow timber trees 
off-reserves is compounded by the fact that farmers do, in general, not own the land they 
cultivate. Practically all land in Southern Ghana is stool land. Accordingly, any revenue from such 
land must be shared between the stool, the traditional authority and the District Assembly (see 
above). The individual, who cultivates the land from which timber is extracted, has no legal rights 
to a share of the generated revenue. 
Summing up, the legislative and financial structures are still favouring timber exploitation rather 
than timber production. Extraction of government revenue from timber exploitation and creation 
of favourable financial conditions for the timber industry is dominating the legislative background 
I House of Chiefs or any councilor body established or recognised as such under 
customary law (Office of the Administrator of Stool Lands Act, 1994; 18). 
xx

--- Page 20 ---

for forest and timber resources management. This is rather unfortunate considering that Ghana 
could easily face a timber deficit and declining timber export revenues in a not so distant future. 
Furthermore, farmers and local communities are deprived an otherwise obvious opportunity to 
generate income from timber production.

1 Introduction 
The present study is the result of studying the technical, political and economic development of 
high forest management in Ghana during the period 1991-99. ill addition to the collection of data 
specifically for the thesis we have undertaken or participated in four consultancies in Ghana, 
which were all related to political and economic issues of high forest management (lIED, 1993; 
Bach, Gram, Treue, 1995; Treue and Armstrong-Mensah; 1995 and Treue and Odoom, 1996). 
Eight visits totalling 13 months have been made to Ghana in connection with the studies. 
Originally our interest in high forest management in Ghana was associated with the early 1990s' 
growing popular concern over tropical deforestation. At that time environmental (green) 
organisations in Europe and North America were quite successful in placing the links between 
tropical deforestation and international trade in tropical timber on the political agenda, demanding 
bans on import of tropical timberl. Despite the rather weak links between international trade in 
tropical timber and tropical deforestation in general, timber trading companies were, for political 
reasons, forced to look at the forest management behind the products they traded. ill this 
connection the Danish timber trading company, Dalhoff Larsen and Borneman Ltd. requested an 
assessment of forest management in Ghana as the company was (and still is) exporting substantial 
volumes of timber from Ghana to overseas markets (Enevoldsen, Kronslev and Treue, 1991). 
1.1 Objective of the Study 
The first visit to Ghana clarified that management of the country's high forest resource was very 
much linked to commercial timber harvest. There were concerns over the green organisations' 
call for banning tropical timber on especially North American and some European markets, but 
other markets in the Middle and Far East were not affected by this trend. It was also doubtful 
whether any tropical timber importing country would unilaterally implement bans on tropical 
timber imports since this was likely to be against the then GATT (now WTO). 
External forces springing directly out of green organisations' campaign against the use of tropical 
timber were, in other words, not likely to radically influence forest management in Ghana. It 
could disturb but hardly undermine the timber export. In any case, the management oftimber and 
forest resources in Ghana would primarily be an internal matter. 
In fact, a process on improving the management of Ghana's timber and high forest resources was 
already ongoing when the international debate on banning and/or labelling tropical timber took 
off. Essentially, the problem in Ghana was that, following a World Bank and IMF supported 
(designed) Economic Recovery Programme, the timber harvest and export began to pick up 
tremendously in the mid 1980s, while knowledge about the timber resource was very limited (see 
section 3.2.1). Clearly, this held little guarantee for a sustainable development. 
The primary forest policy problem in Ghana was (and still is) to manage the development of an 
export oriented wood industry such that it does not undermine the timber and forest resources. 
Initially, the response has been a major piece-meal implemented forest inventory. This was 
accompanied by numerous analyses of economic and political issues influencing forest 
management and timber harvest. During this process it transpired that the fundamental forest 
policy issue in Ghana could be summarised as follows: 
I Today this demand has generally been modified to a promotion of labelling products of 
tropical timber originating from forests under sustainable management such that end-users, 
in addition to price and quality, may choose in accordance with environmental concerns.

--- Page 26 ---

How to create a decision-making environment that is genuinely in favour of 
sustainable forest and timber resources management and willing to adjust rules and 
regulations even if this goes against the (short term) interests of the timber industry? 
Accordingly, this study is concerned with national level forest management and policy. The 
overall objective is to document and analyse recent developments in the strive towards sustainable 
management of the country's natural high forest and timber resource. Among tropical countries 
Ghana is probably one ofthe most advanced in terms of natural high forest management although 
one can hardly say it has reached a state of sustainable forest and timber utilisation. 
It is hoped that the analyses presented may be useful in national forest management planning for 
other tropical countries, since many of Ghana's past and present forestry related problems are 
likely to reflect general issues of managing tropical high forests. In addition, chapters 12 and 13 
should provide relevant analyses for Ghana on the necessity to improve local communities' 
incentives for tending naturally occurring trees on farmland. 
More specifically, the study attempts to: 
• 
Document the links between the World Bank and IMF supported Economic Recovery 
Programme and over-exploitation of the high forest timber resource; 
document the political background for the various timber inventories; 
analyse and assess the technical results and political impacts of the timber inventories; 
• 
document the notorious over-exploitation of certain (the most valuable) tree species; 
• 
document that the over-exploitation of certain species is largely export driven; 
• 
document that the annual allowable cut from forest reserves cannot satisfy the current 
harvest for export as well as the apparent domestic consumption; 
• 
document that in the near future forest reserves alone cannot even satisfy the expected 
domestic demand for timber; 
• 
assess the apparent successful development in wood exports and prove that the develop-
ment in export revenue, when counted per roundwood equivalent, is not so impressing after 
all; 
• 
estimate the "real" financial value of standing timber as opposed to the official forest taxes 
and fees, and prove that annually millions ofDM in forest revenue have been foregone by 
the Government; 
• 
analyse and show that policy failures have until 1999 created economic incentives which 
have worked against sustainable forest and timber resource management; 
• 
analyse the rights to timber and benefits from timber exploitation on- and off-reserves, and 
make probable that sustainable forest and timber resource management in the high forest 
zone may only be achieved if local communities are given extensive rights to off-reserve 
timber resources. 
1.2 Methodology 
The study methodology has mainly been based on two sources of information:Literature; 
• 
semi-structured interviews. 
In addition, direct field observations on- and off-reserves have been used to build an understan-
ding about the realities on the ground. In particular, discussions held in the forest with personnel 
from the Forestry Department and logging companies have, due to the informal setting and the 
obvious observations one can make, provided valuable background information about practical 
realities and how these sometimes differ from theory and official versions. 
2

--- Page 27 ---

1.2.1 Literature 
The literature studies have mainly been based on official sources like statistics and legislation 
combined with so-called "grey" literature, ie. reports of various kinds that have never been 
published. Almost all literature used has, in practice, only been available in Ghana. As a result, 
getting access to files and reports has been crucial for the study. The Ministry of Lands and 
Forestry, the Forestry Department and the Forest Inventory and Management Project have all been 
very generous in this respect. 
1.2.2 Semi-structured Interviews 
During the early stages of the study it became clear that things in the forestry sector were moving 
quite fast. In 1992 it was easy to point out many weaknesses in the system, but it was equally 
obvious that data and analyses, which were likely to trigger important changes, would soon 
become available. 
During the 1990s an impressive amount of data and reports about the forestry sector in Ghana 
have been produced. Understanding the background for and observing the impact of the data and 
reports produced, therefore, became more relevant for a national level tropical high forest 
management study than pinpointing issues which were likely to change in a few years anyway. 
In this respect it has, over the years, been useful to interview (have discussions with) centrally 
placed people in the forestry sector. Again, employees of the Ministry of Lands and Forestry, the 
Forestry Department, and the Forest Inventory and Management Project have generously made 
time for such interviews/discussions. In addition, many owners and managers of logging 
companies and sawmills have provided useful and important information. 
3

--- Page 28 ---

2 Brief Introduction to Ghana's High Forest Zone 
Ghana is situated on the West African Continent. To the North, West, East and South the 
country is bordered by Burkina Faso, Cote d'Ivoire, Togo and the Gulf of Guinea, 
respectively. 
2.1 Forest Type and Vegetation Zones 
The high forest zone2 in Ghana covers approximately 82,000 km2 , or about one third of the 
land area. It is situated in the South Western comer of the country and forms part of the 
Guineo-Congolean phytogeographical region. The flora and fauna have strong affinities 
with the forests of Cote d'Ivoire, Liberia and Sierra Leone and a lesser affinity with the 
Nigerian forests from which they are separated by the arid "Dahomey gap". 
In general, the Ghanaian high forests are wettest in the South West, turning increasingly 
drier as one moves to the North and East. According to Hawthorne (l993b), the high forest 
zone may be divided into nine vegetation zones, each with distinct associations of plant 
species and corresponding rainfall and soil conditions. These are: 
• 
WE 
Wet Evergreen zone 
• 
ME 
Moist Evergreen zone 
• 
MSSE Moist Semi-deciduous South East type

2.2 Biodiversity 
More than 2,100 plant species, of which 23 are endemic, have been found in the high forest 
(Hall and Swaine, 1981). In total, 730 tree species, of which 680 attain a diameter of 5 cm 
or more at breast hight 0.3 metre above the ground) have been found in the high forest 
(Hawthorne, 1989). The wet evergreen and moist semi-deciduous forest zones are the most 
floristically rich, while the drier forest types are relatively species poor. 
The fauna of the high forest zone includes over 200 species of mammal, many of which are 
rare or endangered. These include the bongo (Tragelaphsus euryceros), Ogilby's duiker 
(Cephalophus ogilbyi), golden cat (Felis aurata), chimpanzee (Pan troglodytes), forest 
elephant (Loxodonta africana cyclotis) and the pygmy hippopotamus (Choeropsis 
liberiensis) (Mensah-Ntiamoa, 1989). The high forest zone also supports 74 species of bats, 
37 rodents, a variety of reptiles and over 200 bird species (meN, 1992). 
5

--- Page 30 ---

3 General Issues on Trees and Forests in the High Forest Zone 
Today the high forest zone is dominated by farmland (including fallow and some plantations), 
with forest and wildlife reserves covering approximately 20% of the zone (Hawthorne and 
Musha, 1993; 4). However, in terms of timber the standing volume is not confined to the forest 
reserves only. Farm and fallow land in the high forest zone carry a substantial timber resource in 
the form of scattered trees on agricultural fields, providing shade for the crops, sacred groves 
(pockets of closed-canopy forest serving religious and spirituel purposes) and a few larger areas 
of closed-canopy forest outside forest reserves. 
Despite the substantial timber resource on farmland, forest reserves clearly stand out on satellite 
images as irregular shaped dark polygons from the surrounding farm and fallow land (Hawthorne, 
1989). This tells: 
• 
Since the establishment of forest reserves a substantial off-reserve deforestation has taken 
place; 
• 
the straight -line boundaries of the forest reserves have largely been enforced by ,the Forestry 
Department and respected by the local populations. 
The latter point does, however, cover some conflict areas where reserve boundaries and reserved 
forests have been heavily encroached. Furthermore, the apparent success of protecting forest areas 
through reserves may now begin to face its ultimate test, where off-reserve forests and timber 
resources are becoming depleted (Hawthorne and Musha, 1993; 9). 
Figure 3.1 shows the distribution of forest reserves in the high forest zone. The figure indicates 
which reserves were inventoried by F AO and the Forestry Department 1980-83 and the Forest 
Inventory Project 1985-88 (see chapters 4 and 5). Eventually, all reserves were inventoried by the 
Forest Inventory and Management Project (see chapter 6). 
6

--- Page 31 ---

~ Forest Reserves inventoried

by FAa and FD 1980-8) 
Figure 3.1 Position of forest reserves in the high forest zone 
3.1 Functions of Trees and Forests the High Forest Zone 
Forests and trees on farmland in the high forest zone serve a multitude of environmental and 
social functions. In comparison to the vast forests of, ego The Democratic Republic of Congo and 
Brazil, the fragmented structure of Ghana's high forest together with a fairly well developed road 
infrastructure makes human access relatively easy. On the one hand, this gives the forests of 
Ghana a high social value, since many people derive many goods and services from them. On the 
other hand, easy access for many interested groups (not least logging companies) generally means 
high risk of over-exploitation. 
The products and services provided by forest reserves include (FRMP, 1988; 5-8); 
• 
Timber for export and domestic consumption; 
• 
fuelwood; 
• 
non-timber forest products; 
• 
watershed protection; 
• 
wind shelter (some reserves have "shelter belt" as part of their names); 
7

--- Page 32 ---

• 
refuges for flora and fauna; 
• 
sacred areas. 
The products and services provided by off-reserve trees and forests include: 
• 
Timber for export and domestic consumption; 
• 
fuelwood; 
• 
non-timber forest products; 
• 
watershed protection (patches of forests on hill tops); 
• 
refuges for flora and fauna (sacred groves in particular); 
• 
sacred areas (sacred groves); 
• 
shade on crops (scattered trees on farmland). 
Despite the apparent similar functions of on- and off-reserve trees and forests there are important 
differences. 
3.1.1 Timber 
Timber for industrial purposes has been and still is extensively harvested off-reserves, even 
though the stocking is obviously much lower off- than on-reserves (see chapter 7). The 
development in the pattern of timber harvest indicates that on-reserve timber resources have 
served as a buffer to off-reserve resources and vise versa, but with the distinct effect that timber 
resources off-reserves have been steadily depleted since the establishment offorest reserves. This 
is investigated in further detail in chapter 7. 
3.1.2 Fuelwood 
Fuel wood is mainl y collected off-reserves on farm and fallow land to meet rural household energy 
needs, for rural processing enterprises (food for sale, palm oil and "akpeteshie", a local gin made 
from distillation of palm sap) and for sale to urban areas (Falconer, 1992). It is widely argued that 
Ghana is suffering from a fuel wood crisis, ego FRMP (1988; 6), but it is questionable whether this 
applies to the high forest zone in general. Through an extensive study of six villages in the high 
forest zone Falconer (1993; 81-82) found no signs of fuelwood shortages, and many villagers 
even found questions about fuel wood scarcities difficult to understand. However, the same study 
found that some rural enterprises (food, palm oil and akpeteshie producers) were having 
difficulties with their fuel wood supply, which raises the, yet unexamined, question of possible 
negative long-term effects urban fuel wood consumption may have on rural income generation 
(Falconer, 1993; 82). At the national level, fuelwood and charcoal is of major economic 
importance. It is estimated that the annual household expenditure on charcoal in Ghana is US$ 
66.49 millions (1992 prices), and the figure for fuel wood is US$ 21.98 millions (Townson, 1995; 
20). 
3.1.3 Non-timber Forest Products 
Non-timber forest products include a multitude of commodities collected/hunted for household 
consumption and for income generation. Roughly, NTFPs may be divided into: 
• 
Forest foods; 
• 
forest medicines; 
• 
items for house construction; 
• 
items for household goods and agricultural equipment; 
• 
fodder for livestock. 
8

--- Page 33 ---

Forest foods (uncultivated plants and animals) include seeds, fruit, leaves, roots and tubers, 
mushrooms and bushmeat (forest snails, game and freshwater fish and crabs). They are collected 
both inside and outside reserves and contribute significantly to the rural and urban diet in Ghana, 
both as a buffer when agricultural production fails and as a normal part of the daily diet. To 
children and lactating women, bush foods are probably highly important sources of vitamins and 
minerals during the season where food stocks dwindle and new crops are being planted (Falconer, 
1993). However, bushmeat is regularly consumed by 75% of the population and it is presumably, 
in general, a vital source of protein (FRMP, 1988; 7). 
There is a strong demand for bushmeat in urban markets, where it seems to become a luxury item, 
and this is a significant source of income generation. In a survey stratifying the high forest zone 
into five vegetation zones, Townson (1995) covers approximately 258,000 people who obtain 
income from NTFPs. He estimates that, in the area surveyed, approximately 20% of the 
economically active people derive an income from NTFPs, of which a little less than 20% do so 
from forest snails and bushmeat (Townson, 1995; 6-7). In 1992, the mean annual expenditure per 
household on bushmeat and forest snails was, according to the third Ghana Living Standards 
Survey, US$ 6.77. Extrapolated to all households in Ghana, this indicates an annual value, 
measured by 1992 consumer prices, of approximately US$ 22.56 millions. This may even be on 
the low side since purchases by businesses, government institutions, etc. are not included 
(Townson, 1995; 20). 
Akpeteshie is among all NTFPs the top scorer in terms of consumer value and numbers of people 
deriving income from its production and trade. Measured by 1992 consumer prices, the annual 
value of akpeteshie production in Ghana is estimated to be more than US$ 63 million (Townson, 
1995; 20). In Townson's survey it is estimated that almost five percent ofthe economically active 
population derive an income from akpeteshie (Townson, 1995; 6-7). 
Forest medicines are undoubtedly the most commonly used medicines in rural areas. It is 
noticeable that the medicines are not confined to specialist healers. The most common use is, in 
fact, as self-administered first aid (Falconer, 1992; 7). Western medicines are used in rural areas, 
but generally only if treatment with forest medicines has not been successful. In urban areas the 
choice between forest and western medicines tends to be the other way round, although it seems 
that both rural and urban people believe that certain illnesses (piles, boils, mental and spiritual 
problems) are best treated by traditional plant cures (Falconer, 1992; 8). The market for forest 
medicines in Ghana is not described in detail and much trade is likely to go unrecorded 
(Townson, 1995; 7). 
Items for house construction include poles from tree saplings, raphia palm leaves, bamboo and 
cane. The nature of these items highlights the importance of forests although they are also 
collected off-reserves. A very important characteristic of these NTFPs is that they are extensively 
used in every Ghanaian village. A vilIage house is typically built from standing poles and raphia 
(leaf petioles) or bamboo for the lattice structure, tied to the poles with cane or other climbers, 
and mud to fill out the spaces between the poles and the bamboo or raphia. Metal sheets are often 
used for roofing, but bamboo and raphia leaves are used by the poorest households. People 
generally collect their own building materials which means the items are rarely subject to market 
transactions, although the demand is high. Many chiefs and elders feel strongly that the collection 
of building materials in forest reserves should be an inalienable right (Falconer, 1992; 10). 
9

--- Page 34 ---

Items for household goods and agricultural equipment include furniture, sleeping mats, carved stools, 
mattresses and pillows, bath houses, pit latrines, ladders, sponges, brooms, tool handles, baskets, fish 
traps, dugout canoes and, most valued by the villagers, pestles for pounding the national dish, fufu 3• 
Many NTFPs for making such goods are collected off-reserves, but forest reserves appear to become 
increasingly important in this respect as some raw materials (eg. Celtis spp. and Blinghia sapida for 
pestles) are becoming scarce on farm and fallow land (Falconer, 1993; 80). The items are generally 
made for own use and not subject to much trade. However, the multitude of products and their 
extensive use in rural households and agricultural production obviously indicate that their social value 
is substantial. 
Fodder for livestock is not a major issue, since livestock production is not a major activity in the high 
forest zone. However, those who do keep livestock collect fodder (various types ofleaves) every day 
on farm and fallow land (rarely in forests) even though the animals are often free ranging during part of 
the day (Falconer, 1993; 80). 
3.1.4 Watershed Protection and Shelter Belts 
Forest reserves serve an important function as shelter belts and in terms of protecting large watersheds. 
In fact, the original legal instrument for gazetting forest reserves focused very much on the protective 
functions offorests as it empowered the Governor, on the advise ofthe Conservator of Forests, to 
constitute a forest reserve on lands to (Forest Ordinance (Cap 157), 1927; 4 (4)): 
• 
Safeguard the water supply of a district; 
• 
assist the well-being of the forest and agricultural crops grown on the land [ ... to become a forest 
reserve] or in the vicinity thereof; 
• 
secure the supply of forest produce to the inhabitants of villages situated in the lands [ ... to become 
a forest reserve] or in the vicinity thereof. 
Based on these powers, forest reserves have been defined in all major hills and watersheds. Barrier and 
shelterbelt forest reserves were also established to hold back bush fires and to maintain local rainfall and 
humidity respectively (Hawthorne and Musha, 1993; 3). 
For obvious reasons, the protective functions of small off-reserve forests are much less important on 
the national level compared to the forest reserves. However, many such patches of forest in the farm 
and fallow landscape are found on hill tops near villages, and apart from being sacred groves they 
clearly protect local agricultural land from water erosion. 
3.1.5 Refuge for Flora and Fauna 
Forest reserves as well as pockets of forests on farm and fallow land serve as important refuges for wild 
flora and fauna. Comprehensive inventories and ecological studies of the fauna and its dependency on 
forest resources in southern Ghana are still to be carried out, which makes it difficult to specifically 
assess the zoological importance of forests and their management. However, bearing in mind that forests 
in botanically good condition have come to represent only islands of the original landscape in Southern 
Fufu is made from starchy stables (cassava, plantain and yams) which are boiled, 
then pounded with a pestle and mortar to a sticky (but tasty) substance that is 
served with different soups. 
10

--- Page 35 ---

Ghana, it may be assumed that the best strategy for protecting faunistic biodiversity is to protect as large 
areas as possible from all disturbance, since species which are tolerant to disturbances in the forest 
cover should not be endangered - at least not through loss of habitat. 
In contrast to zoological data, Ghana has a wealth of forest botany studies. All forest reserves in the high 
forest zone have recently been botanically surveyed with respect to their condition (level of disturbance) 
and their content of nationally and globally rare tree species, expressed by a so-called genetic heat index 
(Hawthorne and Musha, 1993). In particular, the forests in the wet evergreen zone (the South Western 
cornerofthe country) are in good condition and have a high density of rare tree species. This botanical 
information about the forest reserves provides a unique basis for taking forest protection adequately into 
account in management planning from the national down to the individual forest level. 
Off-reserve forests have not been subject to extensive botanical studies, but nevertheless there is reason 
to believe they are refuges for flora and fauna that has become extinct from the surrounding farm and 
fallow land. In many cases (eg. on the costal plains) off-reserve forests represent the re~ants of forest 
types which are now long gone (Hawthorne and Musha, 1993; 54). 
3.1.6 Sacred Areas 
Sacred forest areas are found both inside and outside forest reserves, but obviously the off-reserve 
sacred groves form the most conspicuous evidence of the cultural importance of forests in Southern 
Ghana. Inside forest reserves the location of sacred areas are sometimes secret, although the District 
Forest Office often knows of their existence and location (Hawthorne and Musha, 1993; 54). 
The sacred groves and forest areas serve a number of different purposes which often vary from 
community to community. However, the spiritual use of some plants and their use by traditional healers, 
who are also believed to derive their special powers from forest spirits, is closely linked to the 
sacredness of the groves (Falconer, 1992; 8). In addition, sacred groves and forest areas are sometimes 
used as burial grounds. 
Access to and collection of products from sacred groves may be strictly limited, but again this differs 
from community to community. There is a tendency for the younger generations and immigrants to ignore 
taboos related to sacred groves and their spirits. However, neither Christianity nor the level of education 
seem to influence people's belief in these traditional institutions. Being a well educated Christian, or 
Muslim for that matter, certainly does not mean that you brake taboos deliberately a.nd discard 
traditional beliefs and powers as superstitious nonsense (Falconer, 1992; 8 and Personal observations). 
Even expatriates are well advised and generally do make an effort not to brake taboos and enter sacred 
places without permission from spiritual leaders. Anecdotal information about the mysterious and often 
horrible consequences faced by people who deliberately did so anyway is plentiful. 
3.1.7 Trees on Farmland 
Farm and fallow in the high forest zone is more often than not a landscape of scattered trees with various 
degrees of agricultural cultivation underneath. Broadly, trees on farmland may be divided into (i) these 
that are left standing because the work required to remove them is too heavy, (ii) those that are tolerated 
because they have few negative impacts on crops and (iii) those that are actively tended or planted by 
farmers (lIED, 1993; Appendix 4 (1)). 
11

--- Page 36 ---

Depending on the agricultural crops trees on farmland serve a number of purposes. "Good" trees are 
favoured for their high or shallow crown providing a light shade, deep roots which compete less with 
crops, and the quality of mulching material and soil enrichment provided by their leaves, fruits and seeds. 
"Bad" trees have the opposite characteristics and may in addition also harbour pests (lIED, 1993; 
Appendix 4 (3». 
Food crops are not often grown under trees, although Naynkyerene (Ficus exasperata) is widely 
grown in association with plantain. Trees are mostly found in association with cocoa, the prime cash 
crop in the high forest zone. In a survey by lIED (1993) it was found that farmers considered many 
valuable timber species like Odum, Mahogany, Sapele, Emire and Ofram4 "good" trees in association 
with cocoa. However, Wawa, the by volume most important timber species, was by cocoa farmers 
considered to be a "bad" tree (lIED, 1993; Appendix 4 (3-11 ». 
3.2 Deforestation in the High Forest Zone 
Given that the remaining forest in the high forest zone is today largely confined to forest and game and 
wildlife reserves, it is clear that a massive deforestation has taken place in Southern Ghana. Most of this 
deforestation has been legal, intentional and arguably necessary for development of the country. As 
mentioned above, the detrimental effects of uncontrolled deforestation was already a concern for the 
colonial administration, which initiated the national constitution of forest reserves under the 1927 Forest 
Ordinance. It was an unofficial target to reserve approximately 25% ofthe high forest (Foggie, 1962 
in Hawthorne and Musha, 1993; 4), and most of the forest reserves were gazetted in the first half of the 
20th century. Today approximately 20% of the high forest zone is covered by forest and wildlife 
reserves5. 
Deforestation outside forest reserves to make way for agricultural development was encouraged in the 
1948 Forest Policy (Francois, 1989a). The following expansion of farming (including cocoa) together 
with an increasing demand for pit props in newly mechanised gold mines tolled significantly on the off-
reserve forest resources. During the period 1955-72 alone, it is estimated that about one third of the 
forests in Ghana have disappeared (Hall, 1987, in Hawthorne and Musha, 1993; 3). 
3.2.1 The Economic Recovery Programme and Timber Exploitation 
Ghana's economy plummeted during the 1970s and early 1980s. Huq (1989; 16) characterises the 
years from 1972 to 1983 as "complete mismanagement". During this period price controls, deficit 
financing and maintenance of a highly over-valued Cedi (Ghana's currency) were pursued with vigour 
(Huq, 1989; 17). This undermined the entire economy and not least the export sector. Payments in hard 
currencies for export commodities like timber and cocoa became pretty worthless to the exporters after 
the Bank of Ghana had changed the money into Cedis at the official rate. There was little or no 
allowance for exporters to retain some payments in hard currencies and changing Cedis to overseas 
currencies was obviously equally impossible. 
See appendix I for botanical names. 
The Game and Wildlife Department was set up (separated from the Forestry 
Department) in 1965 and has since then taken over the management of some 
previous forest reserves (Bia, Nini Suhien, Ankasa, Kakum and parts of Shai 
Hills Game Production Reserve). 
12

--- Page 37 ---

As a consequence, the export of timber along with other commodities fell drastically and with it 
investments in maintaining the production capacity (logging and saw milling equipment) and the 
supporting infrastructure (harbour facilities, road and rail network). Ghana's timber harvest and export 
during the periods 1960-96 and 1972-96 are shown in figures 3.2 and 3.3, respectively.

FPIB Export Permits (1986-96) 
Finally in 1984, the Government of Ghana, the IMF and the World Bank agreed on the so-called 
Economic Recovery Programme (Hug, 1989; 31). This programme encompassed a number of 
components including, of course, a strategy for enhancing the export of commodities from Ghana (the 
Export Rehabilitation Programme). Timber was an obvious candidate for this programme. 
During the mid 1980s, massive investments went into the Ghanaian timber industry. Precisely how much 
and under which circumstances money was transferred from overseas donors and suppliers of credits 
appears to be one of the misty chapters in Ghana's recent forest history. According to the Financial 
Times (1987) more than US$ 140 millions from the World Bank (IDA), British6 and Canadian bilateral 
aid and credit lines went into the Ghanaian timber industry during the period 1984-86. However, of this 
US$ 90 millions apparently went into transportation machinery, which should be written off the books 
after three years - an average annual debt repayment ofUS$ 30 million on transport alone (Financial 
Times, 1989). 
As the curves in figures 3.2 and 3.3 indicate, the timber industry responded very quickly to the 
incentives provided under the Export Rehabilitation Programme. The annual recorded timber harvest 
A UK£ 149.5 million credit was established through aDA for the purchase of 
logging and saw milling equipment (Frimpong-Mensah in FIP, 1989; 76). 
14

--- Page 39 ---

and wood export went up from approximately 400,000 m3 and 100,000 m3, respectively in 1982 to 
1,200,000 m3 and 530,000 m3, respectively in 1988. 
In the pursuit of economic development, it often becomes an objective to convert available natural 
capital into human capital. In the case offorests, standing trees are converted into logs and processed 
wood products for export and domestic consumption. Logging does not require a high level oftechnical 
knowledge and equipment and it creates job in rural areas. In addition, the strong forward linkages of 
the timber industry with other sectors should make natural forests in LDCs attractive assets from which 
to initiate economic development (Westoby, 1962, in Westoby, 1987; 44). 
Although Westoby later on almost totally abandoned this theory as being politically naive and, in 
practice, leading to severe socio-economic underdevelopment and uncontrolled deforestation in most 
LDCs (Westoby, 1978, in Westoby, 1987; 246-254), he originally warned against the danger of 
mobilising the forest resource for industrial development without controlling the process through 
adequate and enforceable planning in the public interest. On how to mobilise the forest,resource, he 
wrote: 
The technical and economic conditions for establishing new forest 
industries in the developing countries are maturing fast. In the 
course of the coming years many new areas offorests are inevi-
tably going to be brought into use. The choice is between mobilisa-
tion in the public interest based on sound planning and with 
adequate safeguards and with the forest services taking an active 
part and being built up in the process, and mobilisation taking 
place in an uncontrolled and haphazard way, while weak forest 
services stand by helpless. This is the real choice. (Westoby, 1962, 
in Westoby, 1987; 69-70) 
During the early stages of the Export Rehabilitation Programme in Ghana the then Chief 
Conservator of Forests, John Francois often tried to raise the issue of matching the capacity of 
the fast growing timber industry with the production capacity of the country's forest resources 
(Personal communication J. Francois, CCF, 1993 and Financial Times, 1987). Yet, such basic 
principles of caution in planning the utilisation of natural forest and timber resources were largely 
ignored by decision makers. The consequences of this attitude soon began to material~se, and 
since then these have been dominating policy issues within the forestry sector. 
15

--- Page 40 ---

4 The Timber Resource Base, Early Estimates 
Under the forestry component of the Export Rehabilitation Project provision was actually made for two 
special studies to look into (Silviconsult, 1985; 1): 
• 
Strategy Proposals for the Forestry Sector; 
• 
Forestry Department Review and Study of the requirements ofthe Forest Products mspection 
Bureau (FPIB) and the Timber Export Development Board (TEDB). 
The studies were undertaken by a private consultancy company, Silviconsult, and a focus area was to 
determine the annual allowable cut (AAC) of timber. To this end the results of the 1980-82 
FAO/UNDP inventory of 16 forest reserves and permanent sample plot data from the Forests Products 
Research Institute (FPRI) were analysed with a view to estimate the growing stock and annual 
increment within forest reserves in the high forest zone. Furthermore, as there was no data on off-
reserve timber resources, guesstimates were made concerning the off-reserve growing stock and 
increment (Silviconsult, 1985; 6-16). 
4.1 The Growing Stock in 1985 
The 16 forest reserves included in the FAO/UNDP inventory cover an area of approximately 200,000 
ha, and the sampling intensity was 0.46% (Silviconsult, 1985; 153). However, from a national planning 
perspective the inventory results are limited by the fact that reserves inventoried are found in the 
southern part of the Central and Western Region only. 
Ofthe approximately 1.72 million ha forest reserves in the high forest zone, approximately 1.18 million 
ha were designated as production working circles 7 (production forest). Estimates of the growing stock 
within production working circles of the entire high forest zone were arrived at by re-grouping 
FAOIUNDP data according to vegetation zones as classified by Hall and Swaine (1976), and then 
extrapolating the results to account for all forest reserves in each vegetation zone (Silviconsult, 1985; 
151-153). The FAOIUNDP inventory did not include the Dry Semi-deciduous zone which accounts 
for approximately 8% of the total area of production working circles. As a proxy for this zone the mean 
volume per ha for the other three zones (the Wet Evergreen, the Moist Evergreen and the Moist Semi-
Deciduous) was used (Silviconsult, 1985; 153). 
To make inventory results meaningful at the policy level all tree species were classified according to 
commercial importance. The groups were based on the following criteria (appendix 1 details species 
groups for different inventories): 
Group A: 
The 40 species which, at the time, had an established export or domestic 
market. 
Production working circles is a tenn used in the forest working plans of the 
1950s. The primary management objective within production working circles 
was production of timber on a sustained yield basis. These plans were 
designed for 10 year periods, so formally they expired during the 1960s. 
However, since then many of the management prescriptions in these plans 
have, in the absence of new management objectives and prescriptions, been 
considered valid. The estimate of 1.18 million ha of production working circles 
was in 1985 changed to approximately 1.16 million ha under the ODNFD 
Forest Inventory Project (see chapter 5). 
16

--- Page 41 ---

Group B: 
Twenty potentially commercial species which, at the time, were only harvested 
to supply specific orders. 
Group C: 
All other species. 
4.1.1 National Estimates in 1985 ofthe Growing Stock 
The growing stock within forest reserves of the high forest zone was estimated as bole volume 
(presumably above bark) for species groups (cf. above) and girth classes. The silvicultural system was 
(and still is) based on selective felling of trees above a species specific minimum girth. Atthe time all 
trees above minimum girth were allocated for felling, which, in principle, meant that the standing stock 
above felling limit could be considered available for timber harvesting. The inventory estimates are 
summarised in table 4.1. 
Table 4.1

4.1.2 National Estimates in 1985 of Annual Growth and AAC 
Estimates of the annual growth were established by analysis of permanent sample plot data, However, 
the quality and in particular the quantity of data apparently left much to be desired. Periodic girth 
measurements had only been made for some of the species within Group A (groups I and IT in a 
previous classification system used by Forestry Department) and measurements were biased towards 
trees of small diameters. (Silviconsult, 1985; 155). 
The actual number of data on which the growth estimates were based are not stated by Silviconsult 
(1985), neither is there any information on the location of permanent sample plots in relation to 
vegetation zones. Furthermore, the crucial question of mortality rates was not accounted for. If the 
growth of trees in a natural forest is estimated without taking natural mortality into account, the 
calculated annual increment is bound to overestimate actual growth and hence the number and volume 
oftrees which actuall y reach timber size (the minimum girth). Obvi ousl y, the potential error in this 
respect is particularly high when measurement is done mainly on trees of small diameter, since their 
17

--- Page 42 ---

chance of reaching maturity (the minimum girth or more) is smaller than for trees close to the mature 
state. 
Nevertheless, growth estimates on Group A species were calculated and even extrapolated to account 
for Group Band C species, such that national estimates of the total annual increment could be 
produced. The annual allowable cut was then recommended to be 70% of the annual increment to allow 
for wastage in the form of felling damage, road construction etc. The results are summarised in table 
4.2
