Silviconsult (1985; II) 
In addition to the estimated AAC of approximately 1.9 million m3 some considerations were made with 
respect to the potentials for off-reserve timber harvest. 
Basically, no data on off-reserve timber resources existed and guesstimates from so-called informed 
sources varied between 15 and 50 million m3• Concessionaires said that the off-reserve yield per ha was 
generally one quarter to half of what they usually could harvest on concessions within forest reserves. 
Based on this it was assumed that the off-reserve forest resource was equivalent to 0.75 million ha of 
forest reserve. Thus, the off-reserve growing stock should be 100 million m3 of all species, of which 53 
million m3 is above the minimum girth. Accordingly, for group A species (the commercially most desired 
species) the stock above minimum girth should be approximately 21 million m3 (Silviconsult, 1985; 15-
16). 
However, the off-reserve stock was at the time correctly not considered a sustainable timber resource, 
since clearings for agriculture were continuously taking place outside forest reserves. Rather, the off-
reserves timber resource was considered a going resource of which maximum harvest for industrial 
purposes should be pursued while it remained (Silviconsult, 1985; 18). 
4.2 Recommendations on the Timber Harvest in 1985 
With an AAC of approximately 720,000 m3 for group A species and 380,000 m3 for group B species 
within forest reserves alone, the long-term sustainable AAC of commercial and potentially commercial 
species is, therefore, estimated to 1.1 million m3. By comparing this estimate and the guesstimated 
off-reserve resources with the annual timber harvest of 400-600,000 m3 in the early 1980s (see figure 
3.2) it appeared as if there was scope for a significant expansion of the annual harvest. 
18

--- Page 43 ---

All in all this message on the timber resource and the AAC must have suited those promoting 
investments in the timber industry very well. Concerns over the sustainability of increasing the timber 
harvest could be calmed, although investments in the timber industry were already being undertaken and 
the investment policy was hardly subject to change according to the findings of Silviconsult. It is certainly 
not mentioned in Silviconsult' s terms of reference for the study that investments in rehabilitating of the 
timber industry should be considered in relation to the estimated AAC (Silviconsult, 1985; 129-130). 
4.2.1 The Stated Warnings and Unstated Risks 
However, Silviconsult (1985; 13) emphasised thatthe data on growth dynamics of Ghana's forests was 
inadequate to make sufficiently reliable estimates of the annual growth and hence the AAC. 
Furthermore, it was brought to attention that an estimated 90% of the annual harvest fell within the group 
A species. A significant but still sustainable increase of the timber harvest should, therefore, first and 
foremost be concentrated on group B species, which made it essential to develop markets for these 
species (Silviconsult, 1985; 18). 
A glance at harvest rates during the 1970s should have resulted in additional warnings against the risks 
of boosting the capacity of the timber industry (see figure 3.2). Annual harvest rates between 1.5 and 
2.0 million m3 was clearly above the estimated AAC ofthe most desired and harvested species (group 
A), but also above the AAC of group A and B species taken together (se table 4.2). 
The declining timber production during the late 1970s and early 1980s was hardly the result of a 
deliberate policy to reduce the timber harvest. Rather, it was the result of a general economic decline 
in Ghana. Accordingly, it was likely that the timber industry would return to harvest rates ofthe 1970s 
once the capacity to do so was reestablished through the Export Rehabilitation Programme. 
Clearly, the Forestry Department was not able to efficiently enforce harvest regulations within its 
jurisdiction, the forest reserves. Off-reserve the timber harvest was hardly subject to much regulation 
by the Lands Department. Both departments were found to be under-equipped, under-staffed and 
under-funded to live up to their responsibilities and duties in relation to the forest and timberresources 
(Silviconsult, 1985; 32-62). 
It may be considered easy to say afterwards, but three major warnings on rehabilitating the timber 
industry were actually raised by Silviconsult (1985; v): 
• 
The know ledge of the resource was incomplete and insufficient for efficient planning and 
utilisation; 
• 
the field administration seriously lacked facilities for efficient management and control; 
• 
revenues from forests could, at the time, cover only a small fraction of the required budget for 
proper resource management, but they could be considerably increased. 
These were important findings suitable for policy level considerations, but the scientifically questionable 
estimate of the AAC probably had more policy effect at the time, although the general message cannot 
have been overheard. Ironically, more reliable estimates of the AAC have later revealed that the 
estimates of Silviconsult (1985) were dangerously optimistic (see section 7.5). Yet, it took a long time 
before this was realised. 
19

--- Page 44 ---

5 National Forest Inventory (1985-89) 
In 1985, the British Government signed a UK£ 1.146 million aid project with the Government of Ghana 
to undertake a forest inventory in the high forest zone of Ghana, "the Forest Inventory Project" (FIP). 
This project was implemented during the period 1985-89 by the Forestry Department, Ghana (FD) with 
aDA employed expatriate assistance (long and short term advisors and consultants). 
5.1 Background of the Forest Inventory Project 
As mentioned in chapter 4, one of the main findings of Silviconsult (1985) was that the knowledge about 
the growing stock and the annual increment in Ghana's forests was insufficient to allow for adequate 
national level forest management planning. Also, the then Chief Conservator of Forests, Mr John 
Francois had in various fora insisted that the rehabilitation of the timber industry under the Export 
Rehabilitation Project had to be linked to the sustainable supply of wood from the forests of Ghana. 
However, as a result ofthe general economic decline in Ghana during the 1970s and early 1980s it 
became impossible for the Forestry Department to continue ongoing forest inventories as well as to 
implement planned activities in this field, so in 1984 a request was made to aDA for assistance to 
establish the capacity to conduct a national forest inventory (Francois, 1989b in FIP, 1989a; 6). 
an its part aDA is likely to have realised that the support provided to the timber industry in Ghana 
produced an urgent need to strengthen the management of Ghana's high forest. If such management was 
not supported by aDA and timber exploitation in Ghana proved uncontrollable, aDA could easily be 
accused of promoting tropical deforestation for quick financial profits. aDA foresters must to some 
extent have been involved in designing and implementing the aDA supported rehabilitation of the 
Ghanaian timber industry during the period 1985-89 (or at least aware ofthe project). Accordingly, it 
is likely that the concern this development raised among foresters in Ghana, as expressed by the then 
CCF, J. Francois, has found its way back to decision makers in aDA, London. 
However, the mechanisms of identifying the FIP are unclear. A formal ODA evaluation of the FIP could 
not document the identification process or the criteria applied. It was found likely that the project was 
a follow-on to the FAalUNDP inventory project, for which Ghana initially failed to get funding until 
aDA expressed interest (Flint and Hardcastle, 1992; 3). 
5.2 Objectives of the Forest Inventory Project 
The primary objecti ve of the FIP was through a reconnaissance survey to determine the growing stock 
and the annual increment to provide a macro-scale planning figure of the AAC (Howard, 1989 in FIP, 
1989a; 59)8. In further detail, the FIP had three main objectives of which in particular the first was 
considered critical because the rehabilitated timber industry was putting increasing pressure on the forest 
resource (Blackett, 1989 in FIP, 1989a; 14). The objectives were: 
• 
Static timber Inventory; 
• 
dynamic timber inventory; 
• 
training in all aspects of timber inventory. 
The ODA evaluation documents that while there was never doubt about this 
overall objective there was serious confusion whether FIP should produce 
inventory data which was useful at reserve or management unit level. This 
limited the success of the project (Flint and Hardcastle, 1992). 
20

--- Page 45 ---

The FIP developed three species classes reflecting the present and potential commercial importance of 
tree species in Ghana. The species classes were defined as follows (FIP, 1989a; 32): 
FIP class 1: Species at least exported once from Ghana during the period 1973-88. 
FIP class 2: Species attaining 70 cm diameter at breast hight9 (DBH) and occurring at a 
frequency greater than 1 tree per km2• 
FIP class 3: Species not attaining 70 cm DBH or occurring at a frequency less than 1 tree 
per km2 (all other species than FIP class 1 and 2 species). 
Appendix 1 details the species in FIP classes 1 and 2 together with the previous classification of species 
made by Silviconsult. All but one of Silviconsult' s class A species and all but six of Silviconsult' s class 
B species are included in FIP class 1. 
5.2.1 Design of the FIP Static Timber Inventory 
Initially it was the objective to sample approximately SOO,OOO ha of forest reserves throughout the high 
forest zone. In practice, this meant that temporary sample plots (TSPs) were established in about half 
of the forest reserves in the high forest zone. At a later stage, under the Forest Inventory and 
Management Project (FIMP) which followed the FIP, the remaining reserves were covered such that 
by 1994 practically all reserves in the high forest zone had been inventoried. 
Within the four major ecological zones (Wet evergreen, Moist evergreen, Moist semi-deciduous and 
Dry semi-deciduous) 10 a random selection of forest reserves was made such that the area surveyed was 
directly proportional to the area offorest reserves within each zone. It was decided to apply aO.2S% 
sampling intensity as recommended by FAD (Flint and Hardcastle, 1992; IS). In addition, an analysis 
of the O.S% sampling intensity applied under the previous FAOIUNDP inventory had indicated that 
satisfactory accuracy on volume estimates could be obtained using a lower sampling intensity (Blackett, 
1989 in FIP, 1989a; 17). Apparently, a statistical check of the sampling error was conducted only once 
(in 1986) during the FIP. This proved to be 12% (presumably the 9S% confidence interval of the 
volume estimate for all diameter classes within the FIP class I species) which was considered 
acceptable against a target of 20% (Flint and Hardcastle, 1992; IS). 
The TSP layout was done by randomly superimposing a 2 x 2 kilometre grid upon maps of the selected 
reserves, letting grid intersections determine the location of I ha TSPs. In the field the TSPs were 
established by compass and chain survey from obvious geographic features (Blackett, 1989 in FIP, 
1989a; 17). Within each TSP the following measurements and observations were made (FIP, 1989b; 
App. I): 
10 
Defined as 1.3 metre above ground level. 
Eventually the PIP operated with five vegetation zones as it was found useful 
to divide the moist semi-deciduous zone (MS) into a North Western moist 
semi-deciduous zone (MSNW) and a South Eastern moist semi-deciduous 
zone (MSSE). The other three zones are the wet evergreen (WE), the moist 
evergreen (ME) and the dry semi-deciduous zone (OS), sometimes referred to 
as the dry semi-deciduous forest zone (DSFZ). 
21

--- Page 46 ---

• 
On the entire plot diameters for all trees having a diameter above 30 cm DBH or 30 cm above 
buttresses were measured; 
• 
the local species name for all trees was noted, or if unknown a specimen number was given and 
a field specimen collected for subsequent herbarium identification; 
• 
form and quality codes ranging from 1 to 5 (1 being the best) were given to each tree above 30 
cm DBH (to allow for subsequent estimates of utili sable volumes); 
• 
crown classification ranging from I to 5 (1 being unshaded) was given to each tree above 30 cm; 
• 
on a subplot of 0.1 ha all trees above 10 cm DBH were measured; 
• 
on a subplot of 0.1 ha all trees above 5 cm DBH were measured; 
• 
relevant observations for each plot such as slope, position, presence or absence of logging 
activity, burning, rivers, roads, etc. 
A diameter-bole volume equation was developed through detailed volume measurements of 3488 trees 
(Wong, 1990; 8), and volume stand tables generated for trees having form and quality codes 1 or 2 
only. This means that TSP volume data only includes bole volumes of "good" trees for industrial use 
(Wong, 1989 in FIP, 1989a; 28). Furthermore, estimates were made of the utiUsable volumes by 
subtracting the volume of defective sections (bumps, rot, sweeps, etc.) from the bole volume. The mean 
defect per tree was 8% of the bole volume. Roughly this percentage lost due to defects remained 
constant for all trees irrespective of size. This is not reflected in the stand tables, but it should be borne 
in mind when interpreting the results. 
Finally, FlP defined the commercial tree volume as the volume under bark of cross cut logs prepared 
by the timber company minus the volume of defective sections I I. This should approximate the LMC 
volumes recorded by FPill. On average the commercial tree volumes were found to be 10% below 
the TSP bole volumes. If itis assumed that the bark accounts for 2% of the bole volume this meant that 
the commercial volume would be very similar to the FIP utilisable volume less bark (Wong, 1989 in 
FIP, 1989a; 29). 
However, upon further analysis it was concluded that a statistically significant relation between bole 
volumes and commercial volumes could not be established. This was probably due to the SUbjective and 
highly variable partitioning of boles into logs by the various timber companies (Wong, 1990; 9). 
Accordingly, the FlP volume measurements did not provide statistically sufficient basis for establishing 
a mathematical relation between the growing stock and increment (expressed in FlP bole volumes) and 
the annual extraction of logs (expressed in LMC commercial volumes). 
To arrive at national estimates for the growing stock, mean TSP volumes per ha minus the 95% 
confidence interval were simply multiplied by the area of production working circles within the forest 
reserves in the high forest zone. The area figures used by FIP are detailed in table 5.1. 
II 
All 3488 measured trees were felled and utilised normally by timber 
companies. Measurements for the bole volume were done on the felled but 
still not cross cut tree, and measurements of the commercial volume were 
done after cross cutting into logs by the timber company in case.

published by vegetation zone, but by administrative region. This, together with the FIP classification of 
species, makes a direct comparison with the 1985 growing stock estimates impossible, except for the 
national volumes. Yet, as noted above the FIP class 1 covers almost all of Silviconsult' s class A and 
B species. Table 5.2 summarises the 1989 FIP static inventory results.

As table 5.2 shows, the 1989 FIP national estimate of the total growing stock within production working 
circles is 188.60 million m3 (162.73 m3/ha) which is approximately 30 million m3 higher than the 1985 
estimate made by Silviconsult (1985). Also, the estimated 89.70 million m3 (77.39 m3/ha) ofFIP class 
1 species above 70 cm DBH12 is considerably above the 55.7 million m3 of Silviconsult' s group A and 
B species above felling limittaken together (see table 4.1). Accordingly, the positive message from the 
1989 FIP results was that Ghana was well stocked with species which had been exported at least once 
since 1973. 
However, the 1989 FIP results also indicated that there was reason for concern. As table 5.3 shows, 
the growing stock above 70 cm DBH of the ten commercially most desired species was approximately 
37% of the growing stock above 70 cm DBH of FIP class 1 species, but in 1988 these species 
accounted for 77% of the total harvest as recorded by the LMC totals.

5.4 The FIP Dynamic Inventory 
The dynamic inventory attempted to produce timber growth and yield estimates for the forest reserves 
within the high forest zone. To this end measurements and analysis of data from pennanent sample plots 
(PSPs) were undertaken. On this basis a simple growth table for 14 species and a computer based 
simulation of the growth of all species which have ever been exported13 were produced (Alder, 1989 
in FIP, 1989a; 47-49). 
Data from the PSPs only allowed for calculating simple growth tables for 14 species. The annual growth 
per haforeach of the 14 species is calculated as the volume that will grow into a diameterclass14 from 
the class immediately below. It is calculated as the volume of the preceding diameter class times the 
average proportion of surviving trees and the average percentage that will move up into the next class 
(Alder, 1989 in FIP, 1989a; 47). Accordingly, growth of trees which remain within diameter classes 
is ignored. 
The AAC for each of the 14 species was calculated as the volume moving from the di~meter class 
immediately below the felling limit into the diameter class above the felling limit. The time this takes was 
estimated by dividing the annual diameter growth within the particular diameter classes into 20 cm (the 
diameter class width). National AACs were calculated by assuming that the productive forest area is 
1.3 million ha(Alder, 1989 inFIP, 1989a;4). Why the 14 AAC estimates are based on a productive 
forest area of 1.3 million ha while the growing stock estimates were based on an area of approximately 
1.16 million ha (the total area of production working circles) is not explained. The time it takes to grow 
from the diameter class immediately below felling limit into the diameter class above felling limit, and 
AACs together with the rate of extraction in 1988 for the 14 species, are shown in table 5.4. 
13 
14 
It is not clear whether this coincides entirely with the FIP class 1. It must, 
however, be assumed that only a few species additional to those in FIP class 
1 are included. 
Diameter classes of 20 cm are used.

Even though the AACs and lifetime estimates in table 5.4 can only be taken as indicative (they still 
remain the only species specific growth estimates available) the message was very clear. Some species 
(which also tend to be the most valuable) appeared grossly over-exploited while the extraction of other 
species could be expanded. 
The 14 species in table 5.4 were not entirely representative for all species harvested, so they could not 
be used to estimate the AAC for all species in all forest reserves within the high forest zone, the national 
AAC. To this end a computer programme, GHAFOSIM was applied on all FIP class I species. The 
programme combines PSP data on single-tree increment and mortality rates of 24 different species with 
TSP data on the growing stock within diameter classes and crown classification (the latter used to 
predict mortality rates) (Alder, 1990). When, in an attempt to approximate a future commercially driven 
harvest of species, GHAFOSIM was run on only 30 of the 57 FIP class 1 species, this produced a 
number of interesting results l5 (Alder, 1989 in FIP, 1989a; 48 and Alder, 1990): 
15 
Alder (1990) uses, but does not specify, the term "redwoods" together with 
16 other specified species. "Redwoods" is normally used for the commercially 
most valuable species which have reddishlbrown wood. Here this group is 
considered to contain 14 species.

--- Page 51 ---

• 
If the extraction rate remains at the 1988 level of approximately I m3/halyr, this can be sustained 
for about 100 years or more, but the stock of commercially most desired species will become 
progressively depleted; 
• 
the 1989 distribution into size classes may only be maintained if a 100 year felling cycle 16 is 
implemented; 
• 
with a 15 year felling cycle the 1989 stock of mature (big) trees will become depleted, but in the 
long term yields of approximately 3.4 m3/halyr (a national AAC of approximately 4.0 million m3) 
may be sustained; 
• 
with a 25 year felling cycle the 1989 stock of mature (big) trees will become depleted, but in the 
long term yields of approximately 2.2 m3/halyr (a national AAC of approximately 2.5 million m3) 
may be sustained; 
• 
with a 40 year felling cycle the 1989 stock of mature (big) trees will become depleted, but in the 
long term yields of approximately 1.3 m3/halyr (a national AAC of approximately 1.5 million m3) 
may be sustained. 
Alder (1990) argues that while felling cycles shorter than 40 years appear to result in higher annual 
yields, due to the fact that trees are generally removed closerto their point of maximum average annual 
growth and before natural mortality, the 40 year felling cycle is to be preferred because: 
• 
The uncertainties in yield forecasting are considerable due to data constraints and the fact that 
GHAFOSIM was still mostly a theory based growth model for whole stands which had not been 
verified in practice (this is still the case). Therefore, a conservatively set AAC was advis-
able 
• 
the AAC under the 40 year felling cycle (1.5 million m3) approximated the annual demand by the 
industry 
• 
the 40 felling cycle would, compared with shorter felling cycles, minimise the area for harvesting 
operations to be controlled annually by the Forestry Department (1.16 million ha divided by 40 
years gives an annual area of 29,000 ha for harvest) 
• 
the geographical concentration of felling activities would allow for a tighter in-field control of 
loggers and thereby enhance the possibility of requiring and encouraging the industry to market 
the abundant but lesser used species more energetically. 
Compared with Silviconsult's estimated AAC of approximately 1.1 million m3 for its group A and B 
species (see section 4.2), the FIP estimate of 1.5 million m3 for its class 1 species under a40 year felling 
cycle is a little more optimistic. Although the GHAFOSIM estimates of the AAC may have been 
considered the best available at the time, the warnings about the uncertain nature of this AAC essentially 
remained the same as in 1985 (Alder, 1990; 53-55): 
• 
PSP growth data was insufficient to allow for accurate predictions of future yield under various 
management regimes; 
• 
data on the growth of whole stands was (and still is) totally absent; 
16 
The required minimum period of time between any two felling operations 
within a given area. A 25 year felling cycle allows the residual stand a 25 year 
recovery period before the next felling operation. 
27

--- Page 52 ---

• 
growth data on a number of individual species were inadequate or absent; 
• 
the knowledge about mortality rates and how they may be influenced by logging and other factors 
was (and still is) insufficient. 
In fact, upon questioning Alder (1989 in FIP 1989a; 52) admits that the precision of the GHAFOSIM 
simulations of growth and the AAC is about ±50%. Accordingly, Silviconsult's AAC lies within the 
confidence limits of GHAFOSIM's (FIP's) estimated AAC. 
5.5 Other Technical Outputs of the FIP 
An issue of concern was the felling cycle. The traditionally used 25 year felling cycle was replaced by 
a 15 year felling cycle in 1970, with the intention to enable a more rapid removal of old (sometimes 
called over-mature) trees such that they could be utilised before death and such that a higher volume 
increment could be achieved forthe residual stand (Silviconsult, 1985; 51). During the 1980s, the 25 
year felling cycle was re-introduced, but was it sustainable? 
Nolan (1989 in FIP, 1989a; 55) calculates an estimated felling cycle of 64 years by dividing a total 
AAC of 1.4 million m3 into the FIP class 1 growing stock above 70 cm DBH of 89.7 million m3 
(assuming that this total AAC is correct and that the stock above 70 cm DBH equals the total increment 
over a felling cycle). Although this was a very rough and scientifically questionable estimate it 
emphasised, together with the computer simulations in GHAFOSIM and the passage of time for trees 
to grow from the diameter class immediately below the felling limit into the diameter class above felling 
limit, that a felling cycle of25 years was highly unlikely to be sustainable. In fact, Silviconsult came to 
a similar result in 1985 and recommended a 40 year felling cycle (Silviconsult, 1985; 88). 
As mentioned above, the TSPs, sub-plots of 0.05, 0.1 and 0.2 ha were laid out to record all trees of 
DBHs higher than 5, 10 and 30 cm, respectively. This would indicate how well the forest was 
regenerating in the different vegetation zones.

It appeared that the forest in the dry semi-deciduous zone was losing its ability to regenerate, and forest 
fires were likely to be the direct course. 
In general, it was concluded that forest fires were the greatest threat to Ghana's forests and that once 
burned forests are likely to burn again due to the increased level of (i) annual weeds (especially 
Eupatorium), (ii) windflow at ground level and (iii) volume of dead wood in burned compared to 
unburned forests. Therefore, it should be matter of urgency to investigate the seemingly logical 
hypothesis that logged forests are more prone to catch fire than unlogged forests, especially in the dry 
areas (Hawthorne, 1989 in FIP, 1989a; 12). 
5.6 Critique of the FIP 
A major point of criticism against the FIP is that the objectives ofthe project were far from clear and 
consistent (Hint and Hardcastle, 1992; 39). More specifically, FIP appeared to have no formal project 
framework. This allowed for three versions of the project objectives. 
First, as stated in the terms of reference for the evaluation mission in 1992, the objectives ofFIP were 
(Hint and Hardcastle, 1992; 38): 
• 
The primary objective was to undertake an inventory of 500,000 ha of forest reserves to provide 
details of the size of the growing stock and the increment of the forest, thereby enabling a more 
accurate assessment of the annual allowable cut to be calculated. The data would enable a 
29

--- Page 54 ---

sustained yield policy to be formulated so that timber could be extracted from the forest while at 
the same time the floristic and environment values would be maintained 
• 
the second objective was to establish an inventory unit capable of running continuous forest 
inventory with the ability to provide data on area, volume, species, size-classes and regeneration 
consistent with the medium and long-term objectives of the Ghana Forestry Department 
• 
the third objective was to prepare a field guide to the forest trees. 
Second, the objectives of the inventory were, according to FIP (1989b; App. 1), to: 
• 
Fix the prescribed cut at national level; 
• 
prepare inventory reports and harvesting schedules for individual reserves or groups of reserves; 
• 
report on the status of tree regeneration in relation to forest disturbance. 
Third, according to ODA (1987) the immediate objectives of the project were to: 
• 
Carry out an inventory of selected forest reserves totalling 500,000 ha, to conduct mensurational 
studies on felled trees to produce improved indigenous volume tables and to measure Permanent 
Sample Plots to provide data on the increment of the forest for use in the planning model; 
• 
prepare a long-term management plan for forest reserves under various management assumptions; 
• 
train local staff both in Ghana and abroad so that they can take on the forest inventory as soon 
as possible. Skills required included tree identification, measuring with different instruments, 
sample design and layout, team organisation, air photo interpretation and data processing; 
• 
prepare a practical key to the forest trees of Ghana for use by technical field staff and to write a 
key with a view to publishing it; 
• 
develop and document all the necessary data processing systems required to carry out the 
objectives in the future. 
In particular, the confusion over objectives seems to be whether the inventory results were intended to 
be used at management unit level, while it was pretty clear that the results should be used at the national 
level to guide a sustained yield policy through an estimate of the growing stock and an estimated AAC. 
While the forest botanical objective of making a field guide to forest trees in Ghana (Hawthorne, 1990) 
was fully met, there is reason to criticise a number of issues concerning the inventory results 17 • These 
include: 
• 
The static inventory design; 
• 
the AAC estimates; 
• 
the (missing) links between AAC estimates, actual harvest and the management of timber harvest 
inside forest reserves. 
5.6.1 Critique of the Static Inventory Design 
Basically, the policy and management objectives of the static inventory should have been clarified before 
the sampling exercise was designed and implemented. It is, for example, not a logical objective to 
17 
Objectives of the FIP other than those specifically concerned with forest 
management and policy are not considered in detail in this study. 
30

--- Page 55 ---

sample 500,000 ha offorest with a 0.25% intensity. There should exist underlying objectives ofthe 
inventory which justify the extent and methodology applied. 
In general, any inventory based on statistical sampling should include the following steps: 
• 
Define objectives; 
• 
determine population variance and structure, either through pilot sampling or historical 
information; 
• 
define accuracy required; 
• 
rationalise input costs against accuracy; 
• 
check preliminary results against the objecti ves and accuracy requirements and, if required, 
redefine procedures. 
As the project was primarily concerned with the sustainability of commercial timber harvest it may be 
criticised that the project did not focus particularly on big trees within the FIP class 1. The siffilpling was 
done proportional to area rather than considering financial value. However, the most important 
weakness of the sample design lies in: 
• 
The lacking clarity as to which accuracy was acceptable for estimates produced at different 
geographical levels; 
• 
the definition and areas of production and protection forest; 
• 
the fact that only forest reserves were considered. 
The required accuracy of volume estimates appears to have been ±20% on volume estimates, measured 
by the sampling error defined 95% confidence interval (Flint and Hardcastle, 1992; 50). However, as 
table 5.5 shows this level of accuracy was more than achieved for some estimates and far from achieved 
for other estimates. 
Table 5.5 Statistical accuracy of FIP inventory volume 
estimates for class 1 species above 110 cm DBH 
by level of analysis

Source: Flint and Hardcastle (1992; 18) 
31

--- Page 56 ---

At the national level, the sampling design produced very accurate volume estimates for FIP class 1 
species above 110 cm DBH as a group. Even within vegetation zones the level of accuracy for the 
group ofFIP class 1 species above 110 cm DBH was acceptable. However, it is only in 19 ofthe 32 
inventoried FMUs that the SE% for all FIP class 1 species equals or lies below 20% (Flint and 
Hardcastle, 1992; 18). For individual FIP class 1 species above 110 cm DBH, the national estimates 
did not achieve the desired level of accuracy. 
Measured against the (various) objectives of the FIP it is clear that at the national level an accuracy of 
6% on the estimated mean value for the growing stock ofFIP class 1 species above 110 cm DBH is 
more than satisfactoryl8. However, to achieve an accuracy of 20% for the 95% confidence interval on 
the estimated mean volume of all FIP class 1 species above 110 cm DBH it would have sufficed to 
measure approximately 120 ratherthan 1,332 plots as was done (Flint and Hardcastle, 1992; 19). At 
FMU level, and for indi vidual species at the national level, the volume estimates did not, in all cases, 
achieve the desired level of accuracy. 
Accordingly, the FIP static inventory results did only produce statistically satisfactory results at national 
and vegetation zone levels for FIP class 1 species above 110 cm DBH. Practically, all other data on 
the volume of the FIP class 1 growing stock were associated with confidence intervals wider than 20% 
on the estimated means. 
A source of error which was practically ignored by the FIP was the area of production working circles. 
Wong (1989 in FIP, 1989a; 25) only mentions the potential source of area related errors in relation to 
consistency between forest reserves and their map representations, and concludes that the error from 
this source is likely to be small. However, the area of production and protection working circles, which 
were obtained from the regional forest offices, were not investigated by FIP with respect to accuracy 
and relevance. This invited two sources of error: 
• 
The 1.16 million ha of production forest could be an over-estimate ifthe forest condition within 
reserves, due to fire damage, illegal farms, logging damage, etc., suggested that the actual 
productive area was smaller, and/or if the stated 0.43 million ha of protection forest was too small 
to meet national objectives of environmental protection; 
• 
the random identification of plots on forest maps must have placed TSPs within obvious 
protection working circles (steep slopes, watersheds, etc.). If the average stocking of timber in 
these areas differs significantly from obvious production working circles, this would bias the 
national estimates accordingly. 
The above two points relate to the fact (and problem) that the FIP did not stratify the inventory 
according to identified areas within forest reserves which were actually suitable for timber production. 
Ideally, the FIP should have started out by reviewing and perhaps redefining the criteria for (i) 
protection working circles and (ii) areas which are too poorly stocked to justify any harvest within the 
coming 100 years or so. Then such areas should have been identified within all forest reserves and TSPs 
should only have been laid out in areas suitable for logging so as to estimate the growing stock of timber 
available for harvest in the future. 
18 
For FIP class I species above 70 cm DBH the accuracy on the estimated mean 
volume is down to 3% (Flint and Hardcastle, 1992; 94) 
32

--- Page 57 ---

It may be argued that the FIP did not have the information required to identify areas for permanent 
protection and areas for mediumllong-term protection before logging can be allowed. The forest policy 
and forest management prescriptions did at the time not provide an adequate basis for defining which 
areas should be protected, and areal photos or satellite images did either not exist or were unavailable 
at the right quality to geographically locate protection areas. 
However, old maps and management plans might have provided a guide for identifying some areas 
which would qualify for protection. More importantly, the forest quality parameters recorded on TSP 
sub-plots, together with an identification ofTSPs where the stock ofFIP class 1 species were below 
a certain minimum, could have provided valuable information about the area suitable for logging, and 
hence the growing stock available for future harvest. 
Finally, in 1994 the TSP inventory data was analysed according to the recorded parameters offorest 
quality, topographic features, and level of growing stock (FlMP, 1994). The result was thal, depending 
on the criteria for forest protection, the estimated area of production forest was between 0.92 and 0.76 
million ha, (see section 6.1). A similar analysis could, in 1989, have yielded similar information. Since 
FIP failed to do so, the area of production forest was over-estimated by at least 0.24 million ha or 
approximately 20%. 
As it was the increasing timber harvest which primarily produced the need for a forest inventory, it is 
surprising that the FIP never analysed where the extracted wood came from. It was well known that 
timber was extracted both within and outside forest reserves, but at which ratios was pure guesswork. 
The reality was that over 50% of the annual harvest came from off-reserve areas (ITED, 1993; 15). 
With this in mind it is obvious that the inventory should have covered both on- and off-reserve areas in 
the high forest zone. The reason being that when a substantial proportion of the timber harvest comes 
from off-reserve areas it is highly relevant to estimate how long this can continue. Furthermore, the forest 
reserves will be the only source to satisfy the industry's demand for logs after the off-reserve timber 
resources have been logged out. At such point in time it is crucial that the Forestry Department will be 
able to control an increased demand for timber on-reserves. In addition, the substantial off-reserve 
harvest makes it relevant to consider ways ofhaIting or reversing a degradation of the timber resource 
in these areas. 
In summary it may be concluded that: 
• 
The static inventory results as presented in 1989 appeared statistically very accurate, but actually 
the growing stock available for harvest was over-estimated, due to the non-investigated and, 
therefore, over-estimated area of production forese 9 
• 
the inventory results were not accurate enough to be used in management plans for 
FMUs 
• 
the relevance of the inventory in relation to the timber harvest was limited because off-reserve 
areas were ignored. 
19 
It is not possible to calculate how much the growing stock was over-estima-
ted in 1989 by simply mUltiplying the estimated mean volumes per ha by a 
reduced area of production forest, since the average stock per ha varies 
according to different definitions of production forest. 
33

--- Page 58 ---

5.6.2 Critique of the AAC Estimates 
A fundamental problem with national AACs is that, due to data constraints, they are notoriously difficult 
to calculate on a sound scientific basis. At the same time they are treated as highly important figures at 
the policy level. Therefore, FIP' s objective of producing a national AAC appears ill considered, or it 
should at least have been modified when the limitations of the PSP data were realised. 
Of course the difficulty in calculating the AAC is not a valid reason to refrain from attempting to do so. 
After all, any kind of forest management planning will have to make do with the data available. The 
problem arises when policy decisions are based on a calculated national AAC without due consideration 
ofthe fact that this is an estimate with confidence limits of ±SO%, as the case was for Ghana in 1989 
(see section S.4). Hence, the AAC of 1.3 m3/ha Iyear under a 40 year felling cycle for FIP class 1 
species is not an accurate estimate. 
In combination with the over-estimated area of production forest there was obviously a great danger 
(with a likelihood of more than SO%) that the national AAC of I.S million m3 could be far from 
sustainable and damaging to the forest resource if this was actually followed. However, to assess the 
actual harvest against the calculated AAC requires that the actual on-reserve harvest is known. This was 
not the case in 1989. 
5.6.3 The Missing Links Between AAC Estimates, Actual Harvest and Principles of 
Forest Management 
The justification for calculating AACs is naturally to compare these with the actual harvest. Before this 
can take place two things must be fulfilled: 
• 
A system to record the harvest must be in place and it must produce reliable data (recorded 
harvest); 
• 
a relation between recorded harvest and actual harvest must be established. 
Neither of the above two points were fulfilled in 1989. There was a system in place to record extracted 
logs and it produced reasonably reliable data although it is likely that it under-estimates actual extraction 
(the LMC system managed by FPIB). However, the LMC data did not distinguish between logs corning 
from forest reserves and logs coming from off-reserve areas. Only the total volume oflog extracted by 
species was recorded. As mentioned, it took till 1993 before the harvest on- and off-reserves was 
subject to a thorough investigation. This revealed that harvest off-reserves accounted for more than SO% 
of the total harvest (lIED, 1993; 15). 
When, as the case is in Ghana, the AAC refers to bole volume felled and the recorded harvest refers 
to the volume of logs extracted, it is problematic to compare the two without a valid relation between 
them. It is, in other words, necessary to know the level oflogging waste and damage on residual stands 
to calculate an accurate mean ratio between felled volume and extracted volume. Such information was 
not and still is not available in Ghana. In fact, FIP came to the conclusion that is was not possible to 
establish a statistically valid relation between bole volumes and the volume oflogs produced from the 
very same trees (Wong, 1989 in FIP, 1989a; App.l). 
Management of timber harvest inside forest reserves is based on a 100% stock enumeration within 
compartments of approximately 120 ha. Within a compartment all trees above 50 cm DBH are 
34

--- Page 59 ---

identified, their location recorded, their diameter measured and a stock survey number is carved into 
the bark. A "stock map" of the compartment indicating the position, species, diameter and stock survey 
number of each tree is then produced together with a "stock table" where stock survey numbers are 
arranged by species and diameter class. On this basis the trees allocated for felling are selected and a 
yield map and yield table for the compartment showing only these trees is produced. 
Previously, all trees above felling limit were allocated for felling, but in 1990, as a result of the FIP 
results, the first version of the "Interim Yield Allocation Formula,,20 was applied in order to reduce the 
number of trees allocated for felling in each compartment. However, the origin of the formula is obscure. 
Even though the Interim Yield Allocation Formula in its various versions may be considered a valuable 
and simple tool to control the on-reserve harvest, it was not directly derived from PIP data (Flint and 
Hardcastle, 1992; 21). 
Surprisingly, after its introduction the Interim Yield Allocation Formula was not readily applied on TSP 
data to see how the result corresponded with previous estimates of the national AAC. The formula is 
sensitive to the length of the felling cycle and to numbers (and hence volumes) oftrees within diameter 
classes, so applied at the national volume estimates of FIP class 1 species it should produce a pretty 
reliable estimate of the allowed harvest under the formula (leaving out for the moment the then over-
estimated area of production forest). 
Appendix 2 details the result of applying the Normal Interim Yield Allocation Formula (Z = 0.5Y + 
0.2X) on the 1989 TSP volume data assuming a felling cycle of 40 years and approximately 1.16 million 
ha of production forest. For PIP class 1 species taken together it is seen that the annual allowed cut for 
all forest reserves should be approximately 0.94 million m3 or 0.81 m3/ha/year. 
This is only 62% ofGHAFOSIM's estimatedAAC of 1.5 million m3 (1.3 m3/ha/year) under a40year 
felling cycle, and it is below the 1988 recorded harvest of 1.14 million m3• Although the recorded 
harvest cannot, for the above mentioned reasons, be compared directly with calculated allowable or 
allowed cuts, this was actually done. The rough and ready policy analysis was that recorded harvest 
(wrongly interpreted as equal to actual harvest) was within the limits of the AAC. Had the annual 
allowed harvest been calculated according to the Interim Yield Allocation Formula, then the policy 
analysis would have been less optimistic and, despite its questionable interpretation of recorded harvest, 
closer to the truth as subsequent analyses have indicated. 
5.7 Policy Impact of the FIP 
Despite the limited success of the project in terms of producing relevant growing stock estimates, and 
sound estimates ofthe timber growth and AAC, its impact at policy level appears to have been quite 
substantial. Cause and effect relations in policy matters are obviously not possible to establish with a 
100% certainty (or with meaningful confidence intervals for that matter). Policy decisions are always 
20

Allocation Formula calculates the yield as; Z = OAY + 0.2X, where Y is the 
number of trees above felling limit, X is the number of trees in the diameter 
class immediately below felling limit and Z is the number of trees above felling 
limit which are allocated for felling (Ghartey, 1990). However, the formula 
generally applied (called the normal formula) calculates the yield as; Z = 0.5Y 
+ 0.2X (FD, undated; Kakrada and Maginnis, 1992 ; FIMP, 1995b).

influenced by a multitude of factors that sometimes produce the most surprising and unforceable results.

Considering the scientific quality of the data produced by PIP, the alleged policy impact as described 
in table 5.6 is indeed impressing. In particular, it is somewhat surprising that the normally very articulate 
timber industry lobby in Ghana never questioned the validity of AAC estimates.

6 National Forest Inventory Continued (1989-97) 
In 1988, a UK£ 3.7 million agreement was signed between ODA and the GovemmentofGhana to 
essentially continue the FIP under a new name, the Forest Inventory and Management Project (FIMP). 
This project was designed to work within the framework of the wider World Bank, Danida, aDA and 
Government of Ghana sponsored UK£ 20.2 million forestry sector programme, the Forest Resource 
Management Project (FRMP), which commenced in 1988 (FRMP, 1988; 1). 
Unlike its predecessor the FlMP has a project framework. According to this the immediate objectives 
and planned outputs in relation to forest management and inventory were (FIMP, 1988): 
Immediate inventory and management objectives: 
• 
A sustained yield from forest reserves through a 50 year harvesting plan using a 25 year felling 
cycle which applies (a) allowable cut (b) area control (c) minimum girth; 
• 
improvement in the level of efficiency in the management of the forest reserve; 
• 
conservation of the protection working circles, identification of reserves of special floristic interest. 
Planned inventory and management outputs: 
• 
Inventory of 0.6 million ha offorest reserve to complete the inventory of all reserves commenced 
under the ODA's present Forest Inventory Project; 
• 
mapping and inventory of the blocks of manageable forest situated outside the gazetted reserves. 
This will involve the coverage of some 6.4 million ha of land; 
• 
inventory and detailed description of all forest plantations within the tropical high forest zone. 
Believed to cover some 30,000 ha; 
• 
preparation of working plans for the effective management of the forest resources, both with 
respect to the high forest and the plantations. 
• 
rehabilitation of the forest plantations. 
From a cost effectiveness point of view it is surprising that it was possible in 1988 to justify the need for 
a so-called completion of the inventory to cover all forest reserves, since comprehensive data at the 
national level was just about to be produced by the FIP. From the outset of the PIP a larger grid of 
perhaps three by three km covering all forest reserves would probably have been a more sensible 
approach (Flint and Hardcastle, 1992; 67). 
Nonetheless, the remaining forest reserves were inventoried such that during the period 1988-94 a total 
of3, 10 1 TSPs of one ha were sampled from 128 reserves (FIMP, 1995a). Furthermore, the botanical 
work initiated under the PIP yielded impressing and very comprehensive data on the condition of forest 
reserves which included recommendations on forest protection in Ghana. 
The PSP programme was also continued, but as of November 1996 the data collected had not been 
used in an attempt to update forest growth and yield calculations. In any case it is believed that the PSP 
programme should run for another 20-30 years to give definitive data on high forest growth, although 
it should be possible to derive valuable information from the current set of data (Alder, 1996). 
37

--- Page 62 ---

This chapter reviews and summarises the analyses ofTSP data and forest protection strategies which 
have been undertaken during the FTh1P. This work seems to have produced the best documented and 
most comprehensive national level estimates of: 
• 
The production area within forest reserves; 
• 
the growing stock by species and diameter class within forest reserves; 
• 
the sustainable harvest potential in forest reserves; 
• 
the timber resource outside forest reserves. 
6.1 Production and Protection Forest Within Forest Reserves 
In addition to the continued timber inventory a botanical survey was carried out under the FTh1P. The 
aims of the botanical survey were to (Hawthorne and Musah, 1993; 65): 
• 
Build a clearer picture of "what forest, vascular plant lives where" in the forest zone of Ghana and 
thereby to pinpoint parts of Ghana's forested landscape of greater bioquality; 
• 
collect useful background information on the forest with respect to past management, threats to 
the forest, local opinion, etc. which might have some bearing on the protective management of 
the forest. 
The latter aim was subordinate to the former but could presumably be pursued at little extra cost when 
the botanical survey team visited forests to survey plant populations. In addition to the above two aims, 
the botanical survey provided an excellent training ground for botanists at all levels. 
The botanical survey was based on subjectively laid samples (between I and 20 in each forest reserve) 
intended to give the best possible picture of species representation within reserves. Accordingly, the 
survey deliberately made sure to record the species composition in swamps, logged areas, ridge tops, 
steep slopes, river sides, etc. (Hawthorne and Musah, 1993; 66-69). 
6.1.1 Condition Scores for Forest Reserves 
Hawthorne and Musah (1993) apply a condition score between one and six for each forest reserve. The 
scores are based on observation, conversations with forest staff working in the area, the inventory 
results and Spot satellite imagery in descending order of importance (Hawthorne and Musah, 1993; 11). 
Table 6.1 shows the definition of each score.

or peripherical. 
SLIGHTLY DEGRADED: Obviously disturbed or 
degraded and usually patchy, but with good forest predo-
minant. Max. 25% with serious scars and poor regenera-
tion; max. 50% slightly disturbed with broken upper 
canopy. 
MOSTLY DEGRADED: Obviously disturbed and patchy, 
but with bad forest predominant; 25-50% serious scars 
but with max. 75% heavily disrupted canopy. Or forest 
lightly burned throughout. 
VERY POOR: Forest with coherent canopy <25% (more 
than 75% disturbed), or more than half of the forest with 
serious scars and poor or no forest regeneration; or 
almost all heavily burnt with conspicuous Eupatorium 
and other pioneers throughout. Not, however, qualifying 
as condition 6. 
NO SIGNIFICANT FOREST LEFT: Almost all deforested 
with savanna, plantation or farm, etc.; <2% good forest; 
or 2-5% very disturbed forest left; or 5-10% left in extre-
mely poor condition, ego as scattered trees or riverine 
fragments. Remnants with little chance of surviving 10 
years. 
Source: Hawthorne and Musah (1993; II)

The most important information to be drawn from table 6.2 is that 9,021 km2 or about half of the 
gazetted forest reserves are in reasonable condition (scores 1-3) with only 16% of the total 
reserved area having the lightest or no disturbance in recent history (scores 1-2). The reason being 
that large areas, particularly in the semi-deciduous zone, have suffered excessively from fire and 
logging, although a number of reserves in this zone carried no forest at the time of reservation 
(Hawthorne and Musah, 1993; 13). 
6.1.2 Previous Protection of Forest Resources 
Forest conservation in Ghana through the gazettment of reserves was very influenced and guided 
by the environmental and protective functions offorests. However, upon a review of the history 
of forest protection in Ghana Hawthorne and Musah (1993) conclude that: 
• 
Protection working circles within forest reserves were introduced in the working plans of 
the 1950s-1960s, but they lacked a clear overall definition. The establishment of protection 
working circles and prescriptions for their management appear to have rested entirely with 
the district and regional forestry officers; 
• 
practically unloggable reserves were often defined as protection reserves; 
• 
notwithstanding past definition as protection reserves or protection working circles, many 
of these areas have de facto been subject to extremely heavy logging, taungya farming, 
mining, etc. 
The overall description of forest protection in Ghana is a failure of management. To a large 
extent, the concepts have degenerated and upon the slightest pressure the protective legislation 
or management prescriptions have crumbled. Furthermore, a theoretical framework for protection 
has been non-exacting, unclear or very often non-existent (Hawthorne and Musah, 1993; 20). 
Although forest plant communities are not the sensitive ecosystems that many environmentalists 
claim, it is very likely that most of the forests in Ghana would become seriously degraded or die 
if current patterns of utilisation do not change (Hawthorne and Musah, 1993; 42). The types of 
40

--- Page 65 ---

exploitation to be protected against most forcefully are those that affect directly the main 
structural elements of the forest, the canopy trees, soils and water courses. Serious adverse effects 
on the forest as a whole arising from exploitation of non-canopy forest produce is hard to 
envisage, although the populations of the species being exploited may well suffer (Hawthorne and 
Musah, 1993; 21). Accordingly, Hawthorne and Musah (1993) developed a new framework for 
forest protection. 
6.1.3 A New National Framework for Forest Protection 
Against current trends in nature conservation, Hawthorne and Musah (1993; 7) argue that 
protective regulation and needs should be decided at the national level and enforced downwards. 
Local needs for conservation should then be superimposed on the national requirements, not 
replace them. 
This, apparently controversial, approach to forest and nature conservation may be justified by the 
fact that needs for conservation may, in many cases, only be realised at the broadest scale (at 
national level or higher). Very rare plants and animals are, for example, often abundant over small 
areas (Hawthorne and Musah, 1993; 7). 
Based on the botanical survey and the analysis of current measures for forest protection 
Hawthorne and Musah (1993) recommend a new framework for forest protection. This 
framework is divided into two concepts, fine-grained and large-grained protection. 
Fine-grained protection concerns management of single trees and small patches of forest. It 
should generally be interpreted as management rules that apply to all forest uses (logging in 
particular) in all production areas, ie. outside larger areas which are set aside entirely for 
protection. Fine-grained protection should according to Hawthorne and Musah (1993; v-vii) 
include: 
• 
An upper limit on the number of trees allocated for felling per unit area (about 1.5 trees per 
ha); 
• 
yield allocation procedures which take into account the ecology of certain over-exploited 
species, non-pioneer species and species with less widely-broadcast seeds and regeneration 
(special felling permits and reduced yield for certain species); 
• 
protection of desirable phenotypes as seed-trees in yield allocation; 
• 
attention towards the protection of sensitive landscape areas, particularly localised steep 
slopes and streams that do not show on 1 :50,000 maps; 
• 
attention to forest condition when the yield is allocated such that harvest in fire-damaged 
and fire-prone forest is reduced; 
• 
attention to the spatial distribution of other trees at national level and in compartments of 
forest near to the one being considered when yield is allocated; 
• 
total protection of very rare species (eg. Talbotiella gentii which is only found in Ghana); 
• 
a total stop of converting viable natural forest into plantations within reserves; 
• 
control of unavoidable mining and quarrying such that areas for these purposes are kept as 
small as practicable, and progressing activity is made subject to adequate restoration of 
already worked areas. 
Large-grained forest protection will usually be at least the size of a compartment (approximately 
120 ha). Protection in these areas need only apply to trees above 5 cm DBH and other canopy 
plants and rare species. Exploitation of NTFPs is, therefore, not necessarily excluded in these 
41

--- Page 66 ---

areas. Large-grained protection is recommended to include the following (Hawthorne and Musah, 
1993; vii-ix): 
• 
Southern dry forests in the SM and SO forest types. The remaining forests often fall outside 
forest reserves, but in general they represent a threatened forest type and serve as refuges 
for a large number of rare species; 
• 
hill sanctuaries with slopes mostly steeper than 15 degrees; 
• 
fire protection blocks fringing the savanna zone or other very burned and fire-prone areas; 
• 
provenance protection areas for community samples and economic "genebanks" where 
timber trees are completely protected from exploitation; 
• 
convalescence protection areas in severely damaged forest reserves where the forest should 
be allowed to recover from past over-exploitation through the enforcement of a particularly 
long felling cycle (significantly longer than the 40 year cycle applied in well stocked 
forest); 
• 
special biological protected areas with either an unusual abundance of rare species or of an 
unusual community type; 
• 
sacred forests and forest areas which serve religious and spiritual purposes; 
• 
other environmentally protective blocks like extensive swamps. 
It is obvious that, if implemented, the above recommendations on forest protection would affect 
the area of production forest and the allowed level of harvest within protection forests. However, 
Hawthorne and Musah (1993) do not attempt to explicitly estimate the effects offorest protection 
on timber harvest, although they state that a lot of the areas which are recommended to be 
protected from logging are pretty inaccessible anyway. 
6.1.4 Scenarios of Forest Protection 
The survey by Hawthorne and Musah (1993) included reserves and forest areas which were not 
inventoried for timber under the FIMP, but they did not make explicit use of the TSP data in order 
to estimate the cumulative area within forest reserves which they thought should be protected. 
Yet, in 1994, the recommendations of Hawthorne and Musah (1993) were operationalised (PIMP, 
I 994a). 
The approach was, on the basis of Hawthorne and Musah (1993), to develop a number of forest 
protection scenarios for the inventoried forest reserves and then apply the TSP data set, assuming 
that one plot represents 400 ha in accordance with the 0.25% sampling intensity. In other words, 
the scenarios represent different levels of large-grained forest protection. The categories of 
protection areas used to develop the scenarios were defined as follows (FIMP, 1994a): 
• 
Hill sanctuaries: Areas that generally have slopes greater than 30% on 1 :62,500 (inch to 
mile) scale topographic sheets. By translating the maps into TSPs this gives a total area of 
approximately 210,400 ha; 
• 
swamp sanctuaries: Areas where the TSP sub-plot codes indicate more than 60% swamp 
in the dry season. This gave 45 plots in 27 reserves, or a total area of 18,000 ha; 
• 
special biological protection areas: Areas with a high density ofrare species. To a certain 
extent these areas fall under hill or swamp sanctuaries as defined above, but additional 
areas in six forest reserves are included; 
• 
shelter belts: These areas/reserves are not specifically addressed by Hawthorne and Musah 
(1993), but they were originally gazetted with a view to stabilise the local climate. The 
shelter belts are in general only a few kilometres wide and several tens of kilometres long 
42

--- Page 67 ---

which makes them vulnerable to degradation, especially by fire. In total the area of shelter 
belts comes to 22,000 ha; 
• 
intact forest sanctuaries: Areas which, in addition to provenance protection areas (see 
below), contain intact forest. Intact forest is defined by an exceptionally high basal area for 
the vegetation zone and a low density of pioneer species. In total this area amounts to 2,400 
ha, although concrete investigations on the ground may justify a smaller area; 
• 
provenance protection: Areas carrying a high density of high value and heavily exploited 
timber species. The areas are identified on flat lands in all vegetation zones (not in swamps 
or hills) which represent the best forest and because certain high value species do not occur 
in swamps and on hills. In total this area amounts to 8,400 ha, although concrete 
investigations on the ground may justify a smaller area; 
• 
fire protection: Areas, particularly in the DS zone, which form the border of reserves. The 
area for fire protection was identified through TSPs that fall within 1,000 m of the boundary 
of the reserves in the DS zone, assuming that a minimum of 1,000 m width is required for 
fire protection; 
• 
convalescence: Poorly stocked areas which have a basal area between 5 and 15 m2 per ha. 
In total this amounts to 122,000 ha; 
• 
conversion: Areas with less than 5 m3 per ha, ie. areas where extensive planting is required 
to establish a forest. 
In addition, the continued inventory under the FIMP omitted a number of reserves, covering an 
area of270,000 ha, which were known to contain insignificant areas of natural forest or if they 
were too small to be considered for logging. Accordingly, a total of 128 reserves covering 
1,293,600 ha were inventoried through 3101 TSPs of one ha. 
Three scenarios were considered. A minimum scenario (scenario 1) according to which only hill 
sanctuaries, swamp sanctuaries and conversion areas (where logging is difficult or impossible 
anyway) are protected. A compromise scenario (scenario 2) where, in addition to scenario 1, 
special biological protection areas, shelter belts and convalescence areas are protected. A 
sustainable/ideal scenario (scenario 3) where all categories of protection areas are included. Table 
6.3 summarises the area impact of the three scenarios.

In practice, the exact implications in terms of areas to be protected under the three scenarios would to

(1994a), certainly provides the best available information.

6.2 Growing Stock within Forest Reserves 
As indicated above it is not so much the total growing stock within forest reserves which should be of 
interest, but rather the on-reserve growing stock on areas which are suitable for logging and the on-
reserve forest condition (including estimates of the growing stock) in areas which should be protected 
from logging. 
However, such information has only been indirectly available in the form of AAC estimates based on 
the stocking of areas suitable for logging (see below). What has been made available are the mean 
volumes per km2 based on the inventory of31 0 1 plots of one ha in 128 reserves. Table 6.6 summarises 
the result (appendix 3 provides a species specific table for all FIP class 1 species).

As one would expect, the volume estimates in table 6.6 do not differ much from the FIP estimates of

refinements of volume estimates. 
Yet, one of the justifications for continuing the static inventory after 1989 apparently was to produce 
estimates that were sufficiently accurate atFMU level. Although comprehensive direct data on this issue 
has not been published it is possible to make a rough estimate of the 1995 statistical accuracy of volume 
estimates on the basis of (i) the confidence limits shown in table 5.5 and (ii) the increased number of 
TSPs inventoried. 
If it is assumed that the sampling variances are similar for the FIP and FIMP static inventory data 
(reasonably realistic), and that the inventories were based on random sampling (more problematic), then 
the expected confidence interval for estimates based on the FIMP data (SE%FIMP) should be the 
confidence interval for FIP estimates (SE%FIP) times the square root ofTSPs under the FIP (1332) 
divided by the square root of TSPs under the FIMP (3101). Expressed mathematically: 
Although the above assumptions do not hold entirely true, the calculation is likely to produce fairly 
reliable figures for the accuracy of the FIMP volume estimates. Table 6.7 presents the results of applying 
the above formula on the figures in table 5.5.

As table 6.7 shows the probable accuracy for volume estimates at the national and vegetation zone

produce reliable estimates, especially when estimates concern groups of species (cf. below on AAC 
estimates). 
6.3 The Sustainable Harvest Potential in Forest Reserves 
The harvest potential on forest reserves is naturally closely linked to the area of production forest. 
However, the drastically reduced area of production forest from 1,159,000 ha to 762,000 hadoes not 
result in a proportionally reduced AAC, because the growing stock per ha is higher on the smaller area. 
The AAC from reserves does not only depend on the productive area. Fine-grained protection within 
production forest must also be taken into account. In practice, the recommendations of Hawthorne and 
Musah (1993) are being implemented under two headings: 
. 
• 
Yield regulation (according to species rarity and vegetation zone) ; 
• 
protection of areas from logging in production forest. 
6.3.1 Classification of Species According to Rarity 
Yield regulation at species level must be based on the rarity of species. In relation to timber exploitation, 
economic extinction of timber species in Ghana is far more likely than biological extinction since the 
marginal cost of harvesting will become prohibitively high long before the very last trees are felled. 
However, over-exploitation of a given species is likely to focus particularly on the financially most 
desirable phenotypes (straight boles, few branches, etc.). This will reduce genetic variance and the 
species will increasingly be represented by trees that are undesirable for timber production. 
47

--- Page 72 ---

As part of the computer based Forest Reserves Graphical Information Exhibitor (FROGGIE) which 
was developed on the basis of the botanical survey, Hawthorne (1993a & 1993b) developed the 
"Reddish star" terminology. This was done in an attempt to categorise commercial timber species 
according to the risk of genetic variance (quality) being reduced as a result of exploitation. Reddish stars 
were developed into three groups (Hawthorne, 1993b; 31-32): 
Pink Stars: 
Red Stars: 
Scarlet Stars: 
Significantly exploited but not yet so as to cause concern for their economic future. 
Pink species will probably become more exploited. 
Current rates of exploitation present a significant danger of economic damage over, 
say, a ten year period. 
Under more imminent threat than Red Stars. 
In addition to the Reddish Stars, Hawthorne (1993b; 15-19) developed the following Star categories 
to reflect conservation priorities for species that are not commercially exploited: 
Green Stars: 
Blue Stars: 
Gold Stars: 
Black Stars: 
Species of no particular conservation concern. That is, species which are fairly 
common both in the forests of Ghana and surrounding countries; or at a wider 
geographical scale and not heavily exploited in Ghana. 
Species that are not rare or under economic pressure but less common than Green 
stars. In general, they are either fairly common in all Ghana's forests and less 
common in surrounding countries; or restricted to few zones in Ghana while being 
common in surrounding countries. 
Species that are common in only a few zones in Ghana; or not common in Ghana but 
sparsely represented in several zones of near surrounding countries; or widespread 
in Ghana but hardly found outside of Ghana. 
Common in only a few zones in Ghana and not found elsewhere; or not common in 
Ghana but sparsely represented in several zones in near surrounding countries; or not 
common in Ghana and restricted to a few zones in near surrounding countries. 
In order to allocate timber species according to the above classes Hawthorne (1 993b; App. B) presents 
a table including the number of trees by species and diameter class found in 2624 TSPs as well as the 
LMC totals (officially recorded harvest) for 1989. On this basis he determines the degree of exploitation 
by estimating the hypothetical area of "average" forest needed to sustain the 1989 recorded harvest. 
This is simply done for each species by dividing the recorded 1989 harvest by the average population 
density above 90 and 60 cm DBH. The actual criteria used by Hawthorne (1993b) to allocate species 
into star classes are not entirely clear. By analysing Hawthorne's data FIMP (1 994b ) concludes that 
Scarlet Stars are generally those which require more than 500 km2 of forest. Red Stars generally require 
between 100 and 500 km2 of forest. Pink, Blue and Green Stars were allocated more subjectively and 
depended on the size of the population as well as the degree of exploitation. 
FIMP (1994b) modifies the star ratings of Hawthorne (1993b) by using the recorded average harvest 
of species during the period 1989-93 and a calculated annual national felling quota for each species. The 
48

--- Page 73 ---

annual quota is calculated as the national (on-reserve) stocking above minimum felling diameter divided 
by the applied 40 year felling cycle22• Star ratings are then defined as follows (FIMP, 1994b): 
Pink Stars: 
Red Stars: 
Scarlet Stars: 
Species for which the recorded average annual harvest 1989-94 is less than 50% 
of the calculated annual quota. 
Species for which the recorded average annual harvest 1989-94 is between 50 and 
200% of the calculated annual quota. 
Species for which the recorded average annual harvest 1989-94 is more than 200% 
of the calculated annual quota. 
A list of species with previous designations, Hawthorne's (1993b) and FIMP' s (1994b) starrating is 
found in appendix 123 . 
6.3.2 Yield Regulation 
On-reserve yield regulation is currently based on a 40 year felling cycle, species specific minimum felling 
diameters and a yield formula (the Interim Yield Allocation Formula). Within compartments of 
approximately 120 ha all trees above 50 cm DBH are identified according to species, located on a map 
of the compartment (stock map), measured and enumerated with a stock survey number carved in the 
bark (the whole process referred to as a 100% stock survey). The yield formula is applied on each 
species to calculate the number of trees to be allocated for felling. Trees allocated for felling are selected 
on the stock map and identified by their stock survey number. The compartment yield is the total number 
of trees including all species allocated for felling. 
Fine-grained forest protection is pursued by applying the "normal" and the "reduced" yield formula 
according to species rarity and fire-proneness. 
The normal yield formula (Z = 0.2X + 0.5Y) is applied on Red and Pink Star species as defined by 
FIMP (1994b) in the WE, ME, MSSE and MSNW vegetation zones. 
The reduced yield formula (Z = 0.2X + O.2SY) is applied on Scarlet Star species as defined by FIMP 
(1994b) in all vegetation zones and on all species in the DS zone, which is generally more fire-prone 
than other vegetation zones. 
In the above formulae Z is the number of trees allocated for felling, Y is the number of trees above 
minimum felling diameter and X is the number of trees in the diameter class (20 cm) immediately below 
felling limit. In cases where Y is less thanZ the yield is reduced to Y-2, allowing for a retention of 
minimum two seed trees for each species per compartment. 
22 
23 
This calculation assumes (i) that off-reserve harvest compensates for the on-reserve areas 
which cannot or may not be harvested, (ii) that this use of on-reserve TSP data represents 
the best estimate of total national stocking and (iii) that the exploitable stock can be 
replenished during the 40 year felling cycle (FIMP, 1994b). 
In the appendix Green and one Blue Star species occur (see section 6.3.1 for 
definitions). 
49

--- Page 74 ---

Whether the yield formulae are sustainable, in the sense that they will maintain or enhance the harvest 
potential (trees allocated for felling) over subsequent felling cycles of 40 years, is still an open question. 
The formulae will over-allocate the yield if X is not large enough to generate the present number ofY 
for the next felling cycle, taking mortality into account. Assessed against Alder's (1992) method for 
calculating sustainable yield in mixed tropical forests the yield formulae generally produce sustainable 
yields for groups of species, but assessed against Vanclay' s (1993) suggested method of calculating 
critical de Liocourt's coefficients24, the formulae are generally not sustainable (FIMP, 1995b). 
This suggests that the formulae will be modified when new knowledge on forest growth in Ghana arises. 
In the interim, regulation of the yield according to the formulae appears pragmatic and sound, not least 
because they are easy to comprehend and should be possible to implement in practice. 
6.3.3 Fine-grained Protection in Production Forest 
Fine-grained protection in timber production areas is stipulated in the Logging Manual (FD, 1992), 
which restricts logging on slopes between 21 % and 29% and bans it on slopes great\!r than 30%. 
Furthermore, logging is prohibited in a 50 m wide zone on each side of streams occurring on 1 :50,000 
scale topographic maps and in 25 m wide zones on each side of "minor" streams. Hawthorne and 
Musah (1993) recommend that areas of swamp are also included in fine-grained protection, and it is 
the intention of Forestry Department to follow this advise (FIMP, 1995b). 
Through analysis of the TSP sub-plot data which record slope, swamp and watercourse, the area 
impact of fine-grained protection is estimated to take out approximately 14% of the productive forest. 
Accordingly, when fine-grained protection is applied the total on-reserve area of forest suitable for 
logging is reduced from 762,400 ha to 645,080 ha. Under the 40 year felling cycle this yields an annual 
coupe of 16,127 ha. 
However, the impact on species availability is not proportional to the area since the stocking of 
commercial species is relatively low in riverine and swamp areas. The protected 14% ofthe area only 
accounts for 8.7 % ofthe Scarlet, 10.7 % of the Red and 9.9% ofthe Pink Star species (FIMP, 1995b). 
6.3.3 The AAC in Forest Reserves 
By applying the yield formulae on TSP data for areas defined as suitable for logging, the AAC for forest 
reserves has been estimated by FIMP (1995b). Table 6.8 summarises the result (appendix 4 provides 
a species specific list of the AACs). 
24 
The de Liocourt's coefficient, Q is the ratio between the numbers of trees in two adjacent 
size classes expressed as a ratio of the smaller size class (Q>1 is generally the case). Critical 
Qs may be calculated as Q = (1 - 0.5S)I(DG - 0.2S), where S is the 40-year probability of 
survival, G is the probability of surviving and growing one or more classes, D is the 
probability of surviving the harvest undamaged, 0.5 and 0.2 come from the normal yield 
formula. PSP data suggests values for S of 0.658, G of 0.498 and D of 0.8. This gives a critical 
Q of 2.5 for the normal yield formula and 1.9 for the reduced yield formula. Calculated Qs 
(Y/X) for groups of species are, in general, below the critical Qs (FIMP, 1995b). 
50
