Given the high level of accuracy for species specific estimates of standing volumes, the figures 
in table 6.8 must be considered very reliable. Accordingly, the sustainable harvest should be 
between 1.17 m3/ha/year and 1.24 m3/ha/year without and with fine-grained protection, 
respectively. Interestingly these estimates do not differ much from those of Silviconsult (1985) 
and Alder (1989). As a matter of fact, they are practically similar. 
Yet, the estimated total AAC of approximately 850,000 m3 is significantly less than the previous 
estimate of 1.5 million m3 by Alder (1989). The reason being an ill informed and over-optimistic 
assessment of the area of production forest in 1989. As mentioned in chapter 5 the FIP could, in 
principle, have used TSP data in much the same way as FIMP (1995b) in order to estimate the 
on-reserve area of unproductive forest and the on-reserve area of productive forest which should 
be protected. A less optimistic AAC would have been the result. 
Even though an AAC of 850,000 m3 from forest reserves must be considered a very reliable 
estimate, the relation between recorded harvest and the AAC has still not been entirely clarified. 
The effect of logging damage on the residual stand should, in a crude form, be catered for in the 
yield formulae. Yet, the average amount of logging waste and possible under-reporting of 
volumes extracted from forest reserves are still unquantified factors. 
6.4 Off-Reserve Inventory 
As mentioned at the beginning of this chapter the FIMP had among its objectives to undertake 
mapping and inventory of the blocks of manageable forest situated outside the gazetted reserves 
(FIMP, 1988). However, this part of the project was not implemented until 1996-97. Apparently 
there were two reasons for this: 
• 
Off-reserve forest and timber resources did not fall under the jurisdiction and management 
responsibility of the Forestry Department until 1996; 
• 
the off-reserve timber harvest was not believed to be as significant as turned out to be the 
case when the issue was investigated. 
51

--- Page 76 ---

The late implementation of the off-reserve timber inventory is explained by the fact that until 
1996 the Forestry Department's management responsibility was restricted to forest reserves 
(FIMP, 1997a). Since FIMP is implemented within the framework of the Forestry Department it 
is possible that the project could only justify an off-reserve inventory when the Forestry 
Department became official management authority for off-reserve timber and forest resources. 
However, this appears a professionally questionable justification for postponing the off-reserve 
inventory which was planned and included in the FIMP project document (FIMP, 1988) at a point 
in time where the Forestry Department did not hold any management responsibility whatsoever 
for off-reserve forest and timber resources. 
By analysing the annual felling returns for forest reserves (submitted by forest districts to Forestry 
Department Headquarters), lIED (1993; App. 1) managed to estimate the on-reserve harvest by 
species25• Off-reserve harvest by species could then be estimated by subtracting on-reserve harvest 
from LMC totals. 
This exercise produced quite remarkable results. It documented that during the period 1986-91 
off-reserve harvest accounted for well over 50% of the total harvest (lIED, 1993; 15) (see chapter 
7). Furthermore, Hawthorne and Musha (1993) had documented that many off-reserve forest 
resources have a high frequency of rare tree species. In addition, off-reserve forest and trees 
provide environmental services and NTFPs, and serve cultural and spiritual purposes. 
The social aspects of managing off-reserve forest and timber resources are, therefore, manyfold 
and diverse. Yet, a crucial factor is the high level of commercial timber harvest off-reserves. 
Since the timber industry depends heavily on this dwindling resource there is reason to be 
concerned over what will happen if and when forest reserves become the only source to satisfy 
the demand from industrial timber. Will the Forestry Department be able to withstand the 
pressure, or will the walls come tumbling down if financial interests of timber companies clash 
with the principles of sustainable forest management? 
Accordingly, the documentation produced by lIED (1993) and Hawthorne and Musah (1993) were 
good reasons for considering options for managing off-reserve forest and timber resources with 
a view to sustain at least some of them and slow down off-reserve deforestation. An off-reserve 
timber inventory is the obvious basis for turning such considerations into adequate 
management planning. 
6.4.1 Design of the Off-Reserve Inventory 
The inventory covers off-reserve areas in the high forest zone with the exception of towns, 
villages, savanna grassland and large scale agricultural areas (rubber plantations, oil palm 
plantations, commercial farms). A total of 5,482,178 ha was covered by the inventory. 
Originally it was the plan to operate with a sampling intensity of 500 plots, but due to logistical 
constraints it was decided to reduce the total number of plots to 200. The plot size was two ha 
(1000 by 20 m) and the 200 plots were allocated approximately according to the area coverage 
of vegetation zones. The following stratification was applied (FIMP, 1997a): 
25 
The calculations of lIED (1993) assume on the basis of Kakrada and Maginnis (1992) that 
Forestry Department felling returns under-estimate actual harvest by 15%. 
52

--- Page 77 ---

• 
50 randomly located plots within each of the MSSE, the MSNW and the DS vegetation 
zones; 
• 
50 plots randomly located within the area covered by the WE and ME vegetation zones. 
Each plot was subdivided into 20 sub-plots (50 by 20 m) and the following recorded on each sub-
plot: 
• 
Dominant land-use or vegetation type (six categories) 
• 
site factors (five categories) 
• 
trees above 10 cm DBH identified for species and diameter measured 
• 
condition of each tree is determined (nine categories) 
• 
cause of death is recorded for dead trees (six categories).

As table 6.10 shows, the confidence intervals for mean numbers of trees within diameter classes 
meet or are close to the general target of 20%. Presumably, the volume estimates presented in 
table 6.9 are based on (i) the number of trees per ha, (ii) diameter-volume equations from the 
PIMP on-reserve inventory and (iii) the estimated off-reserve area of 5,482, 178 ha. Accordingly, 
the volume estimates must be associated with a somewhat higher standard error (a wider 
confidence interval) than the average numbers of trees per ha. It is not possible to calculate the 
exact size of confidence intervals for volume estimates, but considering the experience from on-
reserve inventories it seems likely that the estimated off-reserve area carrying timber and forest 
resources will change if and when this issue is made subject to further investigations.

The "mining" scenarios of tables 6.11 and 6.12 show that if only the Scarlet Star species are harvested 
off-reserves, this financially most valuable resource will be logged out within a foreseeable future. On 
the other hand, the total size of the off-reserve timber resource suggests that is not too late to implement 
some kind of off-reserve management planning with the objectives to maintain: 
• 
A certain off-reserve tree cover to cater for social and spiritual needs, provide environmental 
protection and preserve biological values; 
• 
a sustained supply oftimber from off-reserve areas in the interest of the timber industry and the 
sustainable management of forest reserves. 
55

--- Page 80 ---

However, time may be running out fasterthan the tables 6.11 and 6.12 indicate. If the substantial off-
reserve harvest is concentrated on Scarlet Star species, and if the off-reserve area where timber harvest 
is financially feasible turns out to be significantly smaller than the estimated 5,4482, 178 ha, then the 
pressure on forest reserves for timber might increase tremendously within the next five or ten years. This 
issue is further analysed in chapter 7. 
In addition to industrial timber harvest, the rural population contributes to off-reserve deforestation. 
Clearings for farmland are bound to continue and people are likely to get rid of trees on fannland unless 
they have c1earpositive financial incentives to grow and protect trees on the land they cultivate. This is 
analysed further in chapter 13. 
56

--- Page 81 ---

7 Analysis of the Timber Harvest 
As mentioned in the previous chapters it is necessary to distinguish between on- and off-reserve timber 
harvest in order to assess the sustainability of timber harvest in the high forest zone. This chapter 
attempts to combine the on- and off-reserve inventory results with the available information about the 
on- and off-reserve timber harvest. 
7.1 The Log Measurement Certificate System 
Since 1988 the Forest Product Inspection Bureau (FPID) has published so-called Log Measurement 
Certificate totals (LMC totals) which detail the recorded annual timber extraction by species and by 
timber company (the latter used to bill timber royalties). Timber trucks are stopped at road check-points 
throughout the high forest zone and the following information about the load of logs is 
recorded: 
• 
Tree species; 
• 
under bark volume of logs; 
• 
number of logs per tree; 
• 
stock survey number (if the log comes from a forest reserve); 
• 
name of forest reserve (if the log comes from a forest reserve); otherwise the log must be marked 
with the letters "OFR" to indicate it comes from an of-reserve area; 
• 
locality mark identifying the origin of the log in terms of administrative district and land owning 
stool (local community); 
• 
the timber company's property mark. 
Until 1993, the LMC system did not distinguish between logs from on- and off-reserves. However, for 
the period 1993-95 LMC data for on- and off-reserve timber extraction, respectively, are not reliable. 
It simply took three years to go from just recording the total extraction to record on- and off-reserve 
extraction specifically. 
7.1.1 The Quality of the LMC data 
In relation to growth estimates and estimates of the standing volume two issues must be considered 
when using the LMC data: 
• 
The relation between bole volume and volume of extracted logs from felled trees; 
• 
the effectiveness of road check-points in monitoring timber extraction. 
The first issue has been assessed in section 5.2.1 and basically the question remains unanswered since 
it has so far proved impossible to establish a statistically acceptable relation between bole and log 
volumes. Yet, it is clear that the volume of extracted logs is lower than the bole volume of trees from 
which they origin. On average this difference is approximately 10% (FIP, 1989a; 29). In addition, 
allowance should be made for the volume of trees which are felled but not extracted due to defects (rot, 
hollowness or cracks). Unfortunately, this is another issue which has not been subjectto quantitative 
assessment. 
Is the LMC system effective? How many logs pass unrecorded by the FPID road check-points? Again, 
these are important questions which cannot be answered given the information available. Since royalties 
are generally billed on the basis ofLMC data, timber companies have a financial incentive to avoid the 
57

--- Page 82 ---

LMC system. Another reason to avoid check-points is when logs from illegally felled trees are 
transported. This may be under-sized trees or trees from areas where the harvesting activity lacks a legal 
basis, ego on other companies' or unallocated concessions. 
Both reasons to avoid the LMC system are known to apply in practice. Timber trucks do sometimes 
not stop at FPIB road check-points, logs are illegally transported during hours when the FPIB road 
check-points are not manned (public holidays, week -ends and after 6 p.m. during working days) and 
trees are illegally felled on other companies' concessions. We have personally witnessed all three 
offences. It is also generally realised within the Ministry of Lands and Forestry that these illegal activities 
do take place. 
Nonetheless, the LMC system has never been subject to a comprehensive official assessment. In view 
of the time and resources spent on timber inventories and the objectives of doing so it appears illogic 
and very surprising that the quality and relevance of harvesting records has attracted so little interest. 
One study by the Forestry Research Institute of Ghana (FORIG) revealed that many logs from under-
sized trees pass through the FPIB check -points. Of 4,023 logs examined at two major FPIB check-
points (Kumasi and Takoradi) approximately 45% were found to stem from under-sized trees, and logs 
from under -sized trees were found for 34 out of the 52 species that were included in the study (FORIG, 
1994; 5-8). Furthermore, the study documented a large number of irregularities in log markings which 
made it impossible to determine the origin oflogs and the company responsible for the felling (which is 
not necessarily the same as the company transporting the logs) (FORIG, 1994; 21-22). These results 
will be further analysed in relation to procedures for managing the timber resource of which the minimum 
felling diameter is one (see section 11.2). 
Unfortunately, the study of FORIG (1994) did not consider to which extent logs pass unrecorded 
through FPIB check -points. However, the obvious financial incentive to do so, together with the number 
of documented other irregularities, suggest that this is likely to happen when research teams and the like 
are not around to detect this. 
Despite the uncertainties and obvious weaknesses of the LMC system it must be concluded that LMC 
totals form the most comprehensive information available about: 
• 
The annual extraction of timber by species; 
• 
the extraction of timber from on- and off-reserves (since 1996). 
However, for comparisons with inventory data it should be remembered that LMC figures undoubtedly 
under-estimate the actual harvest. Judging by the available (but inadequate) information, actual 
harvested volume is likely to be at least 10% above the recorded LMC volume. Whether the correct 
figure is significantly higher (as one might suspect) is an open question. To avoid the introduction of 
questionable and arbitrary assessments, the LMC figures in the tables below and the tables presented 
in appendices to this study are those officially recorded. Implications of the quality ofLMC figures are 
discussed under the interpretation of data. 
58

--- Page 83 ---

7.2 Felling Recorded by the Forestry Department 
The Forestry Department has a system which records on-reserve felling. District forest officers (DFOs) 
are supposed to keep records of on-reserve felling within their district and submit felling reports 
(sometimes referred to as felling returns) to Forestry Department Headquarters. 
7.2.1 The Quality of Forestry Department Felling Returns 
Kakrada and Maginnis (1992) report that the felling returns which forest guards report to their district 
forest offices are often based on information from the concessionaire bush manager. Accordingly, it is 
likely that these felling returns understate the actual harvest, since timber royalties may be billed on the 
basis of felling returns instead of LMC figures. 
Furthermore, Kakrada and Maginnis (1992) report that district forest officers were often unaware of 
the scale of this problem, and it was found that under-reporting was in a range offive to ten percent. 
However, IIED (1993; app. 1) assumes that the under-reporting offelling returns is 15%. Data are too 
meagre to determine the correct degree of under-reporting in felling returns. In any case this is not so 
important because the most interesting information to be drawn from the data is historical trends in on-
and off-reserve harvest at the level of species groups. 
7.3 This Study's Use of Available Data on Timber Harvest 
This study adopts the methodology ofIIED (1993) and estimates off-reserve harvest for the period 
1973-91 by subtracting recorded on-reserve harvest plus 15% fromLMC totals. For 1992, on- and 
off-reserve data are from FIMP (1995b). For 1993, LMC data are used directly although they must 
be considered unreliable. 
For the period 1994-96, the LMC totals specify extracted volumes from unidentified sources. In 1994 
a total of approximately 1,470,000 m3 was recorded as coming from unidentified sources. In 1995 this 
figure was down to approximately 253,000 m3, and in 1996 the LMC totals include only approximately 
60,000 m3 from unidentified sources (see appendix 5). As an estimate this study allocates the volumes 
from unidentified sources to on- and off-reserve extractions by the ratios of 1992. This is only done to 
indicate the development in on- and off-reserve harvest for the entire period 1973-96. 
For specific analysis of harvest and estimated AACs, LMC data from 1996 will be used. These data 
are, despite the problems discussed in section 7.1.1, assumed to be the best available. 
7.4 On- and Off-reserve Harvest 
The total recorded timber extraction from on- and off-reserves is summarised in the figures 7.1 and 7.2 
( appendix 6 provides the data in tabular form).

--- Page 85 ---

The curves in figures 7.1 and 7.2 clearly show that off-reserve timber extraction has always accounted 
for a substantial share of the total recorded timber extraction in Ghana. More often than not the 
recorded extraction of timber off-reserves is higher than the recorded on-reserve timber extraction, and 
during periods where off-reserve extraction is below on-reserve extraction the difference between the 
two is, with the exception of 1971-75, very small. 
From 1971 onwards there is a tendency for decreasing on-reserve extraction to be compensated by 
increasing off-reserve extraction. This has been taken as an indicator of the Forestry Department being 
more successful in controlling on-reserve harvest, which should make timber companies use off-reserve 
resources as a "buffer" to balance variations in the availibility of on-reserve timber resources (lIED, 
1993; 16). 
However, this interpretation of the timber extraction curves does not cover the entire picture. The log 
export market has, for good financial reasons, had a tremendous impact on the felling activity in Ghana. 
This was last documented in 1991-94. Following rumours about a coming log export ban on Wawa, 
this species was harvested and exported at increasing rates until 1993 when the ban became reality. 
Through a series of partial log export bans Malaysia significantly reduced its log exports during the 
period 1993-95 (rITO, 1996; 21). This sent Far East buyers to West and Central Africa in the search 
for logs to offset the declining supply from Malaysia. The effect in Ghana was that log export contracts 
on substantial volumes were offered for species that previously accounted for a very small fraction of 
the annual harvest and log export. In particular, logs of Ceiba and K yenkyen became prominent export 
commodities over-night. 
This sudden change in overseas log demand led timber companies in Ghana to focus on off-reserve 
timber resources, presumably because the harvest control on-reserves was more strict. Yet, the fall in 
on-reserve timber extraction during the period 1991-93 is probably not only a result of strictly enforced 
harvest controls. Rather it is the result of many timber companies allocating resources to participate in 
the log export driven off-reserve harvest galore. During the period 1991-94 many companies simply 
closed their sawmilling activities and devoted their entire capacity to the export oflogs (see sections 
10.2 and 11.1.3 for a more detailed discussion of forest fee policies and the resulting financial incentives 
to export logs rather than processed wood). In 1995 Ghana issued a ban on all log exports to control 
the situation. 
7.4.1 Total Recorded Extraction by Species Groups 
Detailed data for timber extraction by species is only available for 1986 onwards. Figure 7.3 depicts 
the recorded total timber extraction during the period 1986-96 for species within star groups (cf. section 
6.3.1).

Figure 7.3 clearly shows that the mainstay of timber extraction is made up by a substantial and fairly 
stable extraction of Scarlet Star species. This group comprises 15 species (in terms of trade names, 18 
in terms of botanical species). Log export was banned for nine ofthese species in 1979. In 1988 and 
1993, respectively, log exports were banned for two more species and in 1995 log export was banned 
for the remaining four (see appendix 5). Accordingly, the timber industry in Ghana must have well 
established markets for processed Scarlet Star species, which makes it highly dependent on a constant 
supply of these species. This explains the constant and high rate of extraction compared with Red and 
Pink Star species. The falling trend since 1992 in Scarlet Star extraction is, therefore, likely to reflect 
a decreasing (off-reserve) availability of these species combined with a possible stricter on-reserve 
harvest control. Appendix 5 gives the details behind figure 7.3. 
This dependency of the timber industry on Scarlet Star species is a crucial forest policy problem in 
Ghana today. The reason being that AAC estimates show that the current extraction level of Scarlet Star 
species cannot be sustained in the future (see section 7.5). 
The peak in extraction of Red Star species during 1993-94 reflects the sharp rise in extraction of Ceiba 
and Kyenkyen for log exports. The 1993-94 peak in extraction of Pink Star species is mainly due to 
rise and fall in extraction of Of ram and Otie, which like Ceiba and Kyenkyen were part of the log export 
boom to meet the demand from Far East buyers. The extraction level of Red and Pink Star species in 
1995-96 does mark an increase in domestic processing of these species compared to the level in 1992, 
which may be the first sign that the timber industry has initiated a process of diversifying its log demand 
in order to reduce the heavy dependence on Scarlet Star species. However, more radical changes in 
the species composition are required for extraction to match the AAC (see section 7.5). 
62

--- Page 87 ---

7.5 On-Reserve Harvest and AAC 
As mentioned above it is only the 1996 LMC totals which provide a reasonably reliable picture of the

only and (ii) fine- and large-grained protection. Appendix 4 details the recorded extraction and AAC

The general message from table 7.1 is clear. Scarlet Star species are over-harvested, whereas the 
harvest of Red and Pink Star species could be increased substantially. Yet, important details are 
revealed by a closer analysis of the star groups. 
7.5.1 Scarlet Star Species 
According to the estimated AACs, ten out of the 15 Scarlet Star species are seriously over-harvested. 
Moreover, these ten species accounted for 96% of the recorded Scarlet Star species extraction in 
1996. In this connection, it should be remembered that recorded extraction under-estimates actual 
harvest by at least 10%. Furthermore, table 7.1 does not account for the approximately 60,000 m3 
recorded as coming from unidentified sources in 1996. 
Accordingly, the current on-reserve harvest of Scarlet Star species is almost certainly unsustainable. 
Measured against the fine- and large-grained protection scenarios, the annual harvest of the ten 
commercially most desired species should be reduced by at least 75,000 m3compared with the 1996 
extraction. The harvest ofless desirable Scarlet Star species could only be increased by approximately 
15,000 m3 annually (considering that actual harvest exceeds the recorded extraction). 
7.5.2 Red Star Species 
Red Star species are generally extracted at rates below the estimated AACs - although unrecorded 
logging waste and under-reporting of actual log extraction by the LMC system might paint a somewhat 
rosy picture of the realities. However, not all Red Star species could be harvested at rates exceeding 
63

--- Page 88 ---

the 1996 level. Candollei was recorded to be extracted at 247% of the estimated AAC, while Guarea 
and Niangon are reported to be extracted at levels close to their AACs. In fact, these species should 
probably belong to the Scarlet rather than the Red Star category (see section 7.5.4). 
Much of the under-utilised harvest potential within the Red Stars is concentrated on only three species, 
Ceiba, Kyenkyen and Dahoma. The annual harvest of Ceiba could possibly be increased by some 
60,000 m3, while the annual harvest of Kyenkyen and Dahoma might be increased by approximately 
25,000 m3 and 8,000 m3, respectively. The remaining annual harvest potential of approximately 100,000 
m3 is divided fairly evenly between ten different species. 
7.5.3 Pink Star Species 
Pink Star species represent a substantial but largely un-utilised harvest potential of 480,000 to 530,000 
m3 per year depending on which level of forest protection the AAC is related to. Yet, the industrial 
demand for Pink Star species has always been very limited (with the exception of Of ram and Otie during 
the log export boom in 1993-94). 
Counted by trade names, 15 of the 32 Pink Star species were not subject to any harvest in 1996. 
Danta, Otie, Sterculia, Of ram and Avodire are the only Pink Star species which were recorded to be 
extracted at rates above 2,000 m3 in 1996. These five species accounted for approximately 27,000 m3 
or 82 % of the total recorded Pink Star extraction. The un-used harvest potential within the group of 
Pink Star species is concentrated on Celtis, Denya, Ananta, Danta, Asoma, Esia, Of ram and Avodire. 
These eight species represent an AAC of approximately 330,000 m3 (depending on the level of forest 
protection). However, in 1996 the recorded extraction of these species was only approximately 29,000 
m3, which illustrates the currently low market value of this abundant resource. 
7.5.4 The Star Group System and Related AACs Revisited 
It will be recalled that the star group classification used by PIMP is based on the information available 
in 1994, which was (i) recorded extraction from on- and off-reserves assessed against (ii) on-reserve 
inventory data only (PIMP, 1994b). Accordingly, the then developed star group classification rested 
on fairly rough assumptions about the connection between total extraction and the resource base on-
and off-reserves. 
Today the scientifically well founded on-reserve AACs may, together with on-reserve extraction data, 
be considered a better foundation for star classification of tree species. This is specifically ,so because 
the reduced yield formula is only applied on-reserves with the specific objective to reduce felling of 
species threatened by commercial harvest, ie. the Scarlet Star species. 
On the basis of species specific on-reserve AACs and the recorded on-reserve extraction by species 
it may, since recorded extraction notoriously under-estimates actual harvest, be relevantto define star 
categories as follows: 
• 
Scarlet stars: 
• 
Red stars: 
• 
Pink stars: 
More than 90% exploitation. 
Between 50 and 90% exploitation. 
Less than 50% exploitation. 
64

--- Page 89 ---

These criteria would shift a few species from the Scarlet and Red Star groups to the group of Pink Star 
species and some species would go from the Scarlet to the Red Star group and vice versa (see 
appendix 4). Yet, these changes can only be taken as indicative because the AAC calculations are 
based on the reduced yield formula for Scarlet Star species according to the star classification of 1994. 
Available data does not permit re-calculation of AACs and re-allocation of species into star groups on 
the basis of recorded 1996 on-reserve extraction rates. The result would have been a refinement ofthe 
current AAC estimates by species. AACs for current Scarlet Star species which rightfully belong in the 
Red or Pink Star group would be increased, and vice versa for Red Star species which belong in the 
Scarlet Star group (no Pink Star species would change group). 
However, the star group system and AACs should obviously not rely on a single year's extraction of 
timber on-reserves, but rather on average figures for, ego the latest three years. Furthermore, the Scarlet 
Star group AAC is not likely to change much since the currently ten over-harvested species will 
probably remain Scarlet Stars under the above proposed criteria. Today these species account for 87% 
of the Scarlet Star group AAC. 
Since star groups are used to calculate on-reserve yields, the system should allow for species to change 
star groups such that harvest controls may be timely applied when market forces increase or decrease 
the harvest pressure on individual species. An example might be Candollei which is currently placed in 
the Red Star group while the 1996 extraction level suggests that the species is seriously over-harvested. 
7.5.5 Policy Implications of the Extraction Rates and AACs 
It may be concluded that the total on-reserve harvest is well within the limits on the total on-reserve 
AAC, even when the uncertainties of using recorded extraction as a proxy for actual harvest and log 
extraction from unspecified sources are taken into consideration. However, according to the AAC 
estimates approximately 70,000 m3 or 25% of the 1996 recorded timber extraction represents a 
notorious over-exploitation, predominantly of Scarlet Star species. 
This is where the crux of the matter lies. In relation to the resource base, market forces create an 
excess demand for certain species (in general the commercially most valuable ones) which will lead to 
their economic (not botanical) extinction if nothing is done about it. The policy challenge is to: 
• 
Protect over-harvested species against the demand pressure through control-of-supply measures; 
• 
divert demand away from over-utilised and towards under-utilised species. 
It is fortunate for Ghana that the latter option actually exists. The substantial resource within forest 
reserves of under-utilised Red and Pink Star species forms a security of raw material supply and a 
marketing challenge for the timber industry. The over-exploitation of certain species within forest 
reserves forms a management challenge to the Forestry Department, which should set things straight in 
the long-term interest of the nation (including the timber industry). 
7.6 Off-Reserve Timber Extraction and the Resource Base 
As for on-reserves, the most reliable off-reserve extraction figures are those of 1996. Tables 7.2 and 
7.3 summarise the off-reserve recorded extraction for star groups together with the off-reserve inventory 
results of standing trees above felling limit and above 70 cm DBH (see appendix 5 for details).

is similar to table 7.2 except that it assumes a general off-reserve felling limit of70 cm DBH, which is 
probably fairly realistic at least for the Scarlet and Red Star species. 
7.6.1 Off-Reserve Extraction of Scarlet Star Species 
Scarlet Star species are clearly under serious threat of economic extinction. This group of species 
represents a significantly lower growing stock compared to Red and Pink Star groups, while the 
recorded off-reserve extraction of Scarlet Star species accounted for 63% of the total 1996 recorded 
off-reserve extraction. 
Inventory data does not yet allow for an evaluation of the off-reserve extraction by species, but judging 
from the extraction figures certain species are under a more immediate threat than others. Together 
Wawa, Odum, Mahogany, Kyire and Asanfina accounted for 464,082 m3 or 90% of the recorded 
extraction of Scarlet Star species in 1996 (cf. appendix 5). To some extent this obviously reflects the 
availability of species off-reserves, but market demand certainly also plays an important role in the 
selection of trees for harvest off-reserves. 
Considering that the recorded extraction under-states actual harvest by at least ten per cent and that the 
timber industry depends heavily on Scarlet Star species, tables 7.2 and 7.3 predict major changes in the 
timber industry and/or major pressure on the forest reserves within the near future. Despite the 
uncertainty about estimated lifetimes for Scarlet Star species the tables document that this resource is 
being logged out. 
For some species this could easily happen within the next 15 years and for other species the time span 
might be 50 years. The point is that it is happening, so now is the time to take action at the policy and 
management level if Scarlet Star species both on- and off-reserves are to be protected from an even 
heavier over-exploitation than is already the case. 
7.6.2 Off-Reserve Extraction of Red Star Species 
The 1996 recorded off-reserve extraction of Red Star species accounted for 240,633 m3 or 29% of the 
total 1996 recorded off-reserve extraction. Even though this is a substantial volume and at least ten per 
cent below actual harvest, Red Star off-reserve resources should allow for harvest at the present level 
in at least another 100 years (cf. tables 7.2 and 7.3). 
The extraction of Ceiba stands out among the Red Star species. In 1996 it was recorded to be 
approximately 136,000 m3 or 57% of the total recorded Red Star extraction which is substantial. 
However, Ceiba is known to be the most abundant tree species in Ghana, and it is not likely that detailed 
off-reserve inventory data, when it becomes available, will forecast a soon economic extinction ofCeiba 
even if the current extraction level is continued. 
7.6.3 Off-Reserve Extraction of Pink Star Species 
Pink Star species accounted for approximately 65,000 m3 or only 8% ofthe total recorded 1996 off-
reserve extraction. Of the 32 Pink Star species (by trade name) only Sterculia, Of ram, Danta, Esia, Yaya 
and Otie were in 1996 reported to be extracted at rates above 1,000 m3. Together these species 
accounted for approximately 62,000 m3 or 96% of the 1996 recorded Pink Star extraction, while the 
other Pink Star species were either left untouched or extracted at very low rates (cf. appendix 5). 
67

--- Page 92 ---

It is clear that the current off-reserve extraction of Pink Star species reflects market demand rather than 
resource availability. As the case is on-reserves, the stock of Pink Star species represents a resource 
which the industry might tum to when the commercially more attractive Scarlet and Red Star species 
become less available, either because the resource is depleted or because new management procedures 
efficiently control supply. 
7.7 Policy Implications of Extraction and Resource Data 
For the first time since the mid 1980s when the timber harvest in Ghana began to increase as a result of 
the Export Rehabilitation Programme it is, as shown above, now possible to assess the annual timber 
harvest against the resource base both on- and off-reserves. Data is, as discussed in this and earlier 
chapters, not perfect but by far good enough to draw important conclusions at the policy level. 
The timber industry's preference for Scarlet and Red Star species has for many species within these two 
groups led to severe over-exploitation on-reserves. Moreover, inventory data and recorded extraction 
have revealed that off-reserves Scarlet Star species will, according to the most optimistic scenario (table 
7.3), be logged out within the next 39 years, if the 1996 extraction rate continues. 
Accordingly, if the Forestry Department cannot bring the timber exploitation on-reserves under control 
within the coming 15-30 years, this could easily be the end of many Scarlet and Red Star species as far 
as raw material for the timber industry is concerned. Botanically they would probably survive as single 
trees which are too rare and of too poor stem form to be pursued for industrial utilisation. 
What the environmental consequences will be of an on-reserve species composition with fewer Scarlet 
and Red Star species than today is an open question. However, a reduced genetic diversity within and 
between species in a natural forest is hardly justifiable unless it can somehow be proved socially 
desirable. Such justification is absent in Ghana and it is certainly not official policy to mine the 
commerciall y most valuable timber species for short -term financial profits and hand over whatever is left 
to future generations. 
The substantial resource of Pink Star species both on- and off-reserves provides some hope for a future 
where the present extraction level may be maintained, but based on a wider selection of species than 
today. However, this safety net function of the Pink Star species will only materialise if there is a market 
for these species. The current and historical harvesting pattern shows that this is not yet the case. 
It would at this point be naive to expect that the timber industry is voluntarily going to reduce harvest of 
the currently over-exploited Scarlet and Red Star species and focus its resources on developing markets 
for abundant Pink Star specie~. A few sawmills may have the strategic wisdom to do so (we have come 
across one company which did). Yet, the majority of timber industries naturally pursue short-term profit 
maximisation when this is an option, ie. when they have access to Scarlet and Red Star species. 
68

--- Page 93 ---

Particularly off-reserves and to a limited extent on-reserves, the economic system appears to be one of 
open access which is leading to the tragedy of the certain Scarlet and Red Star timber species. The only 
remedy to reverse this trend is policy interventions that combine: 
• 
Economic incentives which favour conservation of timber trees for future use (demand-side 
measures); 
• 
restrictions on felling activities (supply-side measures). 
The latter point is necessary to ensure that long-term social interests of maintaining a diverse on- and off-
reserve timber resource get the upper hand when these are in conflict with short -term financial interests 
of private companies. The options for such policy interventions and results of interventions already 
applied will be analysed in the following chapters. 
69

--- Page 94 ---

8 Timber Exports and the Wood Industry 
The export of wood products and the timber industry as such are prominent in Ghana's economy. 
Estimates suggest that the timber industry accounts for 4-7% of GDP, and that wood exports represent 
some 18% of exports (Overseas Consultancy Service, 1995; 16-17). 
In chapter 3 it was mentioned that part of the 1984 IMFIW orld Bank Economic Recovery Programme 
for Ghana included an Export Rehabilitation Programme including timber and wood products. This 
chapter examines the apparent success of this programme, which until 1994 has resulted in: 
• 
Increasing volumes of wood exports; 
• 
increasing foreign exchange earnings; 
• 
increasing value per cubic metre of wood exports. 
From 1994 to 1996 the export revenue and volume have gone down a bit, but the general trend during 
the period 1986-96 has been increasing export revenue and an increasing volume of pro,cessed wood 
exports. However, the above points do not complete the picture. Clearly, one negative and crucial 
development in Ghana is the fact that the country's timber resource has been and still is seriously over-
exploited. Harvest for export plays a significant role in this respect. Furthermore, the success of further 
processing before export depends, from a resource conservation and foreign exchange earning point 
of view, entirely on how efficient the further processing is. 
8.1 Development in Wood Exports by Volume 
As figure 8.1 shows, the export volume of products has rapidly increased from approximately 90,000 
m3 in 1982 to approximately 960,000 m3 in 1994, after which the export volume dropped to 
approximately 360,000 m3 in 1996. The very high volumes in 1993 and 1994 mainly reflect a sudden 
"log export boom" of especially Kyenkyen, Ceiba, Yaya, Otie and Of ram. 
Until 1994, logs were a major export article closely followed by lumber, which has shown a steadily 
increasing trend since 1982. The sharp fall in log exports after 1994 is due to the complete log export 
ban that was, in effect, implemented administratively during 1995 as the Government stopped issuing 
log export licences. 
Sliced veneer and the group "All Others" have also shown a steadily increasing trend although at modest 
volumes. Appendix 7 provides the details behind figure 8.1. 
70

8.2 Apparent Domestic Consumption 
In addition to export demands the timber harvest must also satisfy domestic needs. No direct data on 
the domestic timber consumption is available, but a rough estimate may be provided by subtracting the 
export harvest from the recorded extraction (stock change is, in other words, ignored). The export 
harvest is here defined as the volume of wood exports converted into roundwood equivalents (RWE). 
This is done by assuming that the recovery rates27 for lumber, sliced veneer and "all others" are 40%, 
30% and 35% respectively (see section 8.3). In estimating the domestic wood consumption it is further 
assumed that 55% of the residues from the export production are re-processed for domestic use other 
than fuel wood and charcoal. For the period 1986-96 figure 8.2 shows the result of this exercise 
(appendix 7 provides the data in detail).

Figure 8.2 
Estimated use of the recorded timber extraction (RWE) 1986-96 
Sources: FPIB Export Pennits (1986-96) 
FPIB LMC Totals (1986-96) 
The falling trend in apparent domestic wood consumption from 1993 to 1996 reflects the export harvest 
in these years making up between 74 and 94% of the recorded extraction. Yet, it seems unlikely that 
the domestic consumption should have fallen to the extent indicated by the graph. A more logical 
explanation would be that a combination of illegal logging and a depletion of stocks of processed wood 
took place during the period 1994-96. Certainly illegal logging was a serious problem during this period, 
where a working group to recommend on control of illegal timber harvesting outside forest reserves had 
to be formed by the Ministry of Forests (FD, 1994a). During the log export boom in 1993-94, exports 
of processed wood continued to increase (see figure 8.1). This suggests that the wood industry, while 
stepping up log exports tremendously, may have managed to increase the export of processed wood 
by depleting stocks. In conclusion, the size of the domestic wood consumption is probably at least 
700,000 m3 in RWE per year, excluding charcoal and fuel wood. 
A major domestic market for timber is the construction industry which is expected to continue to 
expand. The two most important "driving forces" of domestic wood demand are likely to be population 
and economic growth. Only an increase in the real price of wood would tend to depress the demand. 
Based on a population growth of 3.3% and real economic growth of 3.8% in 1994, Overseas 
Consultancy Service (1995; 14) roughly estimates thatthe domestic wood consumption could grow 
3.5% per annum. This would amount to a domestic consumption of nearly one million m3 
by 2005. 
Of course, more sophisticated analyses and less indirect calculations than the above could be made. 
Yet, it seems reasonable to assume that the domestic wood consumption in RWE is going to be in the 
region of one million m3 within a foreseeable future. Since the domestic demand/need is likely to be 
satisfied legally or illegally and the AAC from forest reserves is only between 0.8 and 0.9 million m3, 
72

--- Page 97 ---

it is clear that the present level of wood exports (almost one million m3 RWE in 1996) cannot continue 
without depleting the resource base unless a significant increase in sustained timber yield from the high 
forest zone is accomplished. This could be through plantations (off-reserves or on areas inside reserves 
with no forest) and/or a kind of sustained yield timber production off-reserves (on farmlands). In fact, 
this appears the most important forest policy problem in Ghana today. The financial and legal 
perspectives for adding to the on-reserve AAC through plantations and off-reserve sustained yield 
timber production are further assessed in chapters 12 and 13. 
8.3 Export as the Dominant Driving Force for Timber Harvest 
Apart from estimating the apparent domestic wood consumption, figure 8.2 also indicates that, during 
the period 1986-96, the export harvest made up 60-94% of the total recorded timber extraction. 
Harvest for export has, in other words, been the dominant driving force behind timber exploitation in 
Ghana. 
Naturally, the estimated percentage of harvest for export out of the total recorded extraction depends 
entirely on the assumed recovery rates and the reliability of data. Yet, the recovery rates used here are 
probably on the high side. For example, General Woods (1992; WD no. 4; I-IS), in an extensive 
survey covering about 7S% of Ghana's timber industries, finds that the recovery rates for export lumber 
is between 20% and 40%. For sliced veneer the technically feasible recovery rate on modem mills is 
3S-40%, so the estimated 30% recovery may even be generous given the generally low technical 
standard of Ghanian veneer mills and keeping in mind that the calculations are based on export quality 
products only. The recovery rate of3S% used for the group "All others" is also considered generous 
as this group apart from rotary veneer and boles consists mainly of further processed lumber. 
Accordingly, the export harvest used here is more like to be an under- than an over-estimate. 
The reliability of data on timber extraction has been discussed in section 7.1.1, where it is concluded 
that official timber extraction data most probably under-estimates the actual harvest by at least 10%. 
There are no indications of significant discrepancies between the official data on export volumes and 
actual export volumes during the period 1986-96. 
In addition to the above technically based estimations of how important the export harvest is in relation 
to the total timber harvest, it has clearly been the objective of the Export Rehabilitation Programme, and 
subsequently the World Bank supported Forest Resource Management Project, to boost the timber 
industry and foreign exchange earnings from wood exports (FRMP, 1988; 49). It is, therefore, no 
surprise that the export harvest accounts for a significant if not the major part of the recorded timber 
extraction. 
In 1988, the World Bank naturally emphasised that the AAC would ultimately setthe limits for wood 
export earnings and new jobs created in the wood industry. Yet, at that time the Bank assumed that the 
AAC was not likely to be less than the, at that time available, estimate of 1.1 million m3• In fact, the 
Bank seemed to anticipate that the AAC would prove to be considerably higher, in the range of2.0 to 
2.7 million m3 (FRMP, 1988; 49-S1). This has proved to be completely wrong as the AAC from forest 
reserves is subsequently estimated with fairly good accuracy to be between 0.8 and 0.9 million 
m3• 
73

--- Page 98 ---

AAC, which in itself should be a cause of concern. However, the species composition oftheexport

estimate of the relative importance of species harvested for wood exports may be calculated by 
summing up the export harvest of logs, lumber and sliced veneer for each species28• This is shown for 
star groups in figure 8.3, which also includes the total recorded extraction from on- and off-
reserves.

It is seen from figure 8.3 that, during the period 1986-96, the export harvest of Scarlet Star species has

a span of 100,000 to 400,000 m3 up to the recorded extraction. The average export harvest of Red and 
Pink Star species during this period was approximately 140,000 m3 and 60,000 m3 RWE, respectively. 
However, as figure 8.3 indicates, the average volumes for Red and Pink Star species are influenced by 
the log export boom in 1993-94 where the export harvest of Red Star species went up to some 
400,000 m3 RWE. For Pink Star species the export harvest was approximately 100,000 m3 and 
220,000 m3 RWE in 1993 and 1994, respectively (see appendix 7 for details).

8.4.1 The Unsustainable Export Harvest 
Since forest reserves so far are the only (de jure if not de facto) secured source of raw logs it is of 
interest to compare the export harvest with the estimated on-reserve AACs. The result of doing this is, 
in summary, shown in table 8.1 (details are found in appendix 7).

As table 8.1 shows, Scarlet Star species accounted the majority (74%) of the average export harvest 
during 1986-96. Furthermore, the 1986-96 average export harvest of Scarlet Star species is four to 
five times the on-reserve AAC for this species group, depending on which level of forest protection the 
AACs represent. A more detailed look at the Scarlet Star species shows that all species except Albizia, 
Daniella and Hyedua are harvested for export in volumes that are four to 14 times their on-reserve 
AACs (see appendix 7). 
The average 1986-96 export harvest of Red Star Species represents between 61 % and 68% of the on-
reserve AAC for this group as a whole. Yet, three species, Afzelia, Candollei, and Emire have been 
harvested for export in volumes that are two to five times their on-reserve AACs. Pink Star species 
were all harvested for export at rates that are significantly below their on-reserve AACs. 
Bearing in mind that the above export harvest volumes for star groups are minimum estimates, it is clear 
that the species composition of the wood exports is unfortunate, to put it mildly. The 1996 on-reserve 
recorded extraction of Scarlet Star species was approximately 180,000 m3 (see section 7.5), which is 
1.4 to 1.5 times the AAC. The 1996 off-reserve recorded extraction of Scarlet Star species was around 
520,000 m3 which, if exploitation continues at the 1996 level, will deplete off-reserve Scarlet Star timber 
resources over the next 15 to 40 years (cf. tables 7.2 and 7.3). Accordingly, the timber industry is and 
has at least for the last ten years been mining the Scarlet Star timber resource for export. 
Judging from the 1996 extraction records it may be assumed that the depletion of Scarlet Star species 
has been most intensive off-reserves. The question is what happens when (if) Scarlet Star species are 
75

--- Page 100 ---

logged out off-reserves while the export demand pull for these species probably remains at a level of 
some 600,000 m3 RWE annually. Theoretically and in a perfect world the harvest should, as assumed 
by the World Bank, be reduced to the AAC. Yet, the value of wood exports suggests that vested 
financial interests in the current level and species composition of wood exports are going to make a 
reduction of the Scarlet Star species export rather difficult. 
8.5 The Value of Wood Exports 
The FOB value of Ghana's total wood exports has, measured in 1996 prices, increased steadily from 
approximately DM 145 million in 1986 to approximately DM 220 million in 1996 (see figure 8.4). The 
peak ofDM 365 million in 1994 is mainly due to the log export boom which culminated that year (cf. 
the curve for logs and poles) and fell to zero in 1996 where no logs were exported. The export value 
oflumber, sliced veneer and the group "All Others" has, as figure 8.4 shows, followed a fairly steadily 
increasing trend over the period.

1996 
In terms of species composition it is no surprise that the Scarlet Stars dominate like they do in terms of 
volume. For the export oflogs, lumber and sliced veneer only it is seen from figure 8.5 that, measured 
in 1996 prices, Scarlet Star species have accounted for around DM 150-175 million annually. Red and 
Pink Star species have fluctuated around DM 25 and 10 million, respectively if the log export boom in 
1993-94 is excluded. 
If 1996 is taken as a proxy for the composition of wood exports in the near future, ie. with no export 
oflogs and no radical market changes for other products, it is seen from figures 8.4 and 8.5 that the 
Scarlet Star species will account for more than 70% of the total wood export revenue. Since the export 
markets are not likely to change taste in terms of demand for certain species as long its these are 
available, and since Ghana is a relatively small world market supplier, the major export value and 
notorious over-exploitation of Scarlet Star species clearly poses a serious forest policy problem. 
Essentially, the problem is that a significant number of jobs in the wood industry and a significant fraction 
of the much needed foreign exchange earnings from wood exports are achieved through a mining of the 
Scarlet Star timber resource. The irony is that the obvious imbalance between the timber resource and 
the timber export is created through an IMF and World Bank supported deliberate policy to boost the 
wood export. 
8.5.1 
Unit Values of Wood Exports 
In figure 8.6 the development in FOB export unit value is, in 1996 prices, depicted for the period 1986-
96. If one looks at the total FOB export value per m3 of all products taken together, this has gone up 
impressively from around DM 420 per m3 to DM 600 per m3 during the period. This figure's temporary 
fall to approximately DM 350 per m3 in 1993-94 was a result of the log export boom which was 
concentrated on relative low value Red and Pink Star species. 
77

--- Page 102 ---

Another way to analyse the development in export unit value is to base the calculation on the export 
harvest. When this is done the trend becomes negative. Measured in 1996 prices, the value has gone 
down from DM 270 perm3 RWE in 1986 to DM 230per m3 RWE in 1996 (the detailed data behind 
figure 8.6 is found in appendix 8). This strongly indicates that the apparent successful development of 
Ghana's wood export may not be so successful after all.

A closer look at the FOB weighted average unit export value for commodity groups is provided in figure 
8.7. The graphs in figures 8.6 and 8.7 show that the overall weighted average unit value of wood 
exports closely follows that of lumber. The weighted average export unit value of logs has fallen 
gradually which is also the case forthe group "All others". Sliced veneer has as the only commodity 
group shown a remarkable increase in weighted average unit export value. 
As pointed out earlier, lumber is the dominant wood export commodity both in terms of volume and 
value. A detailed picture of the lumber export value per RWE is provided in figure 8.8. This shows that, 
during the period 1986-96, the weighed average FOB export value in 1996 prices for all species taken 
together (which closely follows the Scarlet Star group) has fluctuated around DM 250 per m3 RWE with 
a slightly falling trend. The weighted average unit value for Red Star lumber is higher than Scarlet Star 
lumber because the relative low value species, Wawa in the Scarlet Star group is exported in much 
higher volumes than any other species.

In conclusion, the unit value per RWE for Ghana's wood export has stayed at a low level and even 
shown a somewhat falling trend during the period 1986-96. Only sliced veneer clearly stands out as an 
apparent positive deviation from the general rule, but this commodity is not exported in big volumes. 
Accordingly, the increasing export of processed wood appears to have had little positive effect on the 
foreign exchange earnings per m3 timber felled for export. In fact, the opposite seems to have been the 
case. This can hardly be termed a successful development. In particular, this is so because the lion's 
share ofthe export revenue has been and still is based on an indisputable over-exploitation of Scarlet 
Star species. 
8.6 The Hidden Costs of Value Added Wood Exports 
Section 8.5 has demonstrated that the increasing overall unit price of wood exports, when calculated 
on the basis of product volumes, provides a false image of the economic realities. When the timber 
resource base and the degree to which it is utilised is considered, the gloss of the wood export is 
wearing off. 
To make things worse, Ghana appears to be foregoing substantial export revenue earnings by promoting 
the export of so-called value added products. This may be illustrated by comparing actual export 
revenues with a hypothetical log export of the timber that has gone into manufacturing the exported 
products (see table 8.2).

The weighted average log export values shown in table 8.2 are calculated on the basis of actual log 
export prices in 1993-94 and 1993 FOB log prices in other West African countries (used for species 
that were not exported in log fonn from Ghana during 1993-94). Accordingly, the table compares 1996 
lumber and sliced veneer prices with 1993-94 log prices. Nevertheless, the results are considered valid, 
as the calculated export revenue foregone can hardly be explained by exceptionally high log prices in 
1993-94 compared with what might have been the 1996 level. Furthermore, the weighted average 
"break even" recovery rates (the recovery rates at which the export revenue per RWE of products 
81

--- Page 106 ---

would equal that oflogs) for "All Species" are only slightly below what is technically feasible within the 
three product categories. Assuming the market is efficient, this supports that the price relations between 
logs and products in the table are realistic29 . 
It is seen from the table that, in 1996, the export revenue foregone by exporting sliced veneer, air-dried 
and kiln-dried lumber alone was in the area ofDM 40 million or 25% of the actual export revenue. In 
addition, it is clear that the export of air-dried lumber and Scarlet Star species in general (the prime 
export product and prime species group) account for the majority of export revenue foregone. The 
better performance of kiln-dried lumber compared to air-dried lumber is perhaps slightly overstated, 
as a 40% recovery rate is used for both groups (some losses due to defects developing under drying 
are bound to occur). 
An interesting point is that the further processing of Red and Pink Star species to kiln-dried lumber and 
sliced veneer generally has a positive effect on the export revenue in comparison with a hypothetical log 
export. Considering the resource base this leaves some hope as to maintaining current export revenues 
through further processing. Unfortunately, the volumes in these categories are relatively small. 
8.7 Structure of the Wood Industry 
Before concluding on the export driven timber harvest a brief overview of Ghana's wood industry is 
relevant. Four issues in this relation have a direct bearing on the overall forest policy problems of 
managing the high forest resource. These are: 
• 
Installed wood processing capacity; 
• 
employment in the wood industry; 
• 
general structure and ownership; 
• 
size of the wood industry in the national economy. 
8.7.1 
Wood Processing Capacity 
The recorded extraction in 1996 (after the complete log export ban) of approximately 1.2 million m3 
shows thatthe processing capacity ofthe wood industry is at least at this level. However, a survey by 
General Woods (1992, WD. no 4) estimated the processing capacity of the wood industry to be around 
2 million m3 annUally. On the basis of a new survey and in the light of the 1995 complete log export ban, 
Overseas Consultancy Service (1995; 21) estimated that the processing capacity in the wood industry 
is growing and could reach a capacity of over 3 million m3 annually in 1997. 
29 
The FOB log prices must reflect overseas buyers' willingness to pay 
for logs, which in turn is a function of their ability to make profits from 
converting them into products (here lumber and sliced veneer). Since 
these products are sold on the same markets as similar products from 
Ghana, a calculation of "break even" recovery rates on the basis of 
FOB log and product prices in GhanalWest Africa should, if the 
market is efficient, be slightly lower than the average recovery rates 
realised by overseas buyers since the unit transport costs of products 
from Ghana should be lower than the unit transport costs of products 
that are based on log imports. 
82

--- Page 107 ---

Although the above estimates on processing capacity may be debatable it is unlikely that they are totally 
off the mark. Accordingly, there can be no doubt that the installed capacity in the wood industry is 
around twice the on-reserve AAC. Clearly this is an unsustainable situation. 
8.7.2 
Employment in the Wood Industry 
Even though the wood industry appears to be running at approximately 50% of its processing capacity 
it employs a significant number of people. The forestry sector employs about 75,000 people, and 
provides a livelihood of an estimated 2 million people (TEDB, 1995; 1). Although the exact number of 
people whose livelihood depends on the timber industry is inherently difficult to estimate, there can be 
little doubt that the wood industry is nationally very important both in terms of direct employment and 
supporting household economies. 
8.7.3 
General Structure and Ownership ofthe Wood Industry 
Today the wood industry is totally dominated by private enterprises as the former big wholly or partly 
state owned companies have been either closed or privatised. According to Overseas Consultancy 
Service (1995; 15), the structure of the industry may be summarised as shown in table 8.3.

to the total recorded extraction would suggest, it is further seen that, with the exception of producers 
offumiture parts, a significant fraction of the wood industries are also exporters. Yet, behind the quite 
high number of wood exporters lies the fact that only a few companies dominate this market. For 1996 
this is illustrated in table 8.5

The message from table 8.5 is quite clear. Very few companies almost entirely dominate Ghana's wood 
export. Actually, the table slightly understates this situation because some companies are among the 
dominant exporters of more than one commodity. For example, the private companies, Ghana 
Primewood and John Bitar & Co. LtD. are dominant in both air-dried lumber and sliced veneer. 
8.8 Conclusion on Wood Exports 
The bottom line currently seems to be that not only does the export of Scarlet Star species deplete this 
group of species at an alarming speed. The export of these species also represents a considerable 
amount of foreign exchange revenue foregone because log exports are not allowed. Ironically, the 
export of Pink and to some degree Red Star species could, from a resource point of view , be increased 
significantly and, judging from table 8.2, these species would, as opposed to the Scarlet Stars, even 
represent a net gain in foreign exchange revenue when exported as products rather than logs. 
Accordingly, the composition of wood exports is not desirable from a long-term national economic point 
of view, but obviously it must be profitable for the wood exporting industries of which very few 
dominate the market. Otherwise the export of wood products would not have continued the way it has 
during the 10 year period from 1986 to 1996. Furthermore, there is a substantial overcapacity in the 
wood industry. 
There can only be one explanation to this. Lacking incentives forthe wood exporting sector to act in 
the long-term interest of the nation and adjust to the resource base. In other words, market failures. The 
courses and possible solutions should, therefore, be sought in the domestic market and politically defined 
regulative mechanisms associated with commercial timber harvest. 
84

--- Page 109 ---

9 Forest Fees and Taxes 
The previous chapters have reviewed the "technical evidence" sustaining that the timber resource and 
certain forest areas in Southern Ghana are being degraded, and that the speed of degradation forms an 
immediate threat of economic extinction for some species. Furthermore, the substantial off-reserve 
timber harvest and the industry's export driven preference for especially Scarlet and to some degree 
Red Star species is a ticking bomb under the potentials for implementing sustainable management or at 
least sustained timber yield management of forest reserves. 
In several places it has been indicated that financial incentives must form a major underlying reason for 
the over-exploitation. Since most ofthe species which are in highest demand are over-exploited and 
since the processing capacity of the wood industry appears to be under-utilised, there should be a 
"seller's market" for logs. In practice, this is not the case because the market does not operate properly. 
The price of standing timber (royalties and other forest fees) and who gets access to harvesttimber is 
determined administratively rather that through market forces. 
This chapter reviews the different forest fees and taxes, induding their legislative background and lays 
the foundation for analysing the financial incentives to harvest and process different timber species. 
Forest fees and taxes may in this respect be considered "demand-side" measures intended to regulate 
the timber harvest. 
Generation of government revenue to finance forest management, induding timber exploitation, is 
another (perhaps the most) important reason for forest fees and taxes. In addition, the legislation also 
covers aspects of rights and responsibilities, of which some of the more important ones are mentioned. 
These issues are further discussed in chapters 11 and 12. 
9.1 Overview of Forest Fees and Related Laws 
There are and have been a number of official forest fees in Ghana. A list of the different fees, the fee 
level (where standardised) and the institution responsible for their collection is presented in 
table 9.l.

9.1.1 Timber Royalties and Stumpage Fees 
Royalties on trees felled within forest reserves have since 1976, when the system was introduced, been 
collected by the Forestry Department (Forest Fees Regulations, 1976). 
The Lands Commission was responsible for collecting off-reserve timber royalties until 1994 where the 
Ministry of Lands and Forestry transferred this responsibility to the Forestry Department, in an attempt 
to streamline the procedures for royalty collection and to improve the collectionrate (MLF, 
1994). 
86

--- Page 111 ---

This change in collection of timber royalties off-reserves had been recommended for a long time by 
various consultants. As part of the Forest Resource Management Project it was even agreed between 
the Government of Ghana and the World Bank that the Forestry Department should have taken 
responsibility for the off-reserve collection of forest fees by October 1, 1990 (FRMP, 1988; 53). 
However, during the period 1988-94 the Lands Commission managed to keep royalty collection off-
reserves under its jurisdiction, even though it was never able to produce any accounts for collected 
timber royalties, which obviously raised doubts about its competence and credibility. 
On April 4, 1991 the Ministry of Lands and Natural Resources (now Ministry of Lands and Forests) 
issued a directive which transferred the responsibility for collection of timber royalties off-reserves to 
the Forestry Department (MLNR, 1991). Yet, with reference to the PNDC Law 4230, according to 
which the Lands Commission claimed to be the only legal authority to collect off-reserve timber 
royalties, and by documenting that the Forestry Department did not re-distribute fees collected off-
reserves to the legal beneficiaries (District Assemblies, Stools and Traditional Councils) the Lands 
Commission managed to fight the ministerial decree with success (LC, 1991). 
After the responsibility to collect off-reserve timber royalties was transferred to the Forestry Department 
a number of complaints, of which some were sent through the Ministry of Local Government (MLG), 
from district councils over not receiving their legal share of these began to materialise. In response, the 
Ministry of Lands and Natural Resources redrew its directive of April 4, 1991 and re-instituted the 
Lands Commission as collector of timber royalties off-reserves as of April 7, 1992 (MLG, 1992 and 
MLNR, 1992). 
When the Ministry of Lands and Forestry was formed under the 1992 Constitution, the Lands 
Commission, in effect, came under the Ministry's jurisdiction (Constitution 1992; 258, 2). This finally 
paved the way for the long awaited transfer of off-reserve royalty collection to the Forestry Department 
(MLF,1994). 
Timber royalties have been defined through legislative instrument (the Forest Fees Regulations), and 
rates were adjusted through revision of the regulations which has been very time consuming compared 
with the obvious alternative, annual administrative revisions in accordance with legally defined guidelines. 
The combination of royalties defined in Cedis (¢) and high inflation rates have eroded theirreal value 
between revisions of the rates. 
Royalties were species specific and until 1994 they were charged on a per tree basis. This system 
opened for a differentiation of royalties according to species value and rarity which, in theory, could be 
used to maximise government revenue and discourage extraction of over-exploited species. The most 
recent revision of royalties was by the Forest Fees (Amendment) Regulations (1993), which was 
gazetted on 21 January, 1994. Apart from raising royalty rates the Forest Fees (Amendment) 
Regulations (1993) stipulated royalties to be charged per cubic metre instead of per tree. 
30 
The PNDC (Provisional National Defence Council) Law 42 (1982) abrogated the 1979 Constitution 
and essentially functioned as Ghana's constitution from 31 December, 1981 to 7 January, 1993 
where the present (democratic) 1992 Constitution came into force. 
87

--- Page 112 ---

In December 1998 timber royalties were substituted by stumpage fees (Ember Resources Management 
Regulations, 1998; 21). A new formula was introduced according to which the stumpage fee for each 
species is calculated as: 
Stumpage fee = Log volume x Timber price x Stumpage rate 
Where: 
• 
Log volume is calculated in m3. 
• 
Timber price is 35% of the FOB price per m3 for air-dried lumber. 
• 
Stumpage rate is 20%, 10% or 5% depending on species (see appendix 9). 
The timber price will be based on the previous quarter's average price as published by the Forest 
Products Inspection Bureau. This should prevent stumpage fees from being eroded by inflation. 
40
