Three important characteristics about the royalty system are illustrated by figures 9.1,9.2 and 9.3. 
These are: 
• 
Between revisions of the rates in 1983, 1986, 1989, 1994 and 1999 the real value of royalties 
has been seriously eroded by inflation; 
• 
the "real" value of royalties as measured in current DM, and particularly in relation to FOB log 
and lumber prices, has stayed at a very low level until 1999; 
• 
during the period 1986-98 the small difference between royalty rates for Scarlet, Red and Pink 
Star species (especially when measured against log and lumber export prices) have hardly created 
effective financial incentives to reduce the harvest of Scarlet Star species and increase the harvest 
of Pink Star species instead. 
The effects of log export bans and costs of domestic processing prior to export of certain species should 
beincIuded in a comprehensive analysis offigures 9.19.2 and 9.3 before the latter two points above 
can be fully documented. Such analyses are found in chapter 11. 
9.1.2 Concession and Contract Area Rent 
Concession rent was based on the entire concession area regardless of timber harvest potentials, ie. 
concession rent was also billed on protected and inaccessible areas within a concession. The rent was 
introduced in paragraph 2 of the Forest Fees Regulations (1976) and was then ¢ 0.25 (DM 0.55) per 
ha per year. In 1983 the Forest Fees (Amendment) Regulations (1983) increased the concession rent 
90

--- Page 115 ---

to ¢ 2 (DM 0.24) per ha per year, and in 1992 it was supposedly increased from ¢ 2 to ¢ 100 (DM 
0.71 in 1992) perha per year (Ahadome, 1992; 2 and FIMP, 1995b; 9). Yet, it is doubtful whether 
this was ever implemented as the Forest Fee Regulations law was not amended in 1992 and the 
previous amendment in 1989 did only regulate timber royalties, not the concession rent (Forest Fees 
(Amendment) Regulations, 1989). In any case, the concession rent has up to 1994 hardly affected the 
logging profitability since there has been little resistance among concessionaires to pay the rent (FIMP, 
1995b; 9). 
With effect from 1 March, 1993 the concession rent was increased to ¢ 1,000 (DM 1.74 in 1994) per 
ha per year (Forest Fees (Amendment) Regulations, 1993). Since then, concessionaires have been 
increasingly calling for waivers of rent for poorly stocked areas, 
which has made the Forestry Department consider whether concession rent should be billed on the 
basis of productive areas only (FIMP, 1995b; 9). 
Concession rent has now been substituted by contract area rents of ¢ 1,200 per ha per year within 
forest reserves and ¢ 1,000 (DM 0.65 in 1999) per ha per year outside forest reserves and (Timber 
Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 27). All old timber concessions and leases have, in 
principle, been cancelled as at October, 1998 (Timber Resources Management Act, 1997). It is the 
duty of the Forestry Department to re-assess all potential timber utilisation areas and on this basis 
advertise timber utilisation contract areas (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 1_9)32. 
Accordingly, timber contract areas must in the future be expected to cover mainly productive 
areas. 
9.1.3 SilvicuItural Fee 
A silvicultural fee was charged both on- and off-reserves. It was legally defined in the Forest Fee 
Regulations (1976) and based on the area which had been harvested over the last year. The fee was 
until recently ¢ 50 and ¢ 20 per ha per year on- and off-reserves, respectively, aleve1 defined last time 
the rates were adjusted (Forest Fees (Amendment) Regulations, 1983). There were at least three 
problems with this fee: 
• 
It was minimal and probably not even enough to cover the costs of collection; 
• 
it was difficult to calculate as the areas harvested are difficult to check; 
• 
it was difficult to justify as the Forestry Department hardly undertakes any silvicultural work in 
areas where logging takes place, especially not off-reserves. 
lIED (1993; 100) assumed that the Silvicultural Fee was discontinued after 1992. However, this did 
not happen until December, 1998 when the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998; 42) 
revoked the Forest Fees Regulations (1976). 
9.1.4 Compensation Payments 
Compensation payments is a charge to concessionaires to cover the costs of restoring site damage from 
timber felling and extraction. The charge is legally backed in the Economic Plants Protection Decree 
(1979) and concerns the damage on agricultural crops caused by the felling of timber trees. In other 
words, the Economic Plants Protection Decree (1979) has been central in the legal relationship between 
32 
See section 12.3.2 for further details. 
91

--- Page 116 ---

timber companies holding off-reserve concessions and fanners who cultivate land which carnes timber 
trees. 
The Economic Plants Protection Decree (1979) opens two ways for specifying the level of 
compensation. It may be set by the courts in accordance with specific cases, or the Commissioner 
(Minister) responsible for cocoa (now Minister of Agriculture) may in liaison with the Commissioner 
(Minister) for Lands and Natural Resources (now Minister for Lands and Forestry) prescribe fixed rates 
for specified plants according to the age of such plants. 
Unfortunately, the law speaks only of cocoa as specified plants to be protected. The Commissioner 
(now Minister of Agriculture) may by executive instrument amend the law to cover other plants, but this 
has never happened. Accordingly, the now Ministers of Agriculture and Lands and Forestry are 
empowered to: 
• 
Let the Economic Plants Protection Decree (1979) cover all relevant agricultural crops; 
• 
specify compensation rates for crop damages due to timber felling and extraction. 
This issue has been and still is crucial for the management of trees on fannland (off-reserve timber 
resources) because logging companies have in many instances managed to avoid paying adequate 
compensation for crop damages if any at all (lIED, 1993; 61 and Personal observations). The reason 
being that individual fanners cannot, in practice, drag logging companies to court and claim their right 
to compensation. 
Since the responsible Ministers have not used their opportunity to amend the Economic Plants 
Protection Decree (1979) the compensation rates for damaged crops have always been subject for 
discussion between farmers and timber companies. On several instances during field trips we have 
personally witnessed (and been dragged into) disputes over compensation payments between 
representatives of logging companies and farmers. 
However, the Timber Resources Management Act (1997; 4 (2d)) now gives the individual fanner ade 
jure right to veto felling of trees on land he/she cultivates, regardless the crops. Accordingly, he/she 
may, in principle, set the price for allowing a certain tree to be felled. This is an improvement of fanners' 
rights. The impact on the ground of this rule is still to be discovered (see section 12.3.6 for further 
analyses). 
9.1.5 Fines and Imprisonment 
Fines are charged and imprisonment imposed as penalties for unauthorised activities. A number of laws 
specify different forest offences and fines, but the most important ones in this respect are: 
• 
The Forest Protection Decree (1974); 
• 
the Trees and Timber Decree (1974); 
• 
the Timber Resources Management Act (1997); 
• 
the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998). 
The Forest Protection Decree (1974) was amended in 1986 and changed name to the Forest Protection 
(Amendment) Law (1986). Within forest reserves it regulates the right to: 
92

--- Page 117 ---

• 
Fell, extract and convey trees and wood products; 
• 
collect, hunt and extract non-timber forest products; 
• 
cultivate land; 
• 
light fire; 
• 
graze livestock; 
• 
obstruct the channel of rivers, streams, canals and creeks; 
• 
use property marks on standing and felled trees. 
All the above activities are illegal without written consent from the competent forest authority (the 
Forestry Department) and offenders may be fined by a maximum of ¢ 10,000 for first time offences and 
¢ 20,000 for second or subsequent offences. In 1986 ¢ 10,000 equalled DM 251.57. The 1999 value 
of ¢ 10,000 is down to DM 6.45, which illustrates how inflation erodes the preventive effect fines may 
have had. However, if offences relate to the felling or transport of trees, timber or other manufactured 
forest produce (eg. cane furniture), the offender shall on top of the fine be liable to pay twice the 
commercial value of each tree or forest produce which is the subject-matter. 
An important legal aspect of the Forest Protection Decree (1974; 4) is that, with reference to the Forest 
Ordinance (Cap. 157) (1927;23), it does not prohibitthe exercise of admitted rights established when 
the forest reserve was constituted. Such rights usually include collection and extraction ofNTFPs by 
the land owners. 
The Trees and Timber Decree (1974) was amended in 1994 and changed name to the Trees and 
Timber (Amendment) Act (1994). Among other issues this law specifies: 
• 
The use of locality and property marks on stumps and trees extracted from on- and off-reserve 
concessions; 
• 
the right of forest officers to arrest suspected offenders. 
With the amendment in 1994 the standard fine for offences was raised from ¢ 5,000 to ¢ 5,000 ,000, 
which is still a substantial sum of money although inflation has reduced the real value of the fine by 
approximately 60% since 1994 (¢ 5,000 ,000equalledDM 8,130 in 1994 andDM 3,226 in 1999). 
The general offences are fraudulent use of property marks (other companies' marks or expired marks), 
incorrect marking of stumps and logs and clearing of land for agriculture in protected areas. 
The Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998; 41) have maintained the provision of summary 
conviction and a maximum fine of ¢ 5,000 ,000 or imprisonment for a term not exceeding 12 months, 
or both for obstructing any authorised person from the Forest Products Inspection Bureau or the 
Forestry Department in carrying out his/her duties and for possessing or transporting timber/wood 
without a valid conveyance certificate. For any officer who is found to be involved in such illegal 
activities the same penalties apply (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 41 (3)). This 
is a new provision which attempts to address the fact that previous legislation did not explicitly mention 
punishment of officials. The 1992 Constitution, however, requires that criminal offences, including 
administrative ones, and penalties must be established by law (Cirelli, 1997; 27). 
Officials charged with management offorest resources who by any act or omission in the performance 
of their duties facilitate the breach of any provision ofthe Timber Resources Management Act (1997), 
93

--- Page 118 ---

or condonelconnive with any person in breach of a provision of the Timber Resources Management Act 
(1997), may on summary conviction be sentenced to imprisonment for not less than six and not more 
than 12 months without the option of afine (Timber Resources Management Act, 1997; 17 (1)). The 
same provision applies for people who harvest, haul, offer for sale or stock timber in contravention with 
the Act (Timber Resources Management Act, 1997; 17 (2)). This is a tightening of previous provisions, 
intended to effectively discourage serious offences even if fines have been eroded by inflation (Cirelli, 
1997; 57). 
9.1.6 Property Marks 
The Trees and Timber Decree (1974) requires all companies to register a property mark at the Forestry 
Department Headquarters before they are allowed to fell any trees. The property mark identifies the 
company and it is endorsed for localities where the concessionaire proposes to operate. According to 
the Trees and Timber (Amendment) Act (1994), property marks must be renewed every six months 
(previously it was every year) and the 1994 amendment substantially increased the fee for renewal of 
the mark from ¢ 1,000 to ¢ 400,000 (DM 650 in 1994 and DM 258 in 1999). 
Apart from generating some government revenue, the renewal of property marks serves an important 
regulatory function. The Chief Conservator of Forests can, at his discretion, refuse to renew or cancel 
a property mark, which means that the company cannot operate legally. The financially most important 
aspect of this power is that the Chief Conservator of Forests may refuse to renew property marks of 
companies that are in arrears with their royalty payments. Concessionaires who are aggrieved by a 
refusal to register or cancellation of their property mark may complain to the responsible Minister (now 
the Minister of Lands and Forestry). 
However, recent history in Ghana has illustrated that such sensible legislation can only produce sensible 
results when it is de facto supported by the political leaders. During the 1980s and early 1990s, 
collection of royalty debts subject to property mark renewals was totally defunct. Companies in arrears 
with royalty payments were allowed to operate even though they had accumulated substantial royalty 
debts. Pressure was put on forestry officers to let timber companies operate, even though the Forestry 
Department had formally refused to renew their property marks. It was not uncommon that people 
claiming to come from "the Castle,,33 turned up at District Offices and told the District Forestry Officer 
to let timber companies operate without valid property marks. These people never carried their message 
in writing and rarely identified themselves. However, some District Forest Officers who continued to 
refuse the operation of companies without valid property marks were called to "the Castle" to explain 
their actions (Personal communication, several forestry officers). 
9.1. 7 Conveyance Certificates 
Conveyance certificates have, at the request of the Ministry of Lands and Forestry, been instituted by 
the Forestry Department in 1994. The system of conveyance certificates was primarily developed in 
response to an accelerating illegal timber harvest (particularl y off-reserves) by making the transport of 
such timber more difficult (PD, 1994a; 29). The legal basis forrequiring conveyance certificates was 
and is the Forest Protection Decree (1974; 7) according to which "the burden of proof that any forest 
33 
The Castle is a commonly used notion for the Danish built fortress, Christians borg in Accra. 
Since independence this has been the seat of the Ghanaian president and his nearest staff. 
94

--- Page 119 ---

produce has not been taken in contravention with this Decree shall lie upon the person in whose 
possession it is found". 
FD (l995b) stipulated that the procedures to be followed by anyone in relation to the felling of any off-
reserve tree should include the following three steps: 
• 
Trees to be felled should be inspected by the applicant (concessionaire or chainsaw operator), 
a representative of the Forestry Department and community representatives. After this inspection 
the District Forest Officer (DFO), acting upon the advice of the community representatives on 
farmers' objection to tree felling, will indicate which trees may be felled. 
• 
A Forestry Department official (in most cases the DFO) must issue a permit to fell trees outside 
a concession area, to saw logs to boards anywhere, or must indicate and certify which trees may 
be felled by a concessionaire as part of his logging plan. 
• 
A conveyance certificate is issued for all logs and boards aftertree felling and processing (where 
appropriate). The Conveyance Certificate is needed by anyone transporting wood anywhere, 
whether it has come from a forest reserve or from outside a forest reserve. This certificate will 
be issued after an inspection by a Forestry Department agent. The same procedures will apply 
for on- and off-reserves. 
Personal field observations in March, 1997 made it clear that all of the above three recommendations 
were not followed by the Ministry of Lands and Forestry, or they were not fully implemented by the 
Forestry Department. The first two points were only partially implemented, whereas conveyance 
certificates were from the outset required to transport all logs and processed wood because the Forest 
Products Inspection Bureau was and is not allowed to issue LMCs unless valid conveyance certificates 
for the logs are presented. 
Today the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998; 24) requires conveyance certificate to 
transport wood and logs anywhere. The rules on inspection of timber before and after felling off-
reserves have been modified according to the new system of timber utilisation contracts and permits (see 
section 12.3). 
The Forestry Department charges a fee of ¢ 10,000 per truck load (DM 6.45 in 1999). As a truck 
carries 10-15 m3 logs per load, this fee equals to a maximum of ¢ 1 ,000/m3 (DM 0.65/m3 in 1999) for 
logs, which must be considered minimal. 
9.1.8 Taungya Fees 
Taungya fees are payments made to the Forestry Department by groups of farmers for demarcation of 
areas within forest reserves allocated fortaungya. The rates are based on the costs of clearing around 
the perimeter of the taungya area. Previously, the taungya system was applied in an effort to re-plant 
degraded on-reserve areas. However, this approach has largely been discontinued because farmers 
tended to clear the same land over and again or clear non-degraded forest and (presumably in collusion 
with Forestry Department personnel) use taungya as window dressing (Personal communication, J. 
Francois, forrnerChiefConservator of Forests, 1993). Accordingly, taungya payments are insignificant 
(lIED, 1993; 99). 
95

--- Page 120 ---

9.1.9 Permits for Major Forest Products 
Major forest products are logs "sold" for special purposes such as carving, making of dugout canoes34, 
etc. Fees for felling pennits are charged at current royalty/stumpage fee rates in accordance with the 
Forest Fees Regulations (1976). Until Conveyance Certificates were required (cf. above), permits were 
subject to widespread abuse (lIED, 1993; 99). 
9.1.10 Permits for Minor Forest Products 
Fees on permits to collect minor forest products (NTFPs) within forest reserves are charged by District 
Forest Officers. The rates are, in principle, set on the basis of an annual review of wholesale market 
prices, but, in reality, there are large variances for the same product from District to District (lIED, 
1993; 99). 
There has been some doubt about the legal basis for the Forestry Department to charge any fees for 
permits to collect NTFPs. Section one in The Forest Protection Decree (1974) makes it illegal for 
anyone to collect and extract such goods from forest reserves without written authority from the 
Forestry Department. However, the Forest Protection Decree (1974; 1 (4)) states that "nothing in this 
section shall prohibit the exercise in a Forest Reserve by any person of any right under the Forest 
Ordinance (Cap. 157) for the time being is, or is treated as an admitted right". 
This fonnulation is copied from the Forest Ordinance (Cap. 157) (1927; 23) which protects existing 
admitted rights within forest reserves. The burden of proof that any good has not been illegally extracted 
from a forest reserve lies upon the person in whose possession it is found (Forest Ordinance (Cap. 
157), 1927; 31). Furthermore, the Governor (now Minister of Lands and Forestry) is empowered to 
prescribe fees for any purpose (Forest Ordinance (Cap. 157), 1927; 35)). 
Accordingly, the Forestry Department is legally allowed to charge fees for pennits to collect NTFPs 
unless the collector can prove that he/she acts in accordance with an admitted right to freely collect such 
products within a forest reserve. Such rights and the persons holding them should be described and 
publicly announced when individual forest reserves are gazetted. For example, when the Sukusuku 
Forest Reserve was gazetted in 1977, admitted rights of "the Subjects of Sefwi Wiawso Traditional 
Area" include free collection ofNTFPs for domestic use, but collection ofNTFPs in larger quantities 
for sale requires a pennit (Land and Concessions Bulletin, 1977; 84-85). Yet, many reserves were 
constituted more than 50 years ago, which has made it increasingly difficult for individuals to prove their 
admitted rights (see also section 12.1). 
9.1.11 Concession Preparation Fee 
In 1992 the Forestry Department introduced a once only fee of ¢ 500,000 (DM 1,789 in 1992 and DM 
322 in 1999) to coverthe costs of preparing each new concession. The legal basis for this fee is found 
in the Forest Ordinance (Cap. 157) (1927; 35). 
34 
Dugout canoes of considerable size (some over 15 m long) are the most common fishing 
vessels in Ghana. They are found in thousands along the coast where local fishermen 
launch them from the beach straight into the ocean. Big Wawa trees have highest preferen-
ce for canoe making. 
96

--- Page 121 ---

9.1.12 Registration Fees 
Section two of the Forest Products Inspection Bureau Law (1985) lists the functions of the Bureau, 
which include registration of all enterprises engaged in timber and wood processing industries. 
Furthermore, subject to written approval by the Secretary (now the Minister of Lands and Forestry), 
the Bureau may levy such charges as it may think fit on persons in the timber industry to cover the costs 
of running the Bureau (Forest Products Inspection Bureau Law, 1985; 7). 
Local wood product dealers are charged an annual registration fee of ¢ 5,000, non-exporting saw 
millers pay an annual fee of ¢ 10,000 and all exporters pay ¢ 30,000 per annum (lIED, 1993; 
100). 
The Ghana Chamber of Commerce, the Council of Export Promotion and the Ghana Shippers Council 
all receive payments for registration or licences to export (see table 9.1). However, the service provided 
by these institutions is practically nil. They are rather institutionalised bottlenecks which make the 
process of exporting unnecessarily cumbersome (Treue and Armstrong-Mensah, 1995). 
9.1.13 "Sale" of LMCs 
As previously described the Forest Products Inspection Bureau issues LMCs for extracted logs in 
Ghana. This is done at road check-points and the Bureau charges ¢ 200 per log in accordance with the 
Forest Products Inspection Bureau Law (1985). 
9.1.14 Export Levies to Cover the Costs of Running TEDB and FPIB 
The Timber Export Development Board and the Forest Products Inspection Bureau receives 1 % and 
2% of the FOB value, respectively, of all wood exports to cover the costs running the Bureaus. The 
Timber Export Development Board Law (1985; 14) and the Forest Products Inspection Bureau Law 
(1985; 14) provide for these charges. 
9.1.15 Export Levies on Logs and Air Dried Processed Timber 
The Trees and Timber (Amendment) Act (1994) introduced export levies on logs and air dried 
processed timber (lumber). The levy on logs varies between 10 and 30% of the FOB price, depending 
on species, and the levy on air dried lumber varies between 10 and 30% of the FOB price. 
Yet, the levy on logs was never applied as, in 1995, the Government put a complete ban on log exports. 
This was done administratively by putting a halt to the issuing oflog export licences. The ban is, in 
principle, temporary and still not included as part ofthe forestry legislation, but the forestry sector 
generally expects it to be permanent. The levies on air-dried lumber were for unexplained reasons not 
enforced before January, 1997 (FPIB, 1997). 
97

--- Page 122 ---

10 The Economics of Timber Exploitation 
Standing timber in Ghana is not sold on a free and open market. Until December, 1998 logging 
companies operated on a concession basis and concessions were allocated by the Government through 
the Ministry of Lands and Forests. With the Timber Resources Management Act (1997) and the Timber 
Resources Management Regulations (1998), timber rights are now supposed to be allocated according 
to technical, financial and social qualifications of timber companies. An expected element of financial 
bidding for acquiring access to standing timber must be considered abolished by the Government of 
Ghana (see section 12.3.2 for further details). By far most land, including forest reserves, is community 
owned (stool land), yet all rights to timber or trees on any land were in 1962 vested in the president in 
trust for the stools concerned (Concessions Act, 1962; 16, 1-3). 
Rights to timber have never been allocated according to a system of competitive financial bidding. The 
only official payments an applicant for a timber concession had to make related to the costs of 
processing the application (Timber Leases and Licences (Amendment) Regulations, 1979). This 
principle has been maintained for the future timber utilisation contracts (Timber Resources Management 
Regulations, 1998). Accordingly, the right to harvest timber trees had and still has no official price. Yet, 
the Government has and is officially influencing domestic timber prices through: 
• 
Timber royalties; 
• 
stumpage fees (substituted royalties in December, 1998); 
• 
export levies; 
• 
forest fees other than export levies; 
• 
log export bans. 
Unofficially the actual procedures for acquiring timber concessions may, of course, also influence the 
market. In any case, the domestic timber market is distorted and domestic log prices do not depend on 
market forces alone. Rather, they grow out of a combination of: 
• 
FOB export prices on wood products; 
• 
export levieslbans; 
• 
forest fees and taxes; 
• 
logging costs; 
• 
costs of further processing; 
• 
informal payments. 
The room for informal payments (bribes) is logically determined by the difference between actual 
willingness to pay for rights to standing timber and the forest fees which must be payed to acquire and 
exercise such rights. If informal payments do not mop up the difference between forest fees and the 
actual willingness of a company to pay for rights to standing timber, then the company earns windfall 
profits (profits that are higher than "normal" profit rates). 
If companies in general cannot afford to make informal payments, forest fees must, on average, equal 
the industry's willingness to pay for rights to standing timber. However, this does not necessarily mean 
that the timber industry is efficient and that the Government is maximising its revenue from forest fees. 
If there are restrictions on log exports or, as the case is in Ghana, a complete ban on log exports, low 
forest fees in combination with the world market prices of processed products may still generate 
"normal" profits at low recovery rates. 
98

--- Page 123 ---

Through a system of forest fees, log export bans and an officially controlled system of allocating rights 
to standing timber, the Government attempts to: 
• 
Protect and develop the domestic timber industry; 
• 
maximise earnings of foreign currency from wood exports; 
• 
control or promote harvest of different species; 
• 
generate revenue for the Government, local institutions and the land owning communities. 
This is quite a cocktail with many conflicting interests. Apart from to the above listed rational reasons 
for a Government to intervene the timber market, the very nature of political market interventions also 
opens the possibility of (unofficially) pursuing other and less commendable objectives. To which degree 
this happens depends on the credibility and accountability of the political and administrative 
system. 
This chapter will document that, until December, 1998, forest fees and taxes have been far below the 
average willingness of the industry to pay for standing timber. This has been of great benefit to the timber 
industry. Companies that operated at relatively high conversion rates could earn windfall profits (or 
finance high unofficial payments) and companies that were utterly inefficient and operated at very low 
conversion rates were able to earn "normal" profits and stay in business. The costs have been borne by 
the average Ghanaian taxpayer and the land owning communities because the most valuable timber 
species have been over-exploited (loss of natural capital). Furthermore, the official forest fees have not 
always covered the costs of forest management and in any case resulted in small benefits for the land 
owners (this is further analysed in section 11.1 and chapter 12). 
10.1 Stumpage Values 
The willingness to pay for rights to standing timber is generally known as the stumpage value of standing 
timber. In a controlled market where the willingness to pay for standing timber cannot be observed 
directly, the stumpage value is defined as the selling prices oflogs or other wood products less the costs 
of production, induding a "normal" profit on capital (FAO, 1983; 33-48). 
The same tree may, therefore, represent different stumpage values depending on which product (lumber, 
plywood, paper, veneer, etc.) is used as basis forthecalculation. Furthermore, different companies 
manufacturing the same product may, due to differences in efficiency and use of installed processing 
capacity, have different willingness to pay for getting access to the same standing timber. 
10.2 Domestic and FOB Log Prices 
The log export from Ghana was first regulated in 1979 where the export in log form of 13 species 
(counted by trade names) was banned. In 1988, log exports of an additional four species were banned, 
and in 1993 the log export ofWawa was banned. Following the log export boom in 1993-94, export 
oflogs was completely banned in 1995. The species banned from log exports are shown in table 10.1 
and appendix 11. It is seen that the export in log form of most Scarlet and many Red Star species was 
already banned in 1979 or 1988. 
99

--- Page 124 ---

It is, therefore, not possible to make direct comparisons between recent domestic and FOB log prices 
for many of the most important export species. However, in 1993, during negotiations over revising the 
timber royalties, TEDB published a comprehensive list of FOB log prices from other West-African 
countries. Domestic prices oflogs delivered at mills are not subject to official monitoring. However, 
price lists for the most important species in 1993, 1994 and 1996 have, for this study, been created 
through a combination of interviews and literature. The summary results are shown in table 10.1 and 
appendix 11 provides the details.

100

--- Page 125 ---

The table clearly illustrates that domestic log prices are much lower than the 1993 FOB log prices. 
Measured against the 1993 FOB log prices or in current DM, domestic log prices have also shown a 
generally falling tendency since 1993. This result is not surprising for species which are subject to the 
log export ban and it illustrates the substantial indirect subsidy that log export bans are to wood 
processing companies. 
Why the domestic log prices for species that could be exported as logs are also generally much lower 
than the FOB log prices is less obvious. One reason might be that logs of otherwise exportable species 
sold at the domestic market are, in general, not of export quality (this may also to some degree be the 
case for banned species). Another reason could be that companies supplying logs to the domestic 
market (these tend to be small enterprises) have not been able to penetrate the export market due to 
lacking organisational skills and/or because their supply capacity per company has been too small to 
attract overseas log buyers. 
There are also indications that the bigger integrated wood industries are suppressing the domestic log 
prices through cartel-like arrangements (Overseas Consultancy Service, 1995; 21). This observation 
is supported by the fact that a few companies are dominating the wood export (cf. section 8.7.3) which 
certainly increases the risk of viable cartel-like arrangements to be formed. Furthermore, the very low 
1996 domestic log prices indicate that, contrary to what the resource scarcity would suggest, the log 
market in Ghana has been a buyer's rather than a seller's market. 
Whatever the complete explanation for the difference between the FOB and domestic log prices might 
be, the magnitude of this difference in itself proves that the domestic log market is heavily distorted. 
Furthermore, it is logical that the log export bans must account for a major part of this price distortion 
and that the bans have greatly strengthened the bargaining power of processing companies over logging 
companies. 
10.3 Estimated Stumpage Values based on Log Prices 
On the basis of the FOB and domestic log prices given in table 10.1 it is possible to estimate the 
stumpage value of standing timber. A problem in this respect is that the costs oflogging may, in general, 
only be obtained through interviews. Since such cost figures can be used politically to increase royalty 
rates, logging companies have an obvious interest in overstating their costs when asked by out-
siders. 
The costs of logging and transport to mill have been estimated by lIED (1993; app. 14), Bach, Gram 
and Treue (1995) and Treue and Armstrong-Mensah (1995; 87). Included in the cost estimates 
are: 
• 
Planning by the logging company; 
• 
road/bridge construction; 
• 
costs of felling (chain saw) and extraction to bush road (caterpillar, skidder or tractor); 
• 
costs of transport to mill (log truck); 
• 
depreciation costs on capital (caterpillars, graders, skidders, tractors, chainsaws and 
trucks); 
• 
a 20% "normal" profit on capital. 
Average figures for the different estimates oflogging costs converted into current DMlm' are shown in 
table 10.2.

For comparison it is of interest to calculate the, for some species, hypothetical 1993 stumpage value on 
the basis of FOB log prices. This involves some extra costs in addition to the logging costs. Namely, 
export levies, port handling charges and bank charges. A 1 % and 2% export levy on all wood exports 
is paid to TEDB andFPIB, respectively. Bank and port handling charges are approximately 1.75% and 
5.25% of the FOB value, respectively (Treue and Armstrong-Mensah, 1995; 103).

Not surprisingly table 10.3 shows that the stumpage values based on 1993 FOB log prices are 
significantly higher than the stumpage values based on domestic log prices. The table also indicates that 
the logging business has become less and less profitable apparently leading to negative stumpage values 
for a number of species in 1994 and 1996. This suggests that the estimated logging costs are on the high 
side, but the generally falling domestic log prices also suggest that the log buyers (processing industries) 
103

--- Page 128 ---

have increasingly succeeded in negotiating log prices downwards. The complete ban on log exports in 
1995 has naturally helped the log buyers in doing so. 
Another issue which strongly suggests the logging costs in table 10.2 are higher than the actual costs is 
that the estimated stumpage values for many species are either negative or do not leave much or any 
room for timber royalties and other forest fees, which have actually been paid. The negative stumpage 
values for Wawa in 1994 and 1996 are particularly surprising and unrealistic since the recorded 
extraction ofWawa was approximately 365,000 m3 and 437,000m3, or 22% and 37% of the total 
recorded extraction in 1994 and 1996, respectively. 
10.4 Estimated Stumpage Values based on FOB Lumber prices 
Since saw mills can export their products it is reasonable to assume that stumpage values calculated on 
the basis of FOB lumber prices might be higher than the ones derived from domestic log prices. 
However, the lumber processing costs must also be based on information obtained from saw mills and 
like the case is for logging companies they too have a political interest in making their processing costs 
appear as high as possible. 
In fact, it seems that the saw mills which have been interviewed about the costs of processing and 
exporting lumber have answered strategically. The results of different saw milling cost surveys are 
summarised in table lOA.

of produced lumber.

of lumber production. The FOB lumber prices for Wawa in table 10.4 are even on the high side when 
used in this context, since they include an unknown proportion of kiln-dried lumber (the dataavailab1e 
does not allow for a separation of kiln- and air-dried lumber).

Naturally, the derived stumpage values in table 10.6 must be on the low side since the calculation 
presupposes that the stumpage value ofWawa is zero. Considering that Wawa by volume is both the 
prime export and most harvested species, its actual stumpage value to both saw millers and loggers is 
hardly zero. Nevertheless, the stumpage values in table 10.6 are likely to be better estimates forthe 
actual willingness to pay for standing timber than the 1996 stumpage values derived in table 
10.3. 
10.5 Conclusion on Derived Stumpage Values 
The most valuable information to be drawn from the above calculations of stumpage values is that the 
available data on logs and lumber processing costs must be rather misleading. Technically this is a 
meagre result, but politically it carries some weight. It shows that, until December, 1998, the policy on 
timber taxation (revision of royalty rates) must have been based on false premises or, alternatively, no 
serious attempt has been made to base the forest fees and timber taxation on what the timber industry 
presumably was willing to pay for rights and access to standing timber. Neither ofthese explanations 
are very flattering. 
It this connection it is worthwhile to mention that both the study of General Woods (1992) and the one 
of lIED (1993) had recommendations on forest fees and taxation among their primary objectives. In 
addition, both of these studies have been used by the Forest Resource Management Project, which has 
policy reform at the top of its agenda (FRMP, 1988; iv). 
The revision and substantial increases of timber royalties, which the new stumpage fee system 
represents, shows that at least some forest policy makers in Ghana have also been aware that the 
willingness of the timber industry to pay for standing timber was probably higher than the various 
estimated stumpage values indicated, and definitely higher than the pre-1999 forest fees and taxes. That 
timber royalties were not increased substantially at an earlier stage probably indicates how difficult it can 
sometimes been to make politicians face the economic, social and environmental realities of timber 
exploitation. 
10.6 Observed Actual Willingness to Pay for Standing Timber 
One of the appraisal studies for the Forest Resource Management Project refers to an observed 
willingness to pay for standing timber. It was noticed that wood processing industries were paying 50% 
of the domestic log price for timber they extracted from other companies' concessions (Wodd Bank, 
1986, Working Paper V; 26). However, it appears that the economic and political significance ofthis 
observation was for a long time not being (allowed to be) pursued in any detail. At least it is not 
explicitly referred to in thefinal Forest Resource Management Programme document (FRMP, 1988). 
Interestingly, the issue was later followed-up by the Ghanaian Government, and timber royalties that 
equalled the actual willingness to pay have, in fact, been enforced for a short period during the early 
1990s. This was when the National Investigation Committee (NIC) looked into various "Malpractices 
in the Timber Sector" and, among a lot of other things, found that, through a sub-letting system, 64 out 
of 438 concessions were operated illegally35 by companies other than the concession holders (NIC 
Report, 1991; Part A. No. II). That this was going on could, however, not have come as a great 
surprise as Silviconsult already pointed it out in 1985 (Silviconsult, 1985; 48). 
35 
No sub-letting of concessions was valid without the written consent of the 
Minister (Concessions Act, 1962; 13), and no timber lease is transferable 
(Timber Lease and Licence Regulations, 1962; 5,3). 
107

--- Page 132 ---

Concerning the commercial value of standing timber, the NIC Report (1991) found that the sub-letting 
system was based on species specific prices per m3 of logs extracted from the concessions. Not 
surprisingly, these prices were simply 50% of the domestic log prices and it was the original concession 
holder who paid the royalties. 
In 1991, most of the companies which operated on sub-let concessions were allowed to continue on 
temporary leases because they employed a lot of people and contributed to the wood export. Some 
of these companies did not have their own concessions and many of the original concession holders did, 
in fact, not have any or sufficient logging equipment to operate their concessions. Under the temporary 
timber leases payments for extracting timber from sub-led concessions (50% of the domestic log prices) 
were continued in the place of royalties. Revenue from this special source was paid into the 
Government's "Special Forestry Improvement Account,,36. Fines and various voluntary payments, 
resulting from the investigation of the timber industry, were also paid into this account. By 1991, a total 
of DM 25,551 ,248 had been paid into the Special Forestry Improvement Account and fines totalling 
DM 32,403,752 and Cedis 72,550,000 (DM 296,558 in 1991) were issued (NIC Report, 1991). 
Following the NIC investigation into the timber industry it was the intention that the principles af 
allocating timber concessions should be completely overhauled. However, no substantial progress on 
this issue was made until 1998 (see section 12.3 for further details). Meanwhile, the timber industry 
needed raw logs and the illegal sub-letting of concessions continued. This has, during the collection of 
data for this study, been confirmed in numerous interviews with timber companies. The timber sector 
referred to the sub-letting system as the "fifty-fifty arrangement", because the concession holder got 
50% of the domestic log price from the company which operates hislher concession. 
The available data of observed willingness to pay for standing timber are for selected species and star 
groups summarised in table 10.7 
By comparing the data in table 10.7 with the estimated stumpage values in table 10.3 it is seen that for 
Scarlet Star species, excluding Wawa, the difference between observed willingness to pay and the 
domestic log price based stumpage value estimates is not so big. This is, however, the case for Red and 
Pink Star species where table 10.7 indicates a much higher commercial value than does table 
10.3. 
36 
It seems that the existence of this account is only semi-official. The MLF has 
not been willing to inform about the current balance of the account and how 
money from it has been used, except that only the Minister can operate the 
account and the money must be used for "special" forestry related purposes.

10.6.1 Validity of the Observed Willingness to Pay for Standing Timber 
Before applying 50% of the domestic log price as a measure for the "real" commercial value of standing 
timber it is reasonable to consider the justification of doing so. The obvious strong point of using an 
actual market price is that it directly reflects the timber industry's willingness to pay for standing timber, 
since the concession holders have paid the timber royalties and other taxes. Accordingly, this value 
measure combines all industrial uses of timber, and it is not influenced by questionable estimates of the 
cost and profitability of logging and further processing. 
A weakness is that an unknown volume, but clearly not all standing timber, has been "sold" at 50% of 
the domestic log price. The observed price could, therefore, reflect the marginal rather than the average 
willingness to pay for standing timber and may, if used on the total recorded timber extraction, over-
estimate the real commercial value of the resource. In other words, companies which have operated 
their own as well as others' concessions and bought some logs at the domestic market may, on average, 
have paid less than 50% of the domestic log price per m3 oflog intake. Yet, the actual price of acquiring 
a timber concession has hardly been limited to the costs of processing the application. 
It should also be considered that, since 1979, the domestic log prices have been heavily distorted 
through log export bans. After the complete log export ban in 1995 domestic log prices may even have 
been further suppressed through cartel-like arrangements between the big processing companies. 
Accordingly, the domestic log prices have probably been below the biggest and most efficient mills' 
actual willingness to pay. 
In conclusion, 50% of the domestic log price is considered the most reliable current estimate of the 
"real" commercial value of standing timber. Theoretically, this may over-estimate the commercial value 
of all timber extracted in the past. Yet, the falling trend in domestic log prices since 1993 and indications 
of cartel-like arrangements among big log buyers suggest that the actual willingness to pay for logs could 
be higher than what the domestic log prices imply. 
Furthermore, from a national economic, resource conservation and industrial development point of view, 
domestic log prices that are far below the FOB log prices cannot be desirable in the longer term, 
because they must reflect either an inefficient domestic wood industry or an inefficient domestic log 
market or (most likely) a combination of the two. Accordingly, in the present situation where domestic 
log prices appear to be not even half the FOB log prices (cf. table 10.1) it would, from a political point 
of view, not be rational to accept 50% of domestic log prices as a long-term maximum commercial value 
of standing timber. Rather, it ought to be regarded as an interim minimum value that, through appropriate 
political interventions, should be gradually increased. 
10.7 Willingness to Pay Compared with Forest Taxes and Fees 
Based on the above considerations about the observed willingness to pay for standing timber it is 
possible to estimate the financial room for informal payments and/or windfall profits in the timber sector. 
The forest fees and taxes to be compared with the observed willingness to pay include, in order of 
magnitude (see appendix 12 for details): 
• 
Timber royalties/stumpage fees; 
• 
concession rent/contract area rent; 
• 
property mark renewal; 
• 
conveyance certificates; 
• 
silvicultural fees. 
110

--- Page 135 ---

The difference between the observed willingness to pay and official forest fees and taxes is, for star 
groups, illustrated in figures 10.1, 10.2 and 10.3 (the data behind the figures is presented in appendix 
12). It is seen that for 1993 this difference is exceptionally high due to the low level of forest fees and 
the apparently very high domestic log prices. Otherwise, during the period 1986-98 the difference 
roughly varies between DM 10 and 40 per m3, depending on year and species group. Apparently, the 
new stumpage fees have closed the gap between forest fees and the willingness to pay for Scarlet and 
Red Star species37. This shows a remarkable change in the political commitment to tax timber 
exploitation. The data available for Pink Star species is somewhat meagre, so the gap between forest 
fees and the willingness to pay in 1999 may not be of the magnitude indicated by figure 10.3. It does, 
however, make some sense ifforest taxes on Pink Star species are kept at a relatively low level, as this 
would promote the utilisation of these species.

The figures in table 10.8 are quite remarkable. It is seen that in 1996 alone the forest taxation revenue 
foregone was in the order ofDM 22 million. Assuming collection rates of 100%, the official forest fees 
and taxes generally appear to have captured only 20-40% of the willingness to pay for standing timber. 
Since table 10.8 counts the forest fees and taxes value forthe total annual extractions but only includes 
the willingness to pay values for species where domestic log prices have been available, the taxation 
113

--- Page 138 ---

revenue foregone is probably not over-estimated. Accordingly, it is likely that, during the period 1993-
96, an annual amount not less than DM 20 million has been available for (i) informal payments, (ii) 
windfall profits and (iii) keeping inefficient wood industries from going bankrupt. 
The huge difference in willingness to pay value, and forest fees and taxes value of the 1993 recorded 
extraction, is partly caused by the unusually high extraction rate that year (the log export boom) and the 
low DM value of forest fees and taxes. In addition, domestic log prices for almost all extracted species 
were available for 1993. Yet, the apparently high, and perhaps more questionable, domestic log prices 
in 1993 account for most of the difference in value. It is, therefore, doubtful whether the 1993 forest 
taxation revenue foregone was actually in the order ofDM 97 million. On the other hand, it seems 
reasonable to assume that the revenue foregone was especially high this year. 
10.8 Commercial Value of Estimated Annual Allowable Cuts 
For obvious reasons it is of interest to estimate the commercial value of on-reserve AACs, since this 
would indicate the size of the long-term available revenue from on-reserve timber extraction. To this end 
it is necessary to operate at least with the following scenarios: 
• 
Value of the AAC under large-grained protection only; 
• 
value of the AAC under large-grained protection only, or of the total on- and off-reserve 
recorded extraction for species which, due to the market situation, are not exploited to the limit 
of their AACs; 
• 
value of the AAC under large- and fine-grained protection; 
• 
value of the AAC under large- and fine-grained protection, or of the total on- and off-reserve 
recorded extraction for species which, due to the market situation, are not exploited to the limit 
of their AACs. 
These scenarios are summarised in tables 10.9, ID.10, 10.11 and 10.12. It should be noted that the 
scenarios operate with average 1994 and 1996 values for willingness to pay for standing timber, which 
makes the number of species for which an observed willingness to pay is available higher than if only 
1996 values were used. Separate calculations are also made that apply minimum star group values for 
species where no 1994 or 1996 data on willingness to pay for standing timber have been available. To 
illustrate the effect of the new stumpage fees, both the 1996 and the 1999 value of AACs in terms of 
official forest fees and taxes are presented in the tables.

The scenarios in tables 10.9 and 10.11 are only theoretical in the sense that the total recorded 1996 
extraction of especially Pink Star species was less than 20% the AAC. Accordingly, the value 
calculations in these two scenarios, where the entire AAC is assumed utilised, should be treated with 
caution. Yet, if the on-reserve harvest of Scarlet and Red Star species could be reduced to their AACs, 
116

--- Page 141 ---

the demand for, and hence, the revenue from Pink Star species would most probably increase. In 1996 
prices, the long-term sustained forest tax revenue from forest reserves might, therefore, be between DM 
14 and 22 million. 
The scenarios in tables 10.10 and 10.12 may, on the other hand, be regarded as realistic since they 
reflect the current market situation. The immediately available annual tax revenue from sustained yield 
timber harvest on-reserves should, therefore, be between DM 10 and 12 million in 1996 prices. 
Measured by official 1999 forest fees and taxes, the annual revenue would be around DM 8 
million. 
Going back to table 10.8 it is seen that official forest fees and taxes due from the, especially for Scarlet 
Star species, unsustainable 1996 extraction was approximately DM 12.6 million. The scenarios in tables 
10.10 and 10.12 suggest that a shift to extraction levels within the species specific AACs and a taxation 
of the timber extraction according to the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber would generate 
a revenue of approximately the same size from forest reserves only. The 1999 forest fees and taxes 
could initially generate around DM 8 million from forest reserves. It should, however, be possible to 
gradually increase the on-reserve revenue through increasing the stumpage fees. In addition, an 
increased extraction of Pink Star species is expectable. 
10.9 Revenue from Timber Off-Reserves 
Sustainable or sustained yield management of timber resources off-reserves can only be achieved if it 
is in the financial interest of the land owners (this issue is further discussed in chapter 12). Such 
incentives might be possible to establish, but even if this is done it is likely that a sustained off-reserve 
timber yield will be much lower than the present extraction. Accordingly, the long-term sustainable 
revenue from off-reserve timber resources is practically impossible to estimate. 
The off-reserve lifetime scenarios for star groups presented in section 7.6 may, however, be used to 
indicate the size of the potential revenue from off-reserve timber resources. If the 1996 off-reserve 
extraction level continues, it was estimated that (i) Scarlet Star species will be logged out within the 
coming 15-39 years, (ii) Red Star species will be logged out within the coming 125-126 years and (iii) 
Pink Star species will be logged out within the coming 315-436 years. 
By combining the 1996 off-reserve extraction with the 1996 DM values of willingness to pay and forest 
taxes and fees, rough estimates ofthe off-reserve revenues may be calculated. This is presented in table 
10.13. 
Since the off-reserve inventory data does not provide species specific data, the value figures in table 
10.13 should only be regarded as rough estimates. Nevertheless, it is obvious that the off-reserve timber 
resource represents a substantial revenue potential for many years to come. In terms of the 1999 official 
forest taxes and fees, the annual revenue could be sustained at more than DM 19 million for at least 
another 15 years, whereas the actual revenue potential in this period is likely to be between DM 25 and 
26 million annually.

This is big money, but the off-reserve revenue potential is likely to disappear very soon unless the annual 
off-reserve timber exploitation is reduced. If the political will is there, it should not be impossible to 
combine the substantial revenue potential with incentives for land owning communities to grow and 
protect off-reserve timber resources. In theory, it just a matter of adjusting the income distribution 
associated with timber harvest. This would help to secure the resource base, which is in the long-term 
interest for the timber industry (and the national economy) and it would also improve the odds for 
reducing the on-reserve harvest to the AAC of currently over-exploited species. However, the 
prognosis for a sustained commercial timber production off-reserves is not particularly good. This is 
further discussed in section 12.3.7. 
10.10 Conclusion on the Economics of Timber Exploitation 
Clearly, three major politically determined issues have created problematic economic incentives in the 
forest sector. These are: 
• 
Log export bans; 
• 
official forest fees and taxes that, until 1999 , were far below and probably not even half of the 
industry's actual willingness to pay for standing timber; 
• 
allocation of rights to standing timber. 
The incentives created have kept domestic log prices artificially low and opened the opportunity for log 
processing companies to: 
• 
Earn windfall profits; 
118

--- Page 143 ---

• 
cover high informal costs; 
• 
operate profitably at very low recovery rates. 
The low domestic log prices obviously produce a false signal of all timber species being abundant. Had 
standing timber been allocated according market forces only, the actual scarcity of especially Scarlet 
and Red Star species would have led to higher domestic log prices, irrespective oflog export bans. 
Accordingly, the financial incentives have, until 1999, strongly favoured the documented over-
exploitation of Scarlet and Red Star species which the wood export based foreign exchange earnings 
depend so heavily on. Furthermore, it appears that the low forest fees and taxes have, at least during 
the period 1993-96, resulted in an annual forest revenue foregone of some DM 20 million. Before 1993, 
the revenue foregone might have been in the order of DM 10 million. 
Since practically all Scarlet and most Red Star species are de facto over-exploited on-reserves, harvest 
control mechanisms other than financial incentives (yield allocation and harvest control) have not been 
working properly. This implies that the Forestry Department field staff has either been de-motivated to 
control logging operations or actively motivated to refrain from controlling logging operations. The 
considerable financial room for informal payments, that some companies must have had, suggests the 
latter possibility cannot be ruled out. 
The new legislation on forest revenues (especially stumpage fees) and punishments for offences 
represents a bold attempt to address the former unfortunate combination oflow motivation to control 
logging operations and funds being available for informal payments (Timber Resources Management 
Act, 1997 and Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998). This is a major improvement of the 
incentives for promoting sustainable timber utilisation which leaves hope for the future. 
Off-reserve timber resources contribute significantly to the annual harvest, and hence, to the revenue 
earned as well as foregone. In 1996 alone, data indicate that approximately DM 17 million of potential 
forest revenue was foregone. The current level of exploitation off-reserves can, however, not continue 
indefinitely, and contrary to forest reserves there is no de jure prescriptions for a sustained yield 
management of timber resources off-reserves. Yet, the substantial value ofthis resource, if measured 
in terms of the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber or even in terms of 1999 forest fees and 
taxes, suggests that appropriate political interventions could create sustainable financial incentives for 
protecting and managing off-reserve timber trees. It should, in other words, be possible to conserve the 
off-reserve timber resource by getting the distribution of wealth from timber exploitation right, now that 
the controlled "price" on standing timber has been adjusted to almost equal the market value. 
The distribution of costs and benefits from timber exploitation is, in addition to the distorted domestic 
log market, another key issue affecting the management of timber resources. Particularly off-reserves 
this is very important because, in practice, financial incenti ves alone determine the management regime. 
On-reserves the silvicultural management prescriptions and the control function of the Forestry 
Department has some influence on the timber harvest, although the pre-1999 financial incentives and 
the distribution of costs and benefits associated with timber exploitation appears to have de-stabilised 
rather than supported this function. Accordingly, it is necessary to consider the distributional effects and 
incentive structure created by the past and newly established system of rights and responsibilities in 
relation to commercial timber exploitation. 
119

--- Page 144 ---

11 Instruments to Regulate the Timber Harvest 
The timber harvest may be regulated through demand-side and supply-side measures. Both are 
politically determined, but they work in different ways and have different strengths and weaknesses. In 
practice, none of the two can stand alone in a politically regulated timber market as the case is for 
Ghana. How well demand-side measures can function depends to a large degree on which supply-side 
measures are in place and vice versa. 
Demand-side measures like forest taxeS/fees, export levies and export bans have three main objectives: 
• 
To regulate the market demand for standing timber; 
• 
to generate (government) revenue from the utilisation of standing timber; 
• 
to develop the domestic wood industry. 
The strength of demand-side measures is that they are fairly cheap to implement and, in most cases, not 
difficult and costly to monitor. Yet, from a harvest control point of view, their great weakne:;s is that they 
work indirectly, ie. through the market which is even distorted by the very same measures. Another 
crucial aspect of demand-side measures is their distributional effects. Politically, it is obviously very 
important how the costs and benefits created by the package of demand-side measures are distributed 
among stakeholders. At least five different stakeholder groups should be considered in this context. 
These are: 
• 
The land-owning communities; 
• 
the average Ghanaian citizen/tax payer; 
• 
the log processing industries; 
• 
the logging companies which are not part of an integrated industry; 
• 
people in or associated with the official forestry/timber administration. 
Supply-side measures have one major objective. To keep the timber harvest within socially, 
environmentally and silviculturally acceptable limits. That is, to ensure that narrow financial interests in 
utilising standing timber do not lead to destruction or degradation of the forest resource and the values 
it represents to society at large. The strength of supply-side measures is that, in theory, they can be 
implemented directly and irrespective of the market forces. The weakness is, of course, that the un-
official side of the market can, in practice, completely undermine the effectiveness of supply-side 
measures. 
11.1 Assessment of Employed Demand-Side Measures 
The criteria which employed demand-side measures should be assessed against are their ability 
to: 
• 
Generate sufficient revenue to manage the forest resource; 
• 
regulate the timber harvest in accordance with the relative scarcity of species; 
• 
promote further and more efficient processing in the wood industry; 
• 
promote an equitable distribution of costs and benefits associated with the timber exploi-
tation. 
120

--- Page 145 ---

The previous chapters have already indicated that, in general, the employed package of demand-side 
measures have until 1999 failed on almost all accounts. General Woods (1992; WD no.3; 26) 
documents that, during the period 1985-91, timber royalties collected covered between 41 % and 95% 
(on average 69%) of the expenditures from the Forest Improvement Fund which is used to finance the 
recurrent costs of the Forestry Department and the Forestry School in Sunyani. For the period 1993-
96, table 11.1 summarises the available data on overall costs of and revenue generated from forest 
management and timber extraction. In addition, the table indicates the potential revenues in terms of 
observed willingness to pay and the new 1999 forest fees and taxes.

It should be noted that the 1995-96 expenditure figures are rough estimates for a proposed new semi-
autonomous Forestry Service (to replace the current Forestry Department) (Touche Ross, 1995; Vol 
II; 35). However, these estimates appear to be taken almost directly out ofthe Forestry Department's 
annual accounts allowing for a 30% salary increase in real terms for professional staffs and a total staff 
number of 4,394 which is slightly below the 1995 staff number of 4,648. Accordingly, the 1995-96 
expenditure estimates are not likely to deviate much from the actual expenditures. 
From the table it is seen that: 
• 
The revenue collected in 1993 did only cover approximately 64% of actual expenditures; 
• 
the revenue collected in 1995-96 is likely to have exceeded actual expenditures by DM 4-11 
million; 
• 
in 1993, approximately DM 1.0 million of old royalty debts was collected; 
• 
in 1994, a royalty debt of approximately DM 11.0 million was built up; 
• 
in 1995, approximately DM 1.2 million of old royalty debts was collected; 
• 
in 1996, approximately DM 1.5 million of old royalty debts was collected; 
• 
if the cost of implementing sustainable/sustained yield forest management is around DM 7.5 
million per year, this may be financed from forest reserves alone under the 1999 taxation; 
• 
if fees and taxes were raised to the industry's willingness to pay, the cost of forest management 
could be covered from forest reserves alone leaving a surplus of approximately DM 4.5 million 
per year. 
It was an old trick of the timber industry to reduce royalty payments in real terms by delaying payments, 
because royalty debts were not adjusted for inflation under the pre-1999 legislation (Personal 
Communication, 1995; Francis Bilson-Ogoe and George Nil Mbroh Ashis, Concessions Unit, FD). 
Judging from the data in table 11.1 this seems to have been going on during the log export boom in 1994 
(in 1994 and 1996 the CediIDM exchange rate was 615 and 1,131 respectively). Clearly, the Forestry 
DepartmentlMinistry of Lands and Forestry has foregone substantial tax revenues by allowing this 
practice. This loophole has now been closed by the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998; 
25) which stipulates bank rates to be charged on royalty debts older than 30 days. 
Summing up, it appears that the exploitation of timber has resulted in a net loss for the average Ghanaian 
taxpayerlcitizen during the period 1986-93. Possibly this was also the case for 1994, but since 1995 
forest taxes and fees collected have probably exceeded the costs of forest management.by several 
million DM. Whether this has resulted in a net gain for the average tax payer is, however, not certain. 
It has not been possible to verify how much of the collected revenue has, in fact, been used to finance 
the Forestry Department and how much has been plowed back to the local communities. If the rules 
have actually been followed, then the average taxpayer has suffered a considerable net loss (see sections 
12.1 and 12.2.2). 
Substantial revenue has been foregone by allowing inflation to erode old royalty debts and by the fact 
that forest fees and taxes were not charged according to the industry's willingness to pay for standing 
timber. In 1996 the potential revenue from the recorded (non-sustainable) extraction could have been 
approximately DM 35 million or almost 2.7 times the revenue actually collected. Harvest according to 
the AAC under large-grained protection only, or according to the 1996 extraction level when this is 
below the AAC would, from forest reserves alone, have generated almost the same revenue as the 
122

--- Page 147 ---

actual collected amount from both on- and off-reserves iffees and taxes had equalled the actual 
willingness to pay. 
The most important conclusion to be drawn from the above analysis is, however, that forest fees and 
taxes even from forest reserves alone could easily finance the costs of sustainable or sustained yield 
management of forest reserves. This conclusion rests on fairly rough cost and, to a lesser degree, 
potential revenue estimates, but the gap of more that DM 4 million between the two sends a clear 
message. It can be done. 
The new stumpage fee system would initially only generate around DM 8.6 million, leaving a surplus of 
approximately DM 1.1 million from forest reserves if the harvest is held within the AAC. While the 
potential revenue appears to be somewhat higher, the (long awaited) abolishment of the low timber 
royalties does mark an important shift in policy towards securing that the costs of wise forest 
management may actually be covered by forest revenues. An outstanding issue in this respect is, 
however, whether it would be politically acceptable if almost 90% ofthe revenue from forest reserves 
should go to fund the Forestry Department (or a future Forestry Service), leaving only 10% to local 
communities (see section 12.1.2 forfurther analyses). 
11.1.1 Timber Royalties and Stumpage Fees 
Among the forest taxes and fees, timber royalties played and stumpage fees play the most important role 
in relation to harvest control because they differ among species. In terms of generating revenue, timber 
royalties and especially the new stumpage fees also stand out as a main source. 
In section 9.1.1 it was documented that: 
• 
Between revisions of the rates, the real value of timber royalties has been heavily eroded by 
inflation; 
• 
measured in current DM and as per cent of FOB log and lumber prices, the royalty rates have 
fluctuated at a very low level; 
• 
the difference in royalty rates between Scarlet, Red and Pink Star species have been rather small; 
• 
the new stumpage fees are significantly higher than the old royalties and they differ substantially 
among Scarlet, Red and Pink Star species. 
From the analysis in chapter 10 it is clear that the timber royalties have, at least since 1986 and probably 
also before that, stayed far below the industry's actual willingness to pay for standing timber. In section 
7.4.1 it was documented that the extraction of Scarlet and Red Star species has remained at a high and 
fairly constant level during the period 1986-96. It may, therefore, be concluded that: 
• 
Timber royalties have not created incentives for the industry to reduce the harvest of over-
exploited species and/or to increase the harvest of "under-utilised" species; 
• 
considered in isolation, timber royalties have not generated the forest revenue they could. 
Timber royalties and other pre-1999 forest taxes and fees have clearly not compensated much for the 
suppressing effect that log export bans have had on domestic log prices. In combination, the log export 
bans and low timber royalties have probably promoted the establishment of new log processing 
domestic companies, but they have definitely not created strong incentives for making these very 
123

--- Page 148 ---

efficient. Compared with a hypothetical log export, Ghana's export of processed wood represented an 
export revenue foregone of some DM 40 million in 1996 alone. Furthermore, it seems very likely that 
the combination oflow royalties and the policy of banning log exports of an increasing number of, and 
eventually all, species has contributed to create the apparent over-capacity ofthe processing industry. 
The distributional effects of timber royalties have not been particularly positive either. Since the timber 
industry could have paid more than it did (at least officially) for standing timber, the pre-1999 level of 
forest taxes have worked as a net transfer of funds from society to the timber industry and to an 
unknown number of corrupt officials with whom the industry is likely to have shared some of its potential 
windfall profits. 
The communities who actually own the land from which timber has been extracted also lost out to the 
industry. This special issue is analysed further in section 12.1. At this stage it may be mentioned that the 
land owners have in many cases not even received the share of the timber royalties which they were 
legally entitled to. Even if this had been the case the fact that royalties have been way below the 
industry's actual willingness to pay for standing timber represents an allocation of available funds from 
the land owners to the industry. 
The new stumpage fees seem to address a lot of the shortcomings that characterised the previous royalty 
rates. The positive issues are that: 
• 
The taxation level almost equals the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber; 
• 
the substantial difference in stumpage fee rates between species should serve as an incentive to 
divert harvest away from over-exploited species towards species which are harvested below their 
AACs. 
• 
the industry gets clear incentives to maximise profits through more efficient processing rather than 
through informal payments, since the room for such payments is significantly reduced. 
Local communities' share of the timber revenue may also be increased through the stumpage fee system 
as more revenue is collected from the industry. Yet, as mentioned above the actual share of revenue 
from forest reserves to local communities will first and foremost depend on how much the Forestry 
Department is allowed to retain to cover its running costs. 
11.1.2 Other Forest Taxes and Fees Directly Related to the Timber Harvest 
In the group of other forest taxes and fees some are essentially payments for services that are directly 
related to the harvest and extraction of timber. Others may be considered actual taxes because they 
either do not relate to a particular service or because they are higher than the costs of providing the 
service they relate to (see also chapter 9). 
Fees that are payments for direct services include: 
• 
Conveyance certificates; 
• 
concession preparation fee; 
• 
"sale" of Log Measurement Certificates. 
124

--- Page 149 ---

It may be debatable whether conveyance certificates represent a service to the timber industry. Yet, 
they are considered a necessary component to control illegal felling and transport of logs. Furthermore, 
the size of the fee (less than DM 0.65 per m3 in 1999 prices) is hardly higher than the costs of 
administration. Concession preparation fees (DM 322 per concession in 1999 prices) and "sale" of Log 
Measurement Certificates (¢ 200 or DM 0.13 per log in 1999 prices) must be considered service 
charges that at best cover the costs of their administration. 
Fees that may be considered as taxation include: 
• 
Silvicultural fee; 
• 
concession/contract area rent; 
• 
fee for renewal of property marks. 
In theory, silvicultural fees should have been a service charge but, in reality, no silvicultural services have 
been provided by the Forestry Department on concessions where logging has taken place. The size of 
the fee has also been pathetically small (less than DM 0.05 per ha in 1997 prices), which hardly covered 
the costs of collection, and in 1998 it was finally abolished (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 
1998; 42). Concession rent (DM 0.90 per ha per year in 1997 prices) which was substituted by 
contract area rents in December 1998 (DM 0.77 per ha per year on-reserves and DM 0.65 per ha per 
year off-reserves in 1999 prices) as well as the fee for renewal of property marks (DM 258 per year 
in 1999 prices) probably cover(ed) more than the costs of collection. 
Although there are relatively many forest taxes and fees additional to timber royalties their combined 
impact on logging profitability is and have not been substantial. In chapter 10 it was estimated that, in 
1996 prices, the combined value of (i) concession rent, (ii) silvicultural fees, (iii) renewal of property 
marks and (iv) conveyance certificates was approximately DM 5.58 per m3• For comparison the 
average 1996 royalty values for Scarlet, Red and Pink Star species were DM 7.34,5.51 and 4.00 per 
m3, respectively. In 1999 prices the combined value of contract area rents and property mark renewals 
is approximately DM 2.95 per m3 and the average stumpage fees are approximately DM 40 per m3 for 
Scarlet Star species, DM 22 per m3 for Red Star species and DM 9 per m3 for Pink Star species (see 
appendix 12). 
Prior to 1999, the group of forest fees and taxes other than timber royalties have, in other words, not 
to any significant degree mopped up the substantial potential forest fee revenue represented by the 
difference between royalties and the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber. Accordingly, a 
critique similar to that on timber royalties applies to the pre-1999 package of other forest fees and taxes 
that are directly related to the timber harvest. In the post-1999 forest fee package, forest taxes and fees 
other than stumpage fees do not represent a high value per m3, but this has been more than offset by the 
stumpage fees which are much higher than the previous 
royalties. 
11.1.3 Log Export Bans 
The log export bans have been subject to much debate. This is not surprising since the bans are obvious 
ly a great benefit to saw mills and other log processing industries, while they have successively barred 
logging companies from the attractive log export market (cf. table 10.3). However, control of the timber 
125

--- Page 150 ---

exploitation rather than protection of the domestic wood industry has often been used officially to justify 
the bans (FRMP, 1988; 18). 
The World Bank did not readily accept that log export bans was an appropriate policy instrument to 
promote further processing and protect the timber resource in Ghana. It was, therefore, made a 
condition forthe Forest Resource Management Project's second trance release ofloan money that a 
study of this issue should form the basis for an agreed action plan between the Bank and the 
Government of Ghana on log export bans and possible alternative measures (FRMP, 1988; 56). This 
study was made by General Woods (1992), which came out againstthe bans. The analysis was that, 
while reducing the harvest of banned species, the effect of the bans in terms of inducing further domestic 
processing was negligible (General Woods, 1992; WD no. 3; 43-52). 
lIED (1993; 110-120) re-analysed the effects of log export bans and concluded that in terms of 
resource conservation: 
• 
Log export bans should be accompanied with supply-side measures; 
• 
in the short term, log export bans have reduced the harvest of banned species; 
• 
in the longer term the bans have been less effective because they reduce the domestic1og price 
of the banned species, leading to increased domestic consumption and, in combination with the 
low forest taxes and fees, wasteful processing for export. 
From a theoretical point of view, these conclusions are not surprising. Naturally, it will take some time 
for the timber industry to switch over from a lucrative log export (most log processing industries also 
exported unprocessed logs) to mainly export processed wood, particularly lumber. 
Species specific data on the extraction of timber has only been available since 1986, which precludes 
a complete analysis of the effectofthe 1979 log export ban. However, figure 11.1 clearly shows the 
short-and longer-term effect of the 1988 and 1993 bans. 
800,000 ..,---,---...,--..,.--..,----,---...,--1 
.....
