Figure 11.1 Total recorded extraction of species subject to log export bans 1986-96 
Source: FPIB LMC Totals (1986-96) 
126

--- Page 151 ---

The following observations may be made from figure 11.1: 
• 
The 1988 log export ban appears to have reduced the annual extraction of the affected species 
by almost 70% in the first year, but already in 1994 the annual extraction was back at the pre-ban 
level; 
• 
the lifting of the 1988 log export ban for Of ram in 1990 resulted in a rapidly increasing extraction 
of this species, which suggests that the continued ban on other 1988 banned species has avoided 
an even bigger increase in their level of extraction than the case has been; 
• 
the 1993, ban on Wawa log exports had some reducing effect on the extraction level in the first 
year (the was effectuated during 1993), but already in 1995 the extraction level was on the rise 
again; 
• 
the complete log export ban in 1995 dramatically reduced the extraction level of hitherto un-
banned species, but not to the level before the 1993 log export boom, and from 1995 the 
extraction of these species shows a slight increase; 
• 
the extraction of 1979 banned species culminated in 1990 after which there has been a steady 
decline in the extraction level for this group. 
Accordingly, the graphs in figure 11.1 support the conclusion that log export bans have only had a 
temporary resource conserving effect. The falling trend, since 1990, in extraction of 1979 banned 
species is hardly a long-term resource conserving effect ofthe ban. On the contrary, it probably shows 
the failure of the ban as a long-term harvest control measure. Most of the 1979 banned species belong 
to the group of Scarlet Star species and the falling extraction of these species since 1990 is likely to 
reflect stricter on-reserve harvest control combined with a decreasing availability of these species off-
reserves (cf. section 7.4.1). Furthermore, figure 8.3 indicates that, since 1992, the export proportion 
of extracted Scarlet Star species has gone up, which suggests that species scarcity is a factor 
contributing to the decreasing extraction of Scarlet Star and therefore 1979 banned species. 
In conclusion, the log export bans have failed miserably as long- and even medium-term resource 
conserving measures. In combination with the pre-I 999 low forest taxes and fees, the log export bans 
have suppressed domestic log prices which has probably induced establishment of new log processing 
firms, but not produced incentives for efficient processing. In other words, the log export bans and low 
forest taxes and fees have encouraged inefficiency and created an over-capacity in the log processing 
industry. 
Apart from subsidising domestic processing at the expense of log exporters the distributive effects af 
log export bans are similar to those created by low forest fees and taxes. People who are employed in 
the wood industry benefit from this situation, but only temporarily, since the over-exploitation of many 
species is likely to be a long-term cost for everybody, not least for the wood industry. 
Based on the above analyses and the agreement with the World Bank it might appear strange that the 
Government of Ghana got away with banning log exports ofWawa in 1993 and subsequently all log 
exports in 1995. Yet, the tum of events during 1993-94 happened so fast that, for political reasons, it 
must have been impossible for the Bank to insist that log export bans should be avoided. 
During 1993, log exports boomed tremendously and it soon became clear that concerns about the 
resource would eventually force the government to reduce the timber exploitation somehow. For 
127

--- Page 152 ---

example, during the presentation of the TIED (1993) report, where senior staff from the Forestry 
Department and the Ministry of Lands and Forestry participated, the then Senior Forester in ODA, 
John Hudson said the massively increasing timber harvest made "people in London" wonder whether 
Ghana had completely lost control with its logging sector (Personal observation, 1993). 
A complete log export ban must, therefore, have been easy to justify. Essentially, it was also the only 
measure that could be implemented effectively and quickly. In addition, the strong domestic lobby in 
favour of log export bans would be pleased. 
Ironically, the log processing companies significantly contributed to create concems over the timber 
harvest going berserk because many mills, during this period, threw all their resources into harvest and 
export oflogs (personal information, numerous sawmill owners). Saw millers simply found themselves 
in a win-win-win situation where they could (i) eam quick profits, (ii) clear old debts and (iii) provoke 
a complete log export ban which would be in their long-term interest. An opportunity too good to let 
go. 
In practice, it is unlikely that the log export ban will ever be lifted even though it may, theoretically, be 
argued that log export levies would be a better overall policy option. This would, in contrast to the ban, 
generate govemment revenue and induce more efficient domestic processing since overseas log buyers 
would be allowed to compete with domestic mills for logs. The interests already vested in the complete 
log export ban are, however, likely to be politically very strong. They may also point to the employment 
generated by further domestic processing and not least to the fact that many other tropical countries 
have also banned log exports entirely. 
The new stumpage fees are likely to form strong incentives for more efficient processing which will offset 
some ofthe negative impacts oflog export ban. Yet, stumpage fees should be gradually increased. 
Otherwise the development of the industry will slow down again and the processing efficiency will 
remain below the standard of developed countries' wood industries. The difference between domestic 
and FOB log prices will be a good indicator for how much stumpage fees could be further increased. 
11.1.4 Export Levies 
With the Trees and Timber (Amendment) Act (1994) log export levies between 10% and 30% were 
introduced for 25 species and export levies on air-dried lumber between 10% and 30% were 
introduced for nine species. However, it is somewhat unclear when this Act was actually implemented. 
No log export levies have ever been collected before the complete 1995 ban on log exports, and a 
circular from FPIB addressed to all saw millers and all commercial banks shows that the levies on air-
dried lumber took effect from January 1, 1997 (FPIB, 1997). 
As this study does not have the export figures from 1997 onwards it is not possible to directly assess 
the effect of export levies on air-dried lumber. As a measure to generate revenue at the expense on 
windfall profits in the saw milling sector and to induce further processing (for which kiln-drying is 
technically essential) it seems appropriate. Compared with other forest fees and taxes, export levies are 
also fairly easy and cheap to collect because the system of collecting the 2 % of FOB value export levy 
to finance the FPIB is already in place. 
128

--- Page 153 ---

The resource conserving effect of export levies is partially positive because all the nine affected species 
are over-exploited and belong to the group of Scarlet Stars. However, since the levy does not cover 
all over-exploited species, this effect might be diluted by an increased pressure on other already over-
exploited species. Another issue in this respect is that the levy is likely primarily to promote efficient kiln-
drying and only to some degree more efficient conversion of logs to lumber. From a resource 
conservation viewpoint the levy is, therefore, not as desirable as other demand-side measures that 
directly increase the "price" on standing timber. 
The distributional effects of export levies are positive in the sense that the government revenue is 
increased. Yet, in relation to the land-owning communities the effect is negative because lumber exports 
cannot, in practice, be traced back to the stump, which would be necessary to calculate the revenue 
share that different land-owning communities should rightfully receive. In this respect demand-side 
measures that directly increase the "price" on standing timber are more equitable. 
11.1.5 General Conclusion on Demand-Side Measures 
Prior to 1999 the applied demand-side measures undermined rather than supported sustainable forest 
management. Log export bans and low forest fees and taxes made standing timber a cheap resource. 
This has promoted (i) over-exploitation of the commercially most valuable species, (ii) wasteful 
processing and (iii) creation of an over-capacity in the wood industry. Furthermore, considerable sums 
in terms of potential revenue for the Government and local communities were foregone. 
With the 1999 package of stumpage fees and contract area rents introduced by the Timber Resources 
Management Regulations (1998) it seems that the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber will 
actually be fully taxed, at least for Scarlet and Red Star species. For Pink Star species there still seems 
to be some additional revenue available, but this may be a justifiable way to promote species which are 
harvested below their AACs. 
On the whole, the new forest fees and taxes, therefore, appear appropriate and mark an important shift 
in policy to use demand-side measures rationally in the attempt to make sustainable forest management 
a reality. Yet, stumpage fees should be gradually increased to promote development of the wood 
industry and to increase revenues. As long as domestic log prices are below FOB log prices there will 
be room for improvements. 
11.2 Supply-Side Measures 
On-reserves supply-side measures encompass the following: 
• 
A 100% stock survey and enumeration of FIP class 1 trees above 50 cm DBH in each 
compartment; 
• 
in principle, a 10% check survey of all stock surveyed compartments; 
• 
calculation and identification of species specific yield per compartment, based on the stock survey 
results38; 
• 
felling checks, marking of stumps and extracted logs and filling out log information forms (since 
Aug. 1995) supervised/performed by a Forest Guard; 
38 
See section 6.3.2 for further details. 
129

--- Page 154 ---

• 
issuing of a conveyance certificate for each truck load oflogs by the District Forest Officer (since 
Aug. 1995); 
• 
FPIB road check -points where the truck driver must show his conveyance certificate to receive 
the necessary log measurement certificates for further transport. 
Off-reserves the only supply-side measure was until August, 1995 minimum felling diameters. From 
1995, when the "Interim Procedures for Felling Trees Off-reserve" came into effect the following steps 
should be followed (FD, 1995b; 1): 
• 
• 
• 
• 
• 
Pre-felling inspection of trees by the applicant, community representatives and District Forest 
Office representatives. On this basis the District Forest Officer indicates which trees may be 
felled; 
a felling permit will, depending on the application, be issued by the District Forest Officer, the 
Regional Forest Officer or the Chief Conservator of Forests; 
a post-felling inspection by a FD officer, preferably accompanied by community representatives, 
must verify proper marking of the stumps and a log information form must be filled out; 
a conveyance certificate valid for three days is issued by the District Forest Officer for every 
truck load; 
FPIB road check-points where the truck driver must show his conveyance certificate to receive 
the necessary log measurement certificates for further transport. 
Presumably, the above mentioned first three points will also apply for future timber utilisation permits 
(see section 12.3.5). For future off-reserve timber utilisation contracts the pre- and post-felling 
inspections of each tree is not likely to be continued, as other provisions in the Timber Resources 
Management Regulations (1998; 1-7) and the Timber Resources Management Act (1997; 4 (2a-d» 
protectthe rights of farmers and land owners (see sections 12.3.6 and 12.3.7). Conveyance certificates 
will still have to be issued for all timber (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 24). In 
effect, minimum felling diameters are, therefore, also in the future likely to be the only off-reserve supply-
side measure once rights to standing timber have been allocated. 
11.2.1 On-Reserve Supply-Side Measures 
Although sustained yield management of reserved forests rests entirely on an effective implementation 
of yield regulation, this issue has received surprisingly little attention. Alder (1996; 11) concludes that 
in spite of the vast inputs of ODA to the Forestry Department, Planning Branch its capacity in forest 
management planning is very weak, and there is virtually no effective monitoring. 
Essentially, two requirements must be fulfilled to properly implement yield regulation on-reserves. First, 
accurate stock surveys and yield selection must be carried out. Second, felling checks must ensure that 
only trees within the yield are felled and that other rules for environmentally sound logging are followed. 
As mentioned above there is no effective system in place to monitor how well these steps function in 
practice. Yet, the few studies available on the issue indicate that this is an area where improvements are 
urgently needed. 
Based on a check-survey and careful yield calculation of35 compartments in 17 districts Kakrada and 
Maginnis (1992; 12) concluded that of all enumerated stems 42% were over-estimated into a higher 
diameter-class, 10% were under-estimated into a lower diameter-class and 12% were under-sized 
130

--- Page 155 ---

(below their minimum DBH) yet promoted into a diameter-class above the felling limit. On average, the 
result was that 20% of all stems designated as suitable for exploitation (above the minimumDBH) were, 
in fact, under-sized. For the most valuable species this figure could be as high as 50%. ill addition, stock 
survey data had rarely been verified through a 10% check survey and no felling checks were 
done. 
ill a study undertaken by the Forest Research illstitute of Ghana (FORIG) on exploitation of under-
sized trees, a total of 557 trees marked as originating from forest reserves were re-measured at different 
FPffi road check-points FORIG (1994). The result of this exercise is summarised in table 11.2.

ill addition to the data presented in table 11.3 it should be mentioned that only three stumps (3%) 
represented trees felled outside the approved yield. All other stumps were, through stock survey 
numbers carved in the bark, positively identified as belonging to trees in the approved yields, even 
though many of these (44%) were, in fact, under-sized. 
131

--- Page 156 ---

The relatively small number of stumps observed in table 11.3 is obviously a statistical weakness. 
However, the results are quite similar to those of Kakrada and Maginnis (1992), which must build on 
a large sample (they do not state the total number of measured). Accordingly, it seems safe to conclude 
that the general tendency in stock surveys to over-estimate the DBH of individual trees is so pronounced 
that it cannot be explained (away) by normal sampling errors and uncertainties associated with 
measuring the diameters. 
The results presented in tables 11.3 and 11.2 are also quite similar. Approximately 60% of the extracted 
Scarlet Star species were, in fact, under-sized. For Red Star species the fraction of extracted under-
sized trees appears to have been between 20% and 35% and 14% to 23% of Pink Star species were 
under-sized. The data in table 11.2 is obviously more statistically reliable than the data in table 11.3. 
Yet, both tables strongly suggest that particularly Scarlet Star species have been felled below their 
minimum DBH. 
In conclusion, the analysis by Alder (1996) combined with the above mentioned technical results 
indicate that the supply-side measures to control timber exploitation on-reserves have not functioned 
satisfactorily and clear indications of improvements are still awaited. Yield selection is obscured by 
over-estimated diameters, and checks at the stump and on the road are not efficient. As a result, under-
sized trees and a higher proportion than intended by the interim yield allocation formula of trees above 
their felling limit have been extracted. This is probably still going on. Overall, the efficiency of on-reserve 
supply-side measures is indicated by the on-reserve LMC totals which, for 1996, clearly show that 
most Scarlet and some Red Star species are being exploited above their AACs (cf. section 7.5). 
FORIG (1994; 16) points to inadequate field staff and high demand for species with limited stocking 
as possible reasons for the documented harvest of under-sized trees. Furthermore, it is noted that lack 
of institutional co-operation between the Forestry Department and FPIB has a tendency to let cases 
on under-sized logs detected at FPIB check-points end up in the files. 
The reasons behind the apparently poor quality of stock surveys are according to Kakrada and 
Maginnis (1992), that the stock survey teams: 
• 
Rarely receive all their travel and transport allowances 
• 
often lack adequate diameter measurement equipment, especially for buttressed trees 
• 
have poor camping, cooking and transportation equipment 
• 
lack proper supervision in the field. 
The above points of Kakrada and Maginnis (1992) are clearly valid but fail to explain the pronounced 
tendency of tree diameters to be over- rather than under-estimated during stock surveys. FORIG 
(1994) undoubtedly hits the spot in assuming that the market demand is responsible for exploitation of 
under-sized trees (what else could it be?), but does not look into how this market affects the yield 
selection and harvest control. 
Interviews with a number of people employed by logging companies and owners oflogging companies 
have added the missing link. After stock surveys and yield allocations are correctly made, the District 
Forest Officer negotiates with the concessionaire over the cost of enhancing (manipulating) the yield by 
promoting individual trees to a higher diameter class. 
132

--- Page 157 ---

This is a simple exercise which is supposed to be widespread. It explains why tree diameters are being 
over- rather than under-estimated and why many under-sized trees are actually allocated in the yield. 
In addition, such cheating is clearly very attractive for loggers since it provides some security against 
post-logging stump and road checks. Even if a tree is found under-sized it may be documented that it 
was in the approved yield. In the absence of independent check surveys the District Forest Officer is 
also fairly well secured from exposure since compromising results of post-logging checks can always 
be blamed on stock survey teams. 
Interestingly, many loggers who told about or admitted to this activity also expressed concems over its 
long-term effect. One said that the Forestry Department staff is easy to bribe because their salaries are 
so low, and in the end he feared the forest would exist on paper only. In fact, many loggers regretted 
the unfortunate state of affairs and referred to the "good old days" when the Forestry Department was 
totally free of corruption, and malpractices of any kind by any logger were swiftly and effectively 
stopped. However, the deteriorating control system has promoted selfish and irresponsible behaviour 
among loggers, because no one can trust that an under-sized tree left today would actually survive till 
the next felling cycle. 
Post-logging checks may, as here demonstrated, reveal that under-sized trees are extracted from forest 
reserves. If done atthe stump they may also verify whether the under-sized trees were included in the 
approved yield. Yet, post-logging checks will not be an efficient means to prevent over-exploitation if 
they are only carried out after a compartment has been logged and not during the operation. In theory, 
a concessionaire should not be allowed to open a new compartment for logging before a post-logging 
check has verified that previous compartments have been logged according to the rules. 
However, a compartment may be logged during a period of three years before it has to be closed. 
Commercially, this is reasonable as it may take time to find markets for the available species, but 
checking three year old stumps is almost impossible. It is our experience that even after two years the 
bark of stumps is falling off and paint is washed away making identification impossible. After three years 
it would also be difficult to establish whether an offence has been committed by the District Forest 
Officer or the Stock Survey Team, should stumps from under-sized trees be identified as allocated in 
the yield. Furthermore, vines and other pioneer species can after two to three years make a 
compartment totally inaccessible for post-logging check teams. 
Improved harvest control on-reserves must rely on correct selection of the yield based on honest and 
thorough stock surveys. To this end Kakrada and Maginnis (1992; 26) recommend that stock surveys 
in general are given higher priority by: 
• 
Making all supervisory staff accountable for the standard of work produced 
• 
paying stock survey teams their entitlements 
• 
providing better supervision, tools and camping equipment to stock survey teams 
• 
ensuring that check surveys are always carried out 
• 
requesting FPIB to immediately furnish the Forestry Department with details on detected under-
sized logs 
• 
examining the scope for felling checks. 
133

--- Page 158 ---

All of these recommendations, not least the first point, are relevant. FORIG (1994; 24) goes a bit further 
and recommends that FPIB should be given legal provision to impound logs suspected to stem from 
under-sized trees pending further investigation. Sometimes the bottom logs of under-sized trees are 
marked as number two logs of larger trees that never existed, and it will take some time to verify 
whether this is the case. 
However, no system can be better than the people implementing it. If there are no rewards for good 
performance (at least a decent salary) and/or punishments for bad performance (risk of fines or getting 
sacked), control and check systems of any kind are not likely to function very well, if at all. The 
development within Forestry Department over the past 20 years or so is an obvious example. 
The rather severe punishments stipulated in the Timber Resources Management Act (1997; 17) and the 
Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998; 41) for illegal felling of timber may deter officials 
from involving themselves in such activities, as the rules do now also apply for officials who fail to 
perform hisJher duty of controlling that timber is felled according to the rules (see section 9.1.5). Positive 
incentives for officials to keep high standards are not yet established. 
11.2.2 Off-Reserve Supply-Side Measures 
Until August, 1995 the only off-reserve supply-side measure was the species specific minimum felling 
diameters. Inspections of felling sites or stump checks were rarely carried out by the Lands Commission, 
which until 1994 was responsible for collection of royalties off-reserves (cf. section 9.1.1). 
In the study of FORIG (1994) 3,463 trees marked as originating from areas outside forest reserves 
were re-measured. The summary result is shown in table 11.4. 
Table 11.4 Summary results of re-measuring logs from off-reserve areas 
Species Group 
Scarlet Stars 
Red Stars 
Pink Stars 
TOTAL 
Source: 
FORIG (1994) 
Observed

(no.)

from off-reserve areas 
(no.)

7.84

45.60 
The table shows that a very high percentage of especially Scarlet Star species were under-sized. The 
high number of trees checked supports that this result gives a reliable picture of the general 
situation. 
Loggers gave two main reasons for felling under-sized trees. One was that big trees had more technical 
defects. The other was that if under-sized trees were left off-reserves, farmers would destroy 
them. 
134

--- Page 159 ---

The second objective of the FORIO study was to examine if defects are more abundant in large than 
in small diameter trees. On this part of the study the results showed that no significant relationship 
between diameter and the incidence of defects could be proved (FORIG, 1994; 20). In other words, 
the perception of loggers that defects were more abundant in trees of large diameter cannot be 
supported and is probably false. 
That farmers would destroy off-reserve timber trees left behind by logging companies is, on the other 
hand, likely to be true in many cases. On several occasions we have seen timber trees being deliberately 
destroyed by farmers because they saw them as potential risks rather than potential sources of income. 
The reason being that farmers rarely received any payments for trees being felled on their farms and 
logging companies rarely payed compensation for damage on crops caused by felling and extraction of 
timber on farmland (see section 12.2). 
The "Interim Procedures for Felling Trees Off-Reserve" attempted to rectify this by specifying that a 
person who cultivates the land (not the land owner) on which a timber tree grows had the right to stop 
the tree from being felled, regardless of the person applying for the tree to be felled holds a concession 
covering the area (PD, 1995b; 3). The idea was to give farmers authority to negotiate a price over and 
above royalties and other forest taxes for the felling of timber trees on the land they cultivate. 
The degree to which this system has benefitted farmers is unclear. Anecdotal information indicate that 
a few loggers were having difficulties with farmers who resisted felling of trees. Yet, during a field trip 
in April, 1997 we could observe that farmers were not significantly empowered by the new rules. 
Informal information from people involved in developing the "Interim Procedures for Felling Trees Off-
Reserve" also confirmed that little had actually changed on the ground. 
The problem was that farmers did not know of their "new" rights. No information campaign had been 
launched to alleviate this, and it appeared that the District Forest Officers did not see it as their duty to 
inform local people about their rights to refuse felling of timber trees in their farms. 
The lack of institutional support to the alleged rights of farmers to veto tree felling on land they cultivated 
may probably be explained by the fact that such rights could be legally challenged in cases where 
farmers did not cultivate cocoa. The Economic Plants Protection Decree (1979; 4) prevents the felling 
of trees on farms where "specified" plants are cultivated, and specified plants are defined as cocoa only 
(Economic Plants Protection Decree, 1979; 7). 
The "Interim Procedures for Felling Trees Off-Reserve" were primarily intended to control the chaotic 
situation of illegal and speculative timber felling off-reserves during the log export boom in 1993-94 (PD, 
1994a; I). They were considered necessary interim measures to be implemented before "the 
inadequacies and deficiencies in the regulatory system could be systematically tackled" (PD, 1994a; 2). 
Yet, the complete log export ban, which also came into force in mid 1995, was probably more effective 
in terms of controlling the off-reserve timber harvest. 
Presumably the Timber Resources Management Act (1997) and the Timber Resources Management 
Regulations (1998) should be considered part of an attempt to address some of the previous regulatory 
system's shortcomings. Farmers are now legally entitled to veto felling oftrees on land they cultivate, 
irrespective of the agricultural crops (Timber Resources Management Act, 1997; 4 (2d)). This should 
135

--- Page 160 ---

protect the individual farmer from suffering undue losses, but it is doubtful whether adequate incentives 
have been created for farmers to actually protect and nurse naturally occurring trees on land they 
cultivate (see sections 12.2.1 and 12.3.6). 
11.3 Summary Assessment of Timber Harvest Regulations 
Overall, the exploitation oftimber has, during the period 1986-93, resulted in a net loss forthe average 
Ghanaian taxpayerlcitizen. Possibly this was also the case in 1994, but since 1995 forest taxes and fees 
collected have probably exceeded the costs of forest management by several million DM. 
Yet, substantial revenues have been foregone by allowing inflation to erode old royalty debts and by the 
fact that, prior to 1999, forest fees and taxes have not been charged according to the industry's 
Willingness to pay for standing timber. Forest fees and taxes even from forest reserves alone could easily 
finance the costs of sustainable or sustained yield forest management iftimber exploitation was taxed 
according to the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber. This conclusion rests on fairly rough 
cost and, to a lesser degree, potential revenue estimates, but the gap of more that DM 4 million between 
the two sends a clear message. It can be done. 
The new stumpage fee system would initially only generate around DM 8.6 million, leaving a surplus of 
approximately DM 1.1 million, from forest reserves if the harvest is held within the AAC. While the 
potential revenue appears to be somewhat higher, the (long awaited) abolishment of the low timber 
royalties does mark an important shift in policy towards securing that the costs of wise forest 
management may actually be covered by forest revenues. An outstanding issue in this respect is, 
however, whether it would be politically acceptable to spend almost 90% of the revenue from forest 
reserves to finance the Forestry Department (or a future Forestry Service), leaving only 10% to local 
communities (see section 12.1.2 for further analyses). 
11.3.1 Specific Demand-Side Measures 
Timber royalties have not created incentives for the industry to reduce the harvest of over-exploited 
species and/or to increase the harvest of "under-utilised" species. Considered in isolation, timber 
royalties have not generated the forest revenue they could. In combination with other forest taxes and 
fees, timber royalties have clearly not compensated much for the suppressing effect that log export bans 
have had on domestic log prices. 
In combination, the log export bans and low timber royalties have probably promoted the esqtblishment 
of new log processing domestic companies, but they have definitely not created strong incentives for 
making these very efficient. Compared with a hypothetical log export, Ghana's export of processed 
wood represented an export revenue foregone of some DM 40 million in 1996 alone. Furthermore, 
it seems very likely that the combination oflow royalties and the policy of banning log exports of an 
increasing number of, and eventually all, species has contributed to create the apparent over-capacity 
of the processing industry. 
The distributional effects of the low timber royalties and other forest taxes and fees have not been 
particularly positive either. Since the timber industry could have paid more than it did (at least officially) 
for standing timber, the pre-1999 level of forest taxes worked as a net transfer of funds from the 
average Ghanaian taxpayer to the timber industry and to an unknown number of corrupt officials. The 
136

--- Page 161 ---

communities who actually own the land on which timber is felled and extracted have also lost out to the 
industry. 
The new stumpage fees seem to address a lot of the shortcomings that characterised the previous royalty 
rates. The positive issues are that: 
• 
The taxation level almost equals the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber 
• 
the substantial difference in stumpage fee rates between species should serve as an incentive to 
divert harvest away from over-exploited species towards species which are harvested below their 
AACs 
• 
the industry gets clear incentives to maximise profits through more efficient processing rather than 
through informal payments, since the room for such payments is significantly reduced. 
Local communities' share of the timber revenue may also be increased by the stumpage fee system as 
more revenue is collected from the industry. Yet, as mentioned above, the actual share of revenue from 
forest reserves going to local communities will first and foremost depend on how much is retained to 
finance the Forestry Department. 
Log export bans have failed miserably as long- and even medium-term resource conserving measures. 
The harvest of banned species has only fallen in a few years after the bans, and then the demand has 
picked up again. Presumably, this reflects the industry's fairly quick ability to shift from export oflogs 
to export of processed wood, especially lumber. 
Apart from subsidising domestic processing at the expense of logging companies, which have 
successively lost access to export markets, the distributive effects aflog export bans are similar to those 
created by the pre-1999 low forest fees and taxes. People who are employed in the wood industry 
benefit from this situation, but only temporarily, since the over-exploitation of many species is likely to 
be a long-term cost for everybody, not least for the wood industry. 
The export levy on air-dried lumber has a partially positive resource conserving effect because all the 
nine affected species are over-exploited and belong to the group of Scarlet Stars. However, since the 
levy does not cover all over-exploited species, this effect might be partly offset by an increased pressure 
on other already over-exploited species. Furthermore, the levy is likely primarily to promote efficient 
kiln-drying and only to some degree efficient conversion of logs to lumber. From a resource 
conservation viewpoint the levy is, therefore, not as desirable as other demand-side measures that 
directly increase the "price" of standing timber. 
The distributional effects of export levies are positive in the sense that government revenue is increased. 
Yet, in relation to the land-owning communities the effect is negative because lumber exports cannot, 
in practice, be traced back to the stump, which would be necessary to calculate the revenue share that 
different land-owning communities should rightfully receive. ill this respect demand-side measures which 
directly increase the "price" of standing timber are more equitable. 
With the 1999 package of stumpage fees and contract area rents introduced by the Timber Resources 
Management Regulations (1998) it seems that the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber will 
actually be fully taxed at least for Scarlet and Red Star species. For Pink Star species there still seems 
137

--- Page 162 ---

to be some additional revenue available, but this may be a justifiable way to promote species which are 
harvested below their AACs. 
On the whole, the new forest fees and taxes, therefore, appear appropriate and mark an important shift 
in policy to use demand-side measures rationally in the attempt to promote sustainable forest 
management. Yet, stumpage fees should be gradually increased to upgrade the wood industry and to 
increase revenues. As long as domestic log prices are below FOB log prices there will be room for 
improvements. 
11.3.2 Supply-Side Measures 
Supply-side measures to control timber exploitation on-reserves do not appear to function satisfactorily. 
Collusion between loggers and Forestry Department staff obscures yield selection through deliberate 
over-estimation oftree diameters. Checks at the stump and on the road are either not efficient or not 
performed. As a consequence, under-sized trees and a higher proportion, than silviculturally intended, 
of trees above their felling limit is extracted. The overall result is illustrated by the on-reserve lMC totals 
for 1996, which clearly show that most Scarlet and some Red Star species are being exploited above 
their AACs. 
Off-reserves control with the timber exploitation has traditionally been very weak and excessive felling 
of under-sized trees is documented to have happened. The "Interim Procedures for Felling Trees Off-
Reserve" (PD, I 995b ) and subsequently the rules stipulated in the Timber Resources Management Act 
(1997) and the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998) seek to establish control by the 
Forestry Department of timber exploitation off-reserves. In addition, farmers' rights to oppose felling 
of trees on land they cultivate have been strengthened. This is likely to slow the off-reserve timber 
exploitation, but it is questionable whether farmers are given sufficient incentives to nurse naturally 
occurring trees on farmland (see section 12.3.7). 
138

--- Page 163 ---

12 Rights to Timber and Benefits from Timber Exploitation 
The distribution of rights to timber and to benefits brought about by timber harvest is one of the most 
critical forest policy matters in Ghana. Rights over land are closely linked with rights to trees and timber, 
and the issue of promoting sustainable forest management or sustained yield timber production may be 
divided into the following main points: 
• 
Rights to land, timber, trees and revenue on-reserves; 
• 
rights to land, timber, trees and revenue off-reserves; 
• 
principles for allocation of rights to standing timber on- and off-reserves. 
These subjects have been guided by an array of legislation that has undergone progressive revision since 
the colonial period. The result has, at least until December 1998, been a successive erosion of 
customary land-owning communities' ("stools" and "skins") rights. This has alienated local communities 
from management of forest reserves and hitherto totally undermined economic incentives for growing 
or tending naturally occurring trees on farms and fallows. 
The winners in terms of rights to manage and collect benefits from exploiting the timber resource have 
been the Lands Commission, the Forestry Department and timber companies. However, as the previous 
chapters clearly show the outcome has been (i) a notorious over-exploitation of the most valuable 
species, (ii) wasteful processing and (iii) a tremendous forest taxation revenue foregone. In other words, 
complete mismanagement. 
12.1 Rights to Land, Trees and Revenue On-Reserves 
The constitution of a forest reserve does not alter the ownership ofthe land (Forest Ordinance (Cap. 
157), 1927; 18 (1)). However, the management right is given to the President. Forest reserves could 
either be managed by the land owners under the direction of the Forestry Department or by the 
Government. In practice, all forest reserves have been managed directly by the Forestry Department, 
not by the land owners. 
Forest reserves were primarily intended to secure environmental services (water supply and protection 
of agricultural land) and to secure the supply of forest products to the surrounding communities (Forest 
Ordinance (Cap. 157), 1927; 4 (4)). Accordingly, the declaration offorest reserves in the absence of 
the land-owners agreement was originally considered exceptional (Cirelli, 1997; 19). Furthermore, the 
owners of a forest reserve were guaranteed to recei ve not less than two thirds of the gross revenue 
accruing from managing the forest (Forest Ordinance (Cap. 157), 1927; 18 (2)). Except for persons 
holding admitted rights (usually established during the process of declaring the forest reserve) it is, in 
forest reserves, prohibited to: 
• 
Fell, uproot, lop, girdle, tap, injure by fire or otherwise to damage any tree or timber; 
• 
establish or cultivate any farm; 
• 
cause any damage by negligence in felling any tree or cutting or removing any timber; 
• 
in any way obstruct the channel of any river, stream, canal or creek; 
• 
hunt, shoot, fish, poison water, or set traps or snares; 
• 
collect, conveyor remove any forest produce for any manufacturing process; 
• 
pasture cattle or permit any cattle to trespass. 
139

--- Page 164 ---

These prohibitions were originally included in the Forest Ordinance (Cap. 157) (1927; 22 (1-10)) and 
they have been repeated in subsequent amendments through the Forest Protection Decree (1974) and 
the Forest Protection (Amendment) Law (1986). Likewise, it has been maintained that the burden of 
proof that any forest produce has not been taken in contravention with the legislation lies upon the 
person in whose possession it is found. 
In 1962 the President's right to intervene and dispose over revenue was enhanced significantly. Any 
stool land may be "vested" in the President if it appears to him that this would be in the public interest 
to do so, and any moneys accruing from such land shall be paid into the "appropriate" account 
(Administration of Lands Act, 1962; 7 (1-2)). Stool land revenue is collected by the Minister and paid 
into a Stool Lands Account (Administration of Lands Act (Act 123), 1962; 17 and 18). However, 
revenue from forest reserves continued to be collected by the Forestry Department and paid into the 
Forest Improvement Fund from which any funds in excess, after costs of timber exploitation and 
silvicultural work have been paid (running costs of the Forestry Department), shall be transferred to the 
Stool Lands Account (Administration of Lands Act, 1962; 17 (3 and 4)). Furthermore, all :'timber" and 
all "trees" on all land including public land, stool land, existing and possible future forest reserves were 
also vested in the President (Concessions Act, 1962; 16). 
The terms "timber" and "tree" are not defined in the Concessions Act (1962). However, the Forest 
Protection Decree (1974; (10)) defines timber as "including trees when they have fallen or have been 
felled, and all wood, whether it has been cut up or fashioned or hollowed out for any purpose or not". 
The Timber Resources Management Act (1997; 20) defines timber as "standing, fallen and felled trees, 
wood for industrial purposes, logs and sawn wood" (no reference is, however, made to the Forest 
Protection Decree (1974) which is still in force). Trees are defined as "including palms, bamboos, 
stumps, brushwood and canes" (Forest Protection Decree, 1974 (10)). That a resource is "vested" in 
the President means that rights over the resource are split into rights of management (legal rights) and 
rights to benefit from resource ownership (benefit rights) (Cirelli, 1997; 19 and FD, 1995a). 
In effect, the 1962 legislation extinguished the rights ofland-owning communities over timber and even 
other "trees" which are used either for subsistence or commercially, ego cane for fumiture production. 
It has been increasingly difficult for people to prove that they hold an admitted right to collect forest 
produce in a forest reserve that was constituted more than 50 years ago, and such rights have become 
lost or fuzzy (FD, 1995a; 19). In addition, the 1962 legislation made1and owners' rightto revenue from 
forest reserves subordinate to the costs of running the Forestry Department. 
During the 1970s, the Forestry Department recognised that, in many instances, there was no revenue 
left for land owners in the Forests Improvement Fund. It was, therefore, administratively decided that 
30% of all royalties should go to the land owners before costs of management were deducted (FD, 
1995a; 17). From being guaranteed minimum 67% of the gross revenue accruing from forest reserves, 
the 1962 legislation gave land owners right to, effectively, first nothing and then, through an 
administrative decision, 30% of the royalties collected on-reserves. 
This is quite a decline, but things were actually much worse, at least for many land-owning communities 
in the Brong Ahafo Region. As of 18 April, 1997 the Forestry Department paid into the Administrator 
of Stool Lands' account Cedis 136,167,898.88 (at that time approximately DM 120,000) for on-
reserve royalties collected in Brong Ahafo Region during the period January 1979 to December 1995 
140

--- Page 165 ---

(FD, 1997). Adjustment for inflation was not made, so the real value of the collected revenue had been 
seriously eroded. Numerous letters to the Forestry Department from disgruntled traditional and elected 
leaders oflocal communities from allover the high forest zone indicate that the problem has prevailed 
a long time and that it has not been confined to Brong Ahafo Region only. Interviews with District Forest 
Officers have confirmed this. 
12.1.1 Future Status of Current and New Forest Reserves 
The Constitution (1992) has changed the legal framework on a number of key forestry related issues. 
In particular, the rights of the President to constitute forest reserve and land-owning communities' rights 
to revenue are affected. 
On stool and skin lands the Constitution (1992; 267 (l)) states "that stool lands vest in the appropriate 
stools on behalf of the subjects of the stool". Expropriation of stool land by the Government in the public 
interest is only allowed upon prompt payment of fair and adequate compensation (Constitution, 1992; 
20 (2,a)). The provisions of the President under the Administration of Lands Act (1962; 7 (1-2)) to 
constitute a forest reserve with or without the consent of the land owners does, therefore, not have any 
basis in the 1992 Constitution (Cirelli, 1997; 32). 
Concerning existing forest reserves, FD (1995a; 18) states thatthe land should not be regarded as stool 
land any more since the Office of the Administrator of Lands Act (1994; 18) defines stool land 
as: 
"Any land or interest in or right over any land controlled by a stool or skin, the head of a 
particular community or the captain of a company, for the benefit ofthe subjects of that stool or 
the members of that community or company". 
The argument being that it is the Forestry Department, not the stools or skins, which controls the use 
and management of forest reserves. Accordingly, forest reserves should be considered "public land" 
as defined in the Constitution (1992; 257 (2)). This would give the Forestry Department full control over 
revenue, timber and other forest produce. Accordingly, it would attain a high degree of freedom to 
negotiate terms and conditions for local people to utilise forest produce and to receive a share of timber 
royalties. It is, however, very likely that this (mis)interpretation of the legislation would be deemed 
unconstitutional (Cirelli 1997; 23). 
New forest reserves can, therefore, only be established with the consent of the land owners unless the 
Government outright expropriates the land. This would, however, require prompt payment of fair and 
adequate compensation from the Government, which it probably cannot afford. Furthermore, existing 
forest reserves must still be considered situated on stool land. 
12.1.2 Rights to Revenue from Forest Reserves 
The administratively decided share of revenue between the Forestry Department and the land owners 
probably became unconstitutional in 1992. Since the ownership ofland was not changed when forest 
reserves were originally declared, they must still be considered located on stool or skin land. Regarding 
such land the Constitution (1992; 267 (6)) stipulates that: 
141

--- Page 166 ---

"Ten per cent of the revenue accruing from stool lands shall be paid into the office of the 
Administrator of Stool Lands to cover administrative expenses; and the remaining revenue shall 
be disbursed in the following proportions: 
a) 
twenty-five per cent to the stool through the traditional authority for the 
maintenance of the stool in keeping with its status 
b) 
twenty per cent to the traditional authority39 
c) 
fifty-five per cent to the District Assembly, within the area of authority of 
which the stool lands are situated." 
Nothing is mentioned about the administrative expenses of the Forestry Department to manage forest 
reserves. The above distribution of stool land revenue is repeated in the Office of the Administrator of 
Lands Act (1994; 8). Furthermore, this Act states that the provisions in the Administrator of Lands Act 
(1962) and the Concessions Act (1962) "shall have effect with such modifications as may be necessary 
to give effect to this Act" (Office of the Administrator of Lands Act, 1994; 17). Accordingly, the legal 
provision of the Forestry Department to retain all revenues from forest reserves necessary to cover its 
running costs fell away with the 1992 Constitution. 
This was a serious problem. Clearly, the Forestry Department could not manage forest reserves if 
reasonable costs of doing so could not be covered. Since the 1992 Constitution was adopted it, 
therefore, appears that the Forestry Department has, dejure, been financed illegally. How this could 
be allowed and how the Office of the Administrator of Lands Act (1994) could be passed without 
specifically considering this rather crucial issue is somewhat mysterious. 
Conserving forest resources in Ghana is, however, difficult to imagine without a kind of Forestry 
Department, and such a body must be financed. A political decision on the matter was, therefore, 
urgently needed (FD, 1995a; 65). Finally in December, 1998 legal basis for funding the Forestry 
Department for timber management services out of revenue arising from timberrevenue was provided 
in the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998; 21 and 26). The amount shaH be decided by 
the Minister for Lands and Forestry in consultation with the Forestry Commission, the Forestry 
Department and the Administrator of Stool Lands. 
It is not specified whether such payments should be deducted from stool land revenue at large or from 
forest reserve revenue only. Furthermore, it is notable that the new legislation does not set a maximum 
limi t for how much may be deducted from stumpage fee revenue to finance the Forestry Department. 
The law says that "stumpagefees represent royalties to the1and owner and charges for the cost of feHed 
timber which provide a basic retum to the landowner and contributes to the cost of forest management 
and timber regulation" (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 21 (2)). On one hand, this 
may allow for financing the Forestry Department through timber related revenue from both on- and off-
reserves if the words "timberregulation" are stretched to cover activities of the Forestry Department 
off-reserves. On the other hand, the word "contribute" should imply that not all revenue may be retained 
to fund the Forestry Department. 
39 
House of Chiefs or any councilor body established or recognised as such 
under customary law (Office of the Administrator of Stool Lands Act, 1994; 
18). 
142

--- Page 167 ---

Unless (i) the revenue from timber exploitation is increased significantly, (ii) the cost of running the 
Forestry Department is reduced significantly or (iii) off-reserve revenue may be used to fund the 
Forestry Department, there will not be much revenue left for local communities who own land on which 
forest reserves are located. As the stumpage fee and contract area rent system has recently been 
introduced, it is not likely that the revenue from forest reserves will be much higher than DM 8.6 million 
annually in the near future (assuming the harvest is held within the AAC). If the annual costs of running 
the Forestry Department is approximately DM 7.5 million, this would leave less than 10% of the revenue 
for the land owners unless other sources may contribute to finance the costs of forest management (see 
section 11.1). Whether this is reasonable is a political decision, but it is quite far from the original 
minimum of 67% defined in the Forest Ordinance (Cap 157) (1927,18 (2)). 
Revenue from forest reserves may in the longer term be higher than DM 8.6 million per year since the 
stumpage fees could be gradually increased, as could the harvest of currently "under-utilised" species. 
Yet, it appears crucial that the Forestry Department will receive funding through other sources than on-
reserve timber revenue to maintain any credibility in relation to local communities who actually own the 
forest reserves. After all the Forest Ordinance (Cap. 157) (1927; 18 (2b)) says that when the 
Government is the manager of forest reserves it should be "for the benefit of the owner or 
owners". 
Naturally, it is not only the land owners' rights, but also the conservation of Ghana's forest resources 
which are at stake. It would seem bizarre if on-reserve timber exploitation could not be controlled 
because the national forest authority cannot be adequately financed. Yet, it is also reasonable that a 
national forest authority is not over-staffed or over-financed at the expense of the land-owning 
communities. In addition, it must be ensured that the forest authority is held accountable for the standard 
of on-reserve forest management. 
12.2 Rights to Land, Trees and Revenue Off-Reserves 
The Concessions Act (1962; 16) vests all timber trees, even off-reserves, in the President. All rights to 
timber trees, whether they stand on cultivated land or not were, therefore, transferred to the company 
which obtained a timber concession in a given area. With reference to the Economic Plants Protection 
Decree (1979; 2 (2)) it was required that the concessionaire must pay compensation for damage of 
crops resulting from felling and extraction of timber. 
In other words, the previous legislation intended to indemnify farmers in connection with exploitation of 
timber, no more. On uncultivated areas farmers are generally in favour oftimberexploitation because 
this clears land for farming. Yet, exploitation oftimber on especially cocoa farms normally leads to a net 
loss for the farmer, because compensation for crop damages was, in general, either inadequate or not 
paid at all (lIED, 1993; 61 and personal observations). 
Logging companies generally want to take all available commercial trees in a concession whether they 
grow on farms or not and, in reality, the few rights offarmers were not protected by the Economic 
Plants Protection Decree (1979). No Government or other sufficiently influential authority seriously 
attempted to enforce the farmers' rights in relation to off-reserve timber exploitation, and an ordinary 
farmer could not, in practice, drag a logging company to court. Instead the idea spread that timber 
companies, if necessary assisted by Government authorities, had every right to exploit timber trees on 
farms (Personal observations). 
143

--- Page 168 ---

Accordingly, farmers did not have any positive incentives for growing or tending naturally occurring trees 
on the land they cultivated, on the contrary. To farmers trees on farmland represented a risk of 
economic losses rather than a potential source of income. 
As at March 1998, the rights of farmers were de jure greatly improved. The Timber Resources 
Management Act (1997; 4 (2d) )40 clearly states that "no timber rights shall be granted in respect of 
lands with farms without the authorisation in writing of the individual, group or owner concerned". In this 
respect farmers are "individuals concerned" as they cultivate the land (Cirelli, 1997; 65). It is, however, 
notable that the Concessions Act (1962; 1 and 16) still apply "with such modifications as may be 
necessary to give effect to the provisions ofthe Timber Resources Management Act (1997; 22)". This 
means that trees on stool land are still vested in the President (see sections 12.3, 12.3.6 and 
12.3.7). 
12.2.1 Current Rights to Revenue from Timber Harvest Off-Reserves 
Farmers rarely own the land they cultivate. Land is generally owned by the stool (Cirelli, 1997; 25). 
Furthermore, rights to natural assets on stool land that are not the result of differentiated identifiable 
human labour are held by chiefs on behalf of their people (FD, 1995a; 23). 
Revenue from timber exploitation must, according to the Constitution (1992; 267 (6», be shared 
between the stool, the traditional authority and the District Assembly (see section 12.1.2). Thus, the 
individual farmer has no direct right to a share of timber royalties, even though trees are extracted from 
the land he/she cultivates. 
In principle, the Timber Resources Management Act (1997; 4 (2d» gives a farmer full rightto refuse 
the felling of a timber tree which stands on land he/she cultivates. A farmer should, therefore, be in a 
position to negotiate a price with a logging company, on top of compensation of crop damage and forest 
taxes, for the right to fell the tree. Whether this is actually going to happen will fundamentally determine 
farmers' incentives to tend (not destroy) naturally occurring trees on land they cultivate, because the 
stool or the traditional authority are not legally required to and do not pay individual farmers for timber 
exploited on land they cultivate. 
12.2.2 Rights of the Forestry Department to Benefit from Off-Reserve Revenue 
After a long battle with the Lands Commission the Forestry Department in 1994 gained the right to 
collect off-reserve timber royalties (cf. section 9.1.1). However, the Timber Resources Management 
Regulations (1998; 25 (3) and 27) states that stumpage fees and rents from timber utilisation contract 
areas on stoollands41 must be collected by the Administrator of Stool Lands. 
As mentioned in section 12.1.2 it is not clear how big a share the Forestry Department can claim of on-
reserve stumpage fees. On areas off-reserves, the Forestry Department is intended to provide little 
management service other than a timber inventory, identification of areas suitable for award of timber 
rights and some control over logging operations (see 12.3.1). On this background it seems difficult to 
40 
41 
The date of assent for this Act was 17 March, 1998. 
Presumably excluding forest reserves where the District Forest Officer is 
supposed to collect stumpage fees. 
144

--- Page 169 ---

justify that the Forestry Department should receive a significant share of the revenue accruing form off-
reserve timber exploitation. 
Yet, an efficient control and monitoring oftimberexploitation provided by the Forestry Department may 
ensure that stumpage fees are actually paid. This would greatly benefit local communities and District 
Assemblies, of which the latter depend heavily on timber revenue (FD, 1995a; 69). The Lands 
Commission's efficiency in collecting royalties has probably not been very high, since it has never been 
able to produce any accounts for timber royalties collected up to 1994. 
12.3 Principles for Allocation of Rights to Standing Timber 
Until 1998 the legal basis for granting rights to standing timber was the Concessions Act (1962; 16). 
The procedures for granting timber concessions were specified in the Concession Regulations (1962), 
the Timber Leases and Licences Regulations (1962) and subsequent amendments of these. 
A key issue in the allocation of rights to standing timber through timber concessions (long-term rights 
to standing timber) or leases (short -term rights to standing timber) was that, save the costs of processing 
applications, such rights were free of other charges than the annual concession rent and other forest fees 
and taxes (cf. chapter 9). As documented in chapter 10 this left substantial room for unofficial payments 
and/or windfall profits. 
The World Bank did not agree with this policy, and made it conditional to the second trance release of 
funds for the Forest Resource Management Project that the allocation of concessions were made 
subject to a tendering process where 50% and subsequently 60% weight should be given to the highest 
bid (FRMP, 1988; 55). Even though this has never been implemented, the Ghanaian Government 
acknowledged it had a problem with the allocation oftimber concessions. At least this has been the case 
since the NIC investigations in 1991 brought up the issue of timber concessions being sub-let without 
the written consent of the Ministry (cf. section 10.6). 
In 1991, the then Ministry of Lands and Natural Resources (now the Ministry of Lands and Forestry) 
froze the granting of new timber leases and thereby, administratively, took over the responsibility of 
granting rights to standing timber from the Lands Commission, on grounds that the Lands Commission 
had a poor record on considerations of conservation issues. Yet, the Ministry did not manage to 
streamline the procedures. On the contrary, it seems, since indications of the Ministry being susceptible 
to lobbying and pressure began to crop up (Office of the President, 1993;1). 
In 1992, by order of the PNDC (the then military Government) the granting of timber leases was, 
temporarily, made the responsibility of the Chief of Staff, PNDC. He established, within the Ministry 
and including representatives from relevant agencies42, a timber concession committee to deal with the 
allocation of timber concessions (Office of the President, 1993;2). 
42 
Two from the Ministry, the Forestry Commission, one from the Lands Com-
mission, five from the Forestry Department, one the Forest Products Inspec-
tion Bureau, one from the Timber Export Development Board and one from 
the Timber Industry (AGTI) (lIED, 1993; 81). 
145

--- Page 170 ---

Apart from allocating concessions, the timber concession committee also worked on designing a new 
framework for the granting of rights to standing timber on- and off-reserves. The authority ofthe timber 
concession committee, established through ad hoc directives and administrative measures, to grant 
timber concessions could, in principle, be challenged by concessionaires, the AGTI and not least the 
Lands Commission which, according to the Constitution (1992; 258 (1 a) and 267 (3)), is the only body 
authorised to grant leases (Office of the President, 1993; 3). 
The need for a comprehensive legislation on rights to standing timber was, therefore, urgent. The Office 
of the President wanted the new legislation to avoid using the terms "lease" and/or "concession", as this 
would definitively prevent the Lands Commission from claiming authority over the granting of rights to 
standing timber. Furthermore, it was argued that timber leases and concessions are in any case different 
from other forms of leases and concessions because they cannot be used as security or collateral 
(Office of the President, 1993; 3). 
Much effort has since 1993 gone into developing what finally became the Timber Resources 
Management Act (1997) which legally defines the concepts of "timber utilisation contracts" and "timber 
utilisation permits". Following a number of drafts the Timber Resources Management Act (1997) came 
into force on 17 March, 1998. This Act was followed by the Timber Resources Management 
Regulations (1998) which came into force on 16 December, 1998. This new legislation on timber rights 
has introduced the following main concepts and principles: 
• 
A new formula for calculating timber royalties (stumpage fees) 
• 
new rates for contract area rents on- and off-reserves 
• 
a timber utilisation contract which is a long-term right to standing timber on a large area 
• 
the Forestry Department's duty to conduct surveys and timber inventories on- and off-reserves 
in order to identify suitable areas for timber utilisation contracts 
• 
a coordinated involvement ofland owners, and traditional and official authorities during the 
identification of timber utilisation contract areas off-reserves 
• 
the right of land owners to refuse a timber utilisation contract to cover their land off-
reserves 
• 
the obligation ofthe Forestry Departmentto obtain written consent ofland owners before an off-
reserve timber utilisation contract can be granted 
• 
pre-qualification criteria for applicants to timber utilisation contracts 
• 
a timber rights evaluation committee to process applications according to the pre-qualification 
criteria and to advise the Minister on the granting of timber utilisation contract 
• 
a minimal element of bidding for timber utilisation contracts, but not for timber utilisation permits 
• 
a termination of all previous timber concessions and leases after a period of maximum six months 
from the date where the Timber Resource Management Act (1997) comes into force 
• 
the right of the Minister, in consultation with the Forestry Commission, to direct such holders of 
timber utilisation contracts to supply the domestic market with timber products of specified 
volume, dimensions and species 
• 
a timber utilisation permit which is a short -term right to a specified number standing timber trees 
in an area not covered by a timber utilisation contract 
• 
the right of the Chief Conservator of Forests to grant timber utilisation permits 
• 
protection of farmers' rights to veto the felling of any tree on land they cultivate 
• 
protection of rights to trees grown by private individuals and groups 
146

--- Page 171 ---

• 
the right of the Minister, through legislative instrument, to make regulations for and prescribe 
conditions under which timber harvesting may take place inside and outside forest reser-
ves 
• 
the right of the Minister, through legislative instrument, to protect any tree or tree species that may 
be considered depleted, threatened or endangered. 
A key issue in the new legislation is that all trees on all land are still vested in the President. Sections 
I and 16 in the Concessions Act (1992) still apply, with such modifications as may be necessary under 
the new Act (Timber Resources Management Act, 1997; 22 (2)). This secures the right of the President 
to grant rights to timber on areas off-reserves, and the right of the Ministry of Lands and Forestry to 
allocate such timber rights, a right that might otherwise easily have fallen to the District, and Assemblies 
(FD, 1995a; 23). 
The principle of acquired rights under a timber utilisations contract not being transferable without the 
written consent of the Ministry of Lands and Forestry has also been maintained (Timber Resources 
Management Act, 1997; 16). Rights acquired through a timber utilisation permit are not transferable 
under any circumstances. 
12.3.1 New Timber Royalties and Contract Area Rents 
The new formula to calculate stumpage fees which have replaced timber royalties is described in section 
9.1.1 and the contract area rent has been considered in section 9.1.2. Here it may be repeated that 
particularly the stumpage fee system represents a major increase in timberrevenue and the package of 
fees and taxes appears to create appropriate incentives to support sustainable forest management and 
to develop the wood industry. 
12.3.2 Timber Utilisation Contracts 
It is envisaged that a timber utilisation contract should run for a period of 40 years inside forest reserves 
(coinciding with the felling cycle) and 5 to 20 years outside forest reserves (FD, 1995c). The Forestry 
Department will at certain intervals review timber operations, and logging companies must submit 
harvest plans every five years (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 14 (lg-h)).1t is 
paramount that a company's right to timber is acquired through a contract relation with the Ministry of 
Lands and Forests and that the conditions of the contract are specified by the Ministry. This underscores 
that the relationship is not one of equals and gives the Ministry authority to cancel the timber utilisation 
contract if a logging company does not live up to the specified conditions (Timber Resources 
Management Act, 1997; 8 and 15 & Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 14). 
The Forestry Department shall identify areas suitable for grant of timber utilisation contracts. On such 
areas, which may be inside or outside forest reserves, the Forestry Department will carry out surveys 
and timber inventories to verify the stocking (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 1). 
When suitable areas are identified, a field inspection must take place including two members of the 
concemed District Assembly, a representative of the Traditional Council, the District Forest Officer and 
two representatives of the land owners, of whom at least one must be a farmer. This team must through 
the Regional Forestry Officer submit to the Chief Conservator of Forests a report detailing on a 
topographic map the area inspected, topographic and man-made peculiarities of the area and the 
estimated quantity, quality and value of timber (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 2). 
147

--- Page 172 ---

When the Chief Conservator of Forests is satisfied that the inspected area is suitable for a timber 
utilisation contract he will, for lands other than public land and forest reserves, direct the District Forest 
Officer to seek the written consent and agreement of the owners of the land. This must be done subject 
to notices, posted at the concemed District Assembly, Traditional Council and Unit Area as well as at 
the District Forest Office, which identifies the proposed area. Any person claiming ownership over or 
interests in the land shall within 21 days inform the District Forest Officer on this. Should there be no 
objections to the proposed timber utilisation contract area, then the District Forest Officer must obtain 
the written consent of the land owners (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 4). 
In case of objections from any person claiming ownership over or other interests in the land, the District 
Forest Officer must refer the matter to a committee consisting of one representative of the Administrator 
of Stool Lands, two representatives of the concerned District Assembly and a representative of the 
Traditional Council who will be the committee chairman. This committee shall hear and consider all 
statements and within 14 days submit a report to the District Forest Officer, stating whether it considers 
the objections reasonable, and the committee shall also recommend on how the conflicting claims may 
be resolved. The District Forest Officer will add his comments to the report and send it to the Regional 
Forestry Officer. After the Regional Forest Officer has satisfied himself about the accuracy of the report 
and attached maps and plans he will add his comments and submit the case to the Chief Conservator 
of Forests. The Chief Conservator of Forests will, based on the report, seek to settle the conflict(s). 
However, no timber utilisation contract on private or stool land may be given out without the written 
consent of the owners (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 5-7). When there is a 
conflict of interests on public land identified as suitable for a timber utilisation contract, the issue must 
be resolved between the Minister of Lands and Forestry and any other Minister concerned (Timber 
Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 8). 
When timber utilisation contract areas have been identified and eventual conflicts resolved, the Chief 
Conservator of Forests will advertise such areas indicating, among other things, the estimated yield and 
call for applications. Three different sizes of timber utilisation contract areas will be made available. 
Small scale areas not exceeding 2,000 ha and only applicable off-reserves. Medium scale areas 
between 2,000 ha and 12,500 ha applicable on- and off-reserves. Large scale areas of more than 
12,500 ha applicable on-reserves only (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 9 (2b)). 
Applicants for timber utilisation contracts will be evaluated by a Timber Rights Evaluation Committee. 
This committee will consist of the Chief Conservator of Forests or his representative (chairman), the 
Forestry Department Planning Officer, the Chief Administrator of the Forestry Commission, the 
Administrator of Stool Lands, the representative of the Lands Commission on the Forestry Commission, 
a representative of the Ghana Institute of Professional Foresters and one other person appointed by the 
Minister (Timber Resources Management Act, 1997; 5). Accordingly, the timber industry will not be 
represented unless the Minister decides this. 
In addition to a number of formalities, applications for timber utilisation contracts will be evaluated 
according to the following criteria (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 11): 
• 
Intentions to further process logs extracted from the area; 
• 
number, qualifications, and competence of logging and other technical staff; 
• 
the timber production and operation record (if any) of the applicant; 
148

--- Page 173 ---

• 
previous timber rights (if any) held by the applicant; 
• 
the proposed harvest plan; 
• 
technical and financial capacity of the applicant to operate the area; 
• 
environmental effects of the proposed logging and the applicant's ability to redress such effects; 
• 
a fire protection plan; 
• 
projections of timber production, income and expenditure from operating the area; 
• 
road, logging and (where applicable) log processing facilities; 
• 
proposed measures to secure the area against illegal operations and encroachment; 
• 
plans for disposal of logging and milling residues. 
Points will be awarded for each of the above aspects according to a system to be decided by the 
Timber Rights Evaluation Committee, and applicants scoring above a certain limit (also to be decided) 
will be invited to submit proposals on (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 13): 
• 
A reforestation or afforestation plan for the establishment and management of forest plantations 
of at least 10 ha per 100 ha contract area; 
• 
a social responsibility agreement to assist inhabitants within the contract area with such amenities 
as shall be specified in the agreement, at a cost of not more than 5% of the annual royalty 
accruing from the operations under the timber utilisation contract. 
On this basis the Timber Rights Evaluation Committee will recommend to the Minister the highest 
scoring applicant to be awarded the contract (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 13 
(2)). In this way the bidding element of awarding timber utilisation contracts has been reduced to a 
minimum. 
While the criteria for assessing applicants' technical and financial ability to carry out a sound and 
profitable logging operation appear sensible enough, social aspects such as workers' safety and medical 
insurance are rather neglected. Furthermore, the system seems vulnerable to abuse as it will be difficult 
to develop an objective scale of points for many of the assessment criteria. Apart from mopping up 
revenue that may be available in excess of official forest fees and taxes, presumably the World Bank 
has been in favour of a competitive bidding element in the allocation of timber rights because the 
evaluation criteria are objective. The contract would go to the highest bid. 
Originally this was also the intention. In order to ensure that applicants for a timber utilisation contract 
had the capacity (technically, financially and socially) to operate on a large area, they were supposed 
to pass some pre-qualification criteria much like the ones listed above. The top five companies were 
then supposed to submit sealed bids specifying in US$ their annual willingness to pay per ha per year 
(in addition to royalties and all other and fees) for acquiring a given contract. The company placing the 
highest bid should be recommended to the Minister for obtaining the contract (FD, 1995c; 6, 
Werbrouck, 1993a, 1993b and Smith, 1993). 
The Timber Rights Bill (1996; 5 (2)) still spoke of a "winning bid" among pre-qualified applicants for 
timber utilisation contracts, but without specifying what kind of bidding process this referred to. In 
conclusion, it seems that, despite the strong feelings of the World Bank on the issue, bidding in terms 
of financial offers to be awarded timber rights was never politically acceptable to Ghana. 
149

--- Page 174 ---

12.3.3 Termination of Existing Timber Concessions and Leases 
As of 18 September, 1998 all existing timber concessions and leases have, in principle, become void, 
but holders of such rights were granted the option to apply for a timber utilisation contract on such areas 
(Timber Resources Management Act, 1997; 19). This must have been a hard blow for active 
concession holders, unless they could be reasonably sure to retain the right to timber on their old 
concessions and leases. In fact, Cirelli (1997; 65) warned against such a measure as it could prove 
rather unpopular and recommended to let existing concessions and leases continue, but subject to the 
new regulations and management procedures. 
Assuming that this cancellation of old timber concessions and leases has not been abused by the official 
system to put undue pressure on active firms, the measure has a commendable and undoubtedly 
deliberate motive. To get rid of speculative concession holdings which were illegally being sub-led to 
acti ve companies. Naturally, holders of such concession stand few chances of being rewarded their 
original areas because they do not have the technical ability to operate them. 
Considered in this light the new legislation seems a bold attempt to effectively redress the system of 
rights to standing timber once and for all. It also suggests that speculative concession holders have lost 
political influence. Furthermore, a complete shift to the new system of timber utilisation contracts closes 
all possibilities for concession and lease holders to claim that they are entitled to operate these areas 
according to the original conditions and not to the conditions stipulated in the Timber Resources 
Management Act (1997) and the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998). 
The currently moderate difference between the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber and the 
stumpage fees plus contract area rent should preclude rent seeking behaviour among officials invol ved 
in allocation of timber utilisation contracts. Rent seeking behaviour will, however, be a risk if stumpage 
fees and other taxes do not follow the industry's willingness to pay for standing timber. It is an 
improvement that stumpage fees are pegged to the export value of lumber, but in the longer term this 
will not be sufficient. Along with the expected improved efficiency of the processing industries, their 
willingness to pay for standing timber will increase. Unless this is captured by forest taxes and fees, the 
room for informal payments will grow and become a temptation that could undermine the credibility of 
the timber rights allocation system. 
12.3.4 Compulsory Supply of Timber to the Domestic Market 
It was mentioned in chapter 8 that the supply of timber to the domestic market could become difficult 
in the not so distant future unless the production of timber was increased or the export volume was 
reduced. This is obviously an issue of political concern, but there is also a strong political will to maintain 
the export revenue from timber exports. An attempt to address this dilemma is apparently being made 
by giving the Minister powers to direct holders of timber utilisation contracts to supply the domestic 
market with timber products of specified volume, dimensions and species (Timber Resources 
Management Regulations, 1998; 36). 
The social motive of securing Ghanaian timber for the Ghanaians is commendable, but will it work? As 
timber from Ghana is likely to fetch higher prices overseas than at the domestic market, processing
