companies would obviously try to avoid compulsory supply for domestic consumption. In addition, the 
law does not guarantee an equitable distribution among holders of timber utilisation contracts. It is the 
150

--- Page 175 ---

Minister who, in consultation with the Forestry Commission, selects the companies that must 
compulsorily supply the domestic market. 
In general, the market always finds a way (officially or unofficially) around political attempts to maintain 
a price differentiation system for similar products. A more viable way to secure timber for domestic 
consumption would probably be to give land owners and particularly farmers more rights over trees on 
their land and land they cultivate, so they could price the resource according to its actual commercial 
value. Unfortunately, the new legislation does not favour this solution, as off-reserve timber is still vested 
in the President. This is likely to undermine incentives for farmers to nurse medium-sized naturally 
occurring timber on land they cultivate (see sections 12.3.6 and 12.3.7). 
12.3.5 Timber Utilisation Permits 
The Chief Conservator of Forests may on an application by a District Assembly, town committee or 
any rural community group issue a timber utilisation permit to the applicant. A timber utilisation permit 
is exclusively for harvesting a specified number of trees on land that is not covered by a timqerutilisation 
contract. Any timber harvested or converted to lumber under a timber utilisation permit may only be 
used for social purposes and may not be sold or exchanged (Timber Resources Management 
Regulations, 1998; 37). It also appears that no royalties are supposed to be paid for timber harvested 
on timber utilisation permits. The obligation to pay stumpage fee and contract area rent is only mentioned 
for holders oftimber utilisation contracts (Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 25 (l)). 
As for the Minister's right to demand compulsory supply of timber to the domestic market, the social 
intention of timber utilisation permits appears commendable. Yet, the concept suffers from the same 
problem. The official system's attempt to control the commercial utilisation of a politically under-priced 
resource. 
How the Forestry Department should, in practice, be able to ensure that no timber from timber utilisation 
permits enters the commercial market is difficult to imagine. The law prohibits lumber produced by 
conversion of logs with chainsaw (chainsaw lumber) from being subject to any commercial exchange 
(Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 32). In practice, it is, therefore, made compulsory 
for holders of timber utilisation permits to use chainsaw lumber only, as they are barred from paying a 
saw mill for converting logs in kind, ie. in lumber or logs. Also, no conveyance certificate may be issued 
forchainsaw lumber, and all timber being transported shall be covered by a conveyance certificate 
(Timber Resources Management Regulations, 1998; 24). However, chainsaw lumber can be re-sawn 
or moulded, making it impossible to distinguish from other lumber, and the fact that no royalties should 
be paid on timber from timber utilisation permits produces an obvious incentive for finding commercial 
markets for it. 
Accordingly, timber utilisation permits could become an unofficial and illegal backdoor to cheap standing 
timber through collusion between saw mills and local communities. Only the Forestry Department's 
ability to monitor activities on timber utilisation permit areas, and to ensure that false conveyance 
certificates are not issued for logs or lumber from such areas, may prevent abuses. If the number of 
timber utilisation permits becomes significant, which can be expected over a few years, then the control 
functions of the Forestry Department could easily become overwhelming. 
151

--- Page 176 ---

12.3.6 Protection of Farmers' Rights 
"No timber rights shall be granted in respect oflands with farms without the authorisation in writing of 
the individual, group or owner concerned" (Timber Resources Management Act 1997; 4 (2d)). 
The individual farmer is, in other words, given a de jure right to veto felling of trees on land he/she 
cultivates. In principle, farmers may, therefore, set a price for allowing a certain tree to be felled. 
Whether this will de Jacto happen only time can tell, but without information campaigns and actual 
support to farmers who attempt to use their new rights the chances appear rather slim. This is 
particularly so because previous legislation, rules and practices have created a perception among 
farmers that they have no rights whatsoever to trees on land they cultivate. Furthermore, individual 
farmers may come under pressure by the Stool, the Traditional Council and the District Assembly. 
These bodies will all benefit from timber exploitation through their constitutional right to a share of the 
timber revenue, but they are not obliged to pay anything to the individual farmer. 
Had farmers been granted general ownership to trees on land they cultivate, very clear and unambiguous 
incentives for growing and tending naturally occurring timber trees on farmland would have been 
produced. Indigenous land and tree tenure before the colonial period actually supports that cultivators 
ofland should also hold rights to naturally occurring trees on such land (FD 1995a; 23). Yet, a return 
to pre-colonial rules would constitute a major change in the fortune of chiefs and District Assemblies. 
In addition, it would probably be in conflict with the constitutional provisions on sharing of stool land 
revenue (Cirelli, 1997; 25). 
To sum up, the strongest incentive for promoting off-reserve timber production, giving farmers full 
ownership over trees on farmland, appears impossible to implement for legal and cultural reasons. The 
probably next best (easiest) way to promote off-reserve timber production, to divest the President of 
rights in trees off-reserves, is not being pursued for political reasons. 
This consolidation offarmers' s [ackof rights to trees on land they cultivate is likely to become the root 
of many future forest policy problems in Ghana. Instead of creating clear and unambiguous incentives 
to nurse naturally occuning trees on farmland, the new legislation reflects a political preoccupation with 
rights to benefit from exploitation oftimber off-reserves until the resource is gone. This focus on timber 
extraction rather than production undermines the potentials of making farmers timber growers. 
Accordingly, the timber related income, which is not the result of a deliberate and planned promotion 
of timber production, to Stools, Traditional Councils and District Assemblies will dwindle together with 
the off-reserve timber resource. As a result the timber industry is likely to be short of raw 
material. 
12.3.7 Protection of Rights to Cultivated Trees 
To at least produce some incentive for growing trees off-reserves the new legislation states that "no 
timber rights shall be granted on lands with timber grown or owned by any individual [ or] group without 
the written authorisation of the individual, group or owner concerned" (Timber Resources Management 
Act, 1997; 4 (2b)). The term "grown" is not specified, but it must be assumed to cover planted trees 
and naturally occurring trees which have been nursed from sapling, ie. not mature and medium-sized 
naturally occurring trees which are or will soon become commercially valuable for the industry. 
The intention seems to be that, in time, all trees off-reserves will be planted or nursed by individuals or 
groups who will then have full ownership to such trees (FD, 1995a; 23). This is a very long but currently 
152

--- Page 177 ---

not particularly attractive investment perspective, considering that many naturally occurring mature and 
medium-sized timber tree are still found off-reserves. The industry's access to timber off-reserves will 
be secured through timber utilisation contracts. Local communities, NGOs and the like may get access 
to cheap timber through timber utilisation permits. So why grow trees on farmland? This will only 
become really attractive when other resources are running out. However, by then the market for at least 
export oriented industrial timber may have collapsed. 
Meanwhile, farmers are not likely to invest anything in nursing naturally occurring timber trees which are 
bigger than seedlings on land they cultivate. A lot of pole size timber trees which would need another 
20-50 years before they are suitable for industrial use will probably end up as firewood long before 
then. Timber utilisation contracts or not. In the end, no logging company or official institution can prevent 
farmers, who are the actual managers of trees off-reserves, from doing what they find fit. Protection 
without a guaranteed payment of existing medium-sized timber trees for the future benefit of the timber 
industry is not likely to rank high on any farmer's list of priorities. 
12.3.8 Protection of Forest Plantations 
The Timber Resources Management Act (1997; 4 (2a)) protects land with forest plantations from 
becoming part of a timber utilisation contract. At least this provides a minimum guarantee that 
investments in timber plantations will benefit the investor and not some other company which acquires 
rights to timber in a given area that happens to include a timber plantation (actually such guarantees did 
previously not exist). The question is, however, whether this is enough to initiate investments in timber 
plantations. 
Under the established incentive structure it seems likely that, unless investments in fairly big tree planting 
programmes off-reserves or inside degraded forest reserves are undertaken, industrial timber will be 
available from forest reserves only, once the existing naturally occurring off-reserve resource has been 
logged out. In terms of securing its resource base the domestic industry should, therefore, be interested 
in timber plantations since it cannot expect farmers to invest in planting or tending naturally occurring 
trees on farmland. 
Timber plantations would require long-term contracts between land owners and the investor plus 
substantial funds. Long-term contracts with land owners should be possible to organise (oil palm 
plantations have been established through such arrangements). Yet, funds for establishing timber 
plantations will only be available if the investment is likely to be profitable. 
This is the weak point. Investments in timber plantations are not likely to be profitable. Why? Mainly 
because the expected selling price of plantation logs is too low. Why? Because domestic log prices of 
competing species from forest reserves are kept artificially low through the log export ban and the 
package of forest taxes and fees (HTS, 1995, appendix D; 22). 
One might expect that timber plantations will be relati vely more valuable in the future than today, 
because domestic log prices are bound to rise when existing off-reserve timber resources are 
increasingly exhausted. Yet, in a controlled market it is basically up to the politicians to secure that this 
actually happens. In other words, the profitability of plantation investments is highly dependent on 
political decisions, which does not guarantee that prices will reflect supply and demand in a meaningful 
way. 
153

--- Page 178 ---

The study of HTS (1995) on plantation forestry in Ghana was a consultancy for ODA. One of the 
objectives was to assess the potentials for investing in plantation forestry in Ghana (mainly Teak) and 
consultations on this issue were held between HTS and the Commonwealth Development Corporation 
(HTS, 1995; 3).1t speaks for itself that the Commonwealth Development Corporation has decided not 
to invest in plantations in Ghana. 
In theory, plantation forestry might be profitable for domestic timber industries since they could use the 
produced logs as raw material instead of having to sell them at the domestic market. In practice, the 
industry is not particularly interested in plantation wood because its machinery and skills is geared for 
big diameter logs (Grut, 1996; 16). 
Already existing plantations have not received much interest from the industry either. In the early 1970s 
around 50,000 ha of mainly Teak plantations were established. Today it is estimated that only 
approximately 15,000 ha of these plantations have survived (FD, 1993; 4). About 50,000 transmission 
poles are annually taken out of these plantations and for the period 2005-20 15 an annual volume of 
approximately 300,000 m3 saw logs may be extracted from clear fellings of approximately 35 year old 
stands (FD, 1993; 5). This will, however, be relatively small dimension logs as it would probably require 
about 80 years to produce Teak of good quality with a high proportion of the valuable and durable 
heartwood (Grut, 1996; 15). 
Accordingly, existing Teak plantations are not likely to deliver logs that would be of interest to the 
current wood processing industries for another 60 years. Another option is to clear fell the stands at the 
age of35, but for which buyers? Since the resource might run out over a 10 year period, investments 
in new machinery specifically for small dimension timber appears rather unattractive. 
12.3.9 Powers of the Minister 
The rights of the Minister to prescribe conditions under which timber harvesting may take place inside 
and outside forest reserves are fairly elaborate in the Timber Resources Management Regulations 
(1998). They appear to represent a genuine attempt to avoid any unclarity on who has authority to 
define (i) rules for logging and (ii) conditions for timber utilisation contracts and permits. The Forestry 
Department has some bitter experience is this respect as logging companies have previously succeeded 
to legally outmanoeuvre attempts of the Department to enforce better logging practices. 
In 1992 the Forestry Department submitted a Logging Manual (FD, 1992) which all logging operations 
were supposed to follow. The manual generally stipulated sensible technical and environmental 
standards for good logging practice. Furthermore, it introduced a "completion certificate" which, subject 
to inspection by the District Forest Officer, should be issued for each logged-over compartment before 
the concession holder was allowed to begin logging in a new compartment (FD, 1992; 7). Yet, this 
never happened because the Logging Manual had no legal backing. A District Forest Officer told that 
he was "booted out of court" when in one case he tried to stop a contractor who refused to follow the 
Logging Manual (FD, 1992) (Personal information). In any case, the Forestry Department never tried 
very hard to enforce it. 
The rights of the Minister to protect trees and tree species which are considered endangered refer to 
various international treaties and conventions that Ghana is signatory to and to concerns over the 
depletion of different species. It should, for example, be possible for the Minister to ban the felling of 
species listed by CITES. Such rights are, however, not new. With reference to the Trees and Timber 
154

--- Page 179 ---

Decree (1974; 17 (1 f)), the harvest of Sub aha was in 1993 banned in order to protect banks of rivers 
and streams where this species is normally found. Special felling permits, which must be issued by the 
Chief Conservator of Forests, are today required for the following species (Timber Resources 
Management Regulations, 1998; 40): 
• 
Edinam (Entandophragma angolense). 
• 
Sapele (Entandophragma cylindricum). 
• 
Utile (Entandophragma utile). 
• 
Candollei (Entandophragma candollei). 
• 
Mahogany (Khaya anthothecalgrandifolialivorensis). 
• 
Odum (Milicia excelsalregia). 
• 
Kusia (Nauclea diderrichii). 
• 
Makore (Tieghmella heckelii). 
• 
Hyedua (Guibourtia ehie). 
• 
Afrormosia (Pericopsis elata). 
It is seen that all species except Candollei belong to the group of Scarlet Stars. As mentioned in section 
7.5.2 Candollei should be moved from the Red to the Scarlet Star group, so the list of species is very 
sensible. 
Whether the special felling permit system will serve to especially protect these species, which are all in 
very high demand, depends on how it is implemented. A constructive way of using the system would, 
for each of the restricted species, be to require that a certain minimum number of trees above a certain 
diameter must be situated on the same compartment as the tree for which a felling permit is requested. 
12.4 Summary Assessment of Rights to Timber and Related Benefits 
Past legislation has successively eroded the rights ofland-owning communities to benefits and revenue 
from forest reserves. Local communities' rights to extract forest produce have become lost or fuzzy 
because such rights were established when the forest reserves were declared some 50 years ago. 
Furthermore, extensive rights were given to the President to constitute forest reserves with or without 
the consent of the land owners and without paying any compensation to land owners (Administration 
of Lands Act, 1962; 7 (1-2)). 
Today the right to constitute forest reserves without paying compensation to land ownerS must be 
considered unconstitutional (Cirelli, 1997; 23 and Constitution, 1992; 20 (2a)). The rights ofland-
owning communities to extract forest produce is, however, an outstanding issue. It appears that the 
Forest Department would like to be given authority to control not only the extraction of timber but also 
all forest produce in order to secure a sustainable utilisation of the resource (FD, 1995a; 18). Another 
approach would be to grant land owners extensive rights to extract forest produce for domestic 
consumption, while allowing for some control of the Forestry Department over some produce for which 
there is a documented risk of over-exploitation. This would avoid a lot of unnecessary control functions, 
which the Forestry Department may not be able to lift anyway, and bring some substance to the original 
intention that forest reserves should the managed for the benefit of the land owners (Forest Ordinance 
(Cap. 157), 1927 (18; 2b). 
155

--- Page 180 ---

12.4.1 Revenue from Forest Reserves 
The right of land owning communities (stools or skins) to revenue accruing from forest reserves was with 
the Administration of Lands Act (1962; 17 (3 and 4)) reduced from minimum 67% of the gross revenue 
to what was left over after the Forestry Department had deducted the costs of its management. In 
practice, nothing was left for the land owners, so it was administratively decided to pay minimum 30% 
of the timber royalties from forest reserves to land owners. Yet, the Forestry Department apparently 
did not consider this decision binding, as it did not pay anything to many stools over a long 
period. 
The Constitution (1992; 267 (6)) clearly stipulates that stool land revenue, after a retention of 10% for 
administrative costs of the Administrator of Stool Lands Office, should be divided as follows: 
a) 
twenty-five per cent to the stool through the traditional authority for the maintenance of the 
stool in keeping with its status; 
b) 
twenty per cent to the traditional authority; 
c) 
fifty-five per centto the District Assembly, within the area of authority of which the stool 
lands are situated. 
The ownership ofland did not change when forest reserves were constituted (Forest Ordinance (Cap. 
157), 1924; 18 (I)), so practically all forest reserves are situated on stool land. Accordingly, the 
retention of funds by the Forestry Department to cover the costs of management probably became 
unconstitutional after 1992. The continued practice of the Forestry Department to retain revenue from 
forest reserves was, therefore, probably illegal. 
In December, 1998 legal basis to pay the Forestry Department for management services out of timber 
revenue was provided in the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998; 21 and 26). It is not 
specified whether such payments should be deducted from stool land revenue at large or from forest 
reserve revenue only. Neither does the new legislation set a maximum limit for how much may be 
deducted from stumpage fee revenue to finance the Forestry Department. 
Unless (i) the revenue from timber exploitation is increased significantly, (ii) the cost of running the 
Forestry Department is reduced significantly or (iii) off-reserve revenue may be used to fund the 
Forestry Department, there will not be much revenue left for local communities who own land on which 
forest reserves are located. The new system of stumpage fees and contract area rent is not likely to 
generate much more than DM 8.6 million annually from forest reserves in the near future (assuming the 
harvest is held within the AAC). If the annual costs of running the Forestry Department is approximately 
DM 7.5 million, this would leave less than 10% of the revenue for the land owners unless other sources 
may contribute to finance the costs of forest management (cf. section 11.1). Whether this is reasonable 
is a political decision, but it is quite far from the original minimum of 67% defined in the Forest 
Ordinance (Cap 157) (1927, 18 (2)). 
Revenue from forest reserves may in the longer term be higher than DM 8.6 million per year, since the 
stumpage fees could be gradually increased as could the harvest of currently "under-utilised" species. 
Yet, it appears crucial that the Forestry Department receives funding through other sources than timber 
revenue from forest reserves if it is to maintain any credibility in relation to local communities who 
actually own the land carrying forest reserves. After all the Forest Ordinance (Cap. 157) (1927; 18 
156

--- Page 181 ---

(2b)) says that when the Government is managing forest reserves it should be done "for the benefit of 
the owner or owners". 
Naturally, it is not only the land owners' rights, but also the conservation of Ghana's forest resources 
which are at stake. It would seem bizarre if on-reserve timber exploitation could not be controlled 
because the national forest authority cannot be adequately financed. Yet, it is also reasonable that a 
national forest authority is not over-staffed or over-financed at the expense of the land-owning 
communities. In addition, it must be ensured that the forest authority is held accountable for the standard 
of on-reserve forest management. 
12.4.2 Revenue from Off-Reserve Areas 
It is not clear how big a share of off-reserve stumpage fees the Forestry Department can claim. In fact, 
the new legislation seems to be deliberately held in unspecific terms concerning how the Forestry 
Department may receive funding through stumpage fees. Off-reserves, the Forestry Department is 
intended to provide little management service other than a timber inventory, identification of areas 
suitable for award of timber rights and some control over logging operations. On this background it 
seems difficult to justify that the Forestry Department should receive a significant share of the revenue 
accruing from off-reserve timber exploitation. 
Nevertheless, the new legislation appears to be designed such that the Forestry Department may claim 
a fairly big share of the timber revenue accruing from areas off-reserves. Otherwise it would, as 
indicated above, not be in a position to fund its management of forest reserves and pay a reasonable 
share of the on-reserve timber revenue to land-owning communities. 
Today timber grown or owned by any individual or group is protected from being included in a timber 
utilisation contract area and farmers' right to veto felling of trees on land they cultivate has also been 
given full legal backing (Timber Resources Management Act, 1997; 4 (2)). However, the Timber 
Resources Management Act (1997; 21) has maintained the general vestiture of trees on stool land in 
the President. This secures that the authority to grant rights to naturally occurring trees off-reserves 
remains with the Government. As a result, the authority to regulate and allocate such rights stays with 
the Forestry Department. The Forestry Department can, therefore, claim that it is entitled to a share of 
off-reserve stumpage fees in accordance with the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998; 
12 (2)), because it is managing and regulating off-reserve timber resources. 
12.4.3 Poor Incentives to Tend Natural Trees on Farmland 
By securing the Forestry Department a formal right to off-reserve timber revenue the Timber Resources 
Management Act (1997) and the Timber Resources Management Regulations (1998) consolidate 
farmers' lack of rights to currently valuable trees on land they cultivate. Instead of creating clear and 
unambiguous incentives to nurse naturally occurring trees on farmland, the new legislation seems to 
reflect a political preoccupation with rights to benefit from off-reserve timber exploitation until the 
resource is gone. This focus on extraction rather than production undermines the potentials of making 
farmers timber growers. Accordingly, timber related income to Stools, Traditional Councils and District 
Assemblies will dwindle together with the off-reserve timberresource. As a result, the timber industry 
is likely to be short of raw material. 
It may be argued that the constitutional provisions on sharing of stool land revenue rule out the strongest 
incentive for promoting off-reserve timber production, giving farmers full ownership over trees on 
157

--- Page 182 ---

farmland. However, the probably next best (easiest) way to promote off-reserve timber production, to 
divest the President of rights in trees off-reserves, has not been pursued either. Political concerns over 
how to finance the Forestry Department appear to be an underlying political reason. 
12.4.4 Supply of Timber to the Domestic Market and for Export 
The dual objective of being able to meet the future domestic demand fortimber and to maintain foreign 
exchange eamings from wood exports is rightfully a source of concern. The AAC of approximately 0.85 
million rn3 from forest reserves alone will obviously not be able to serve both objectives, since the 
domestic demand alone is already some 0.70 million m3 (cf. section 8.2). 
The Government's approach to this problem is an attempt to control the domestic timber market and 
the off-reserve timber exploitation rather than one of creating incentives for increasing the off-reserve 
timber production. On one hand, the Government is subsidising export of value added products through 
log export bans and forest fees that result in domestic log prices which are significantly lower than the 
FOB log prices. On the other hand, the Government reserves the right to direct holders of timber 
utilisation contracts to supply wood products to the domestic market (Timber Resources Management 
Regulations, 1998; 36). 
Whether such a system can actually work when it becomes necessary (when the sustainable supply of 
raw logs cannot meet the combined export driven and domestic demand) appears doubtful. The obvious 
alternative, over-exploitation of the remaining resource, will definitely be a risk. 
In any case, the very introduction of rights to demand compulsory supply of wood products to the 
domestic market shows that the Government is aware that the production of timber may not be able to 
keep up with the demand. The irony is that strong incentives for sustaining a substantial off-reserve 
timber production have deliberately not been established. Presumably, the background is (at least partly) 
a wish to secure the Forestry Department a right to off-reserve timber revenue such that it can finance 
the management of forest reserves (cf. sections 12.4.2 and 12.4.3). 
Establishment of plantations to prevent future shortages of timber is not likely to be undertaken and plans 
of establishing big mainly Teak plantations through foreign investments have recently been scraped. The 
main reason appears to be that the profitability of plantations is undermined by the artificially low 
domestic log prices on naturally occurring species, with which wood from plantations would have to 
compete. The domestic industry is not geared to process small diameter plantation logs, which is a 
reason for it not to invest in plantations, at least not in the foreseeable future. 
Accordingly, the medium long-term timber supply scenario is likely to show: 
• 
Decreasing volumes being extracted from areas off-reserves as the resource dwindles; 
• 
substantial volumes of medium-sized naturally occurring timber trees on farmland being cleared 
for non-industrial purposes; 
• 
inadequate investments in plantations to keep up with the combined demand for domestic 
consumption and wood exports. 
Assuming that existing medium-sized timber off-reserves will be cleared for other than industrial 
purposes (which seems likely), the lifetime of Scarlet Star species off-reserves may only be 15 years 
(cf. section 7.6). By that time plantations would not be yielding any saw logs even if they were 
158

--- Page 183 ---

established today. The approximately 15,000 haof reasonably good condition Teak plantations may 
offer some temporary relief, but the stands are generally less than 25 years old. Even 20 years from 
today, saw logs from these plantations will still be of small dimension compared with the indigenous 
timber that the industry is geared to handle. Overall, the industry is, therefore, likely to become 
increasingly desperate for logs to maintain export earnings. Meanwhile, the domestic demand for timber 
is likely to have reached some one million m3• This is approximately 0.15 million m3 above the on-
reserve AAC. 
12.4.5 Conditions for Managing Forest Reserves 
Following the above analysis, a tremendous pressure on forest reserves for timber may be expected 
within the coming 10-15 years. Whether the Forestry Department will be able to control the on-reserve 
timber harvest is an open question. In terms of harvest control the recent record of the Forestry 
Department is not exactly impressing, so it is paramount that the supply-side regulative function of the 
Forestry Department is re-habilitated. 
The Timber Resources Management Act (1997) and the Timber Resources Management Regulations 
(1998) appear to provide a reasonably good framework for managing on-reserve timber harvest. The 
right to define standards for logging operations is clearly with the official authorities and punishments for 
breaking the rules and regulations are quite severe for both loggers and officials. 
Positive incentives, such as reasonable salaries, for officials to efficiently perform logging control on the 
ground are yet to be established. This is pursued through lifting the existing Forestry Department out of 
the civil service system and making it a semi-autonomous body, to be named the Ghana Forest Service. 
Among other things, this would allow for paying employees higher wages than under the civil service 
structure. 
However, the combination of (i) an AAC from forest reserves which is expected to be below the 
domestic demand in about 10 years, (ii) a high demand for raw logs from a wood industry that is 
politically strongly encouraged to be export oriented and (iii) poor incentives to invest in timber 
production, presents a serious risk of failure no matter how well structured and well motivated a future 
Forest Service might be. This is likely to become the cardinal problem for managing the forest reserves. 
159

--- Page 184 ---

13 Forest and Timber Resources Management in the Future 
During the implementation of the Forest Resource Management Project and other associated forestry 
sector related projects, a process of revising the 1948 forest policy took place. It was not difficult to 
see that the 1948 policy on several points had been outdated for some time. Particularly the 
environmental and economic wisdom of continuing a deliberate deforestation of areas outside forest 
reserves to make way for agriculture had come into question. The growth of the timber industry, its 
impact on the resource base as well as its economic importance and potential had also created a need 
for re-assessing and clarifying the policy on commercial timber utilisation. Furthermore, the philosophy 
of enhancing public participation in forest and timber resources management had increasingly gained 
ground in decision-making circles. 
Finally in 1994, following numerous workshops and stakeholder consultations, the Ministry of Lands 
and Forestry submitted a new Forest and Wildlife Policy (1994), which stipulates the principles for 
development of the forestry sector. It is recognised that, during the past decade, the Governments' 
attempts to stimulate wood processing and exports have "yielded varied results", which, is explained 
by inadequate institutional capacity (Forest and Wildlife Policy, 1994; 4). The previous chapters in the 
present study suggest that a more direct way of putting it would be to state that, during the past decade, 
commercial timber harvest has led to over-exploitation of the resource and yielded questionable 
economic results for the country. 
In any case, the 1994 Forest and Wildlife Policy is very comprehensive and rather detailed on aims and 
objecti ves of managing the forest resource, not least in relation to commercial timber production. 
Reference is also made to the Intemational Tropical Timber Organisation, of which Ghana is a member, 
and its "Target 2000" aimed at ensuring that all trade in tropical timber will be sourced from sustainably 
managed forests by the year 2000 (Forest and Wildlife Policy, 1994; 5). 
The overall aim of the 1994 Forest and Wildlife Policy is "conservation and sustainable development 
of the country's forests and wildlife resource for maintenance of the environmental quality and perpetual 
flow of optimum benefits to all segments of society" (Forest and Wildlife Policy, 1994; 8). With respect 
to different stakeholder groups, the policy objectives and strategies may be summarised as 
follows: 
The Timber Industry: 
• 
The private sector is acknowledged as the engine of economic growth; 
• 
promotion of responsible resource utilisation through long-term concessions; 
• 
allocation of rights to standing timber on the basis of competitive bidding among properly 
equipped processors and entrepreneurs; 
• 
encouragement of more rational resource utilisation and in particular production of value added 
products for export through forest fees and taxes that are regularly reviewed to reflect the value 
of the resource and to recover optimum revenue for resource management; 
• 
transformation from a high volume low value to a low volume high value sector through promotion 
of secondary and tertiary processing; 
• 
development of the marketability of lesser used timber species. 
160

--- Page 185 ---

Institutions: 
• 
Forest fees and taxes to be regularly reviewed to reflect the value of the resource and to recover 
optimum revenue for resource management; 
• 
effective capability to be developed at national, regional and district level to ensure sustainable 
management of forest and wildlife resources; 
• 
unreserved forests to be included under the Forestry Department's management system for 
regulating timber harvest and collection of forest revenue; 
• 
reorganising the Forestry and Wildlife Departments as autonomous or semi-autonomous agencies 
with a view to upgrade staff performance and to improve monitoring, coordination and 
accountability. 
Local People: 
• 
Rights to have access to natural resources for maintaining a basic standard of living; 
• 
incorporation of traditional methods of resource management where appropriate; 
• 
rights to a share of the financial benefits arising from resource utilisation; 
• 
rights to be involved in matters concerning their welfare; 
• 
consultative and participatory mechanisms to be developed with a view to enhance land and tree 
tenure for farmers; 
• 
support with respect to management and legal protection of natural resources which local 
communities decide to protect and manage for domestic and economic purposes. 
Concerning the resource it is the objective of the 1994 Forest and Wildlife Policy to: 
• 
Protect and manage areas already set aside for permanent forest and wildlife conserva-
tion/preservation; 
• 
expand the conservation area through a dedication of various land categories with potential for 
nature protection and production of timber and other products; 
include off-reserve timber resources under Forestry Department's management system for 
regulating timber harvest and collection of forest revenue. 
13.1 Development of the Timber Industry 
As described in chapters 10 and 11 the intentions on developing the timber industry have to a large 
degree been followed up by the Timber Resources Management Act (1997) and the Timber Resources 
Management Regulations (1998). The system of awarding rights to standing timber has been revamped 
in a way that should benefit active and dedicated companies, although the bidding element has, in effect, 
been scraped, which could make the system somewhat vulnerable to abuse. 
The forest fee and taxation system has also undergone a highly needed transformation that (i) links 
stumpage fees to the FOB value of air-dried lumber, (ii) differentiates adequately between species 
according to their abundance and (iii) has increased the taxation level significantly such that forest taxes 
and fees almost equal the current willingness of the industry to pay for standing timber. In combination 
with the log export ban, forest taxes and fees, therefore, produce clear incentives for further and more 
efficient processing. 
161

--- Page 186 ---

The log export ban and the current level of forest fees and taxes secure cheap logs for the domestic 
industries. Accordingly, a transformation of the timber industry from a high volume low value to a low 
volume high value industry will to a high degree depend on how quickly the gap between domestic and 
FOB log prices can be closed. 
The log export ban and the forest fees are subsidising domestic processing by keeping domestic log 
prices below world market prices, but by doing so incentives to grow timber or invest in tending 
naturally occurring trees are obviously reduced. This anti-production effect is even amplified through the 
rules and regulations on rights to standing timber (see section 13.3). In fact, it seems that no matter how 
efficient the industry may become over the next decade, it could easily be short of raw material if 
incentives to produce timber are not improved. 
13.2 A Semi-Autonomous Forest Service 
The Forestry Department should be lifted out ofthe civil service system and restructured under a new 
name, The Ghana Forest Service. It is not considered possible to create an effective nati~mal forestry 
institution without loosening it from the constraints associated with being a part of the civil service sector. 
In particular, salary levels are critical in this respect. 
The financial base for a new Forest Service has obviously be improved through the stumpage fee 
system. Yet, as illustrated in table 11.1 the annual costs of running a new Forest Service are likely to 
be approximately DM 7.5 million, excluding development costs (Touche Ross, 1995; 21). This is almost 
90% of the expected revenue from forest reserves, which would leave the new Forest Service with a 
credibility problem if additional sources of income cannot be found. To this end it appears that the 
intention is to claim a share of the off-reserve timber revenue with reference to the legally defined 
regulatory functions of the Forestry Department (cf. section 12.4.2). Reducing the costs of running the 
Forestry Service may, theoretically, be another option, but for political reasons this is not likely to 
happen as it would require a sizable reduction of the number of employees. 
Financially, the viability of a new Forest Service will, therefore, depend on a sustained flow of funds 
from off-reserve areas. This is a big challenge as the incentives for investing in a sustained timber yield 
from off-reserve areas are currently rather poor. Individual farmers are not likely to invest in naturally 
occurring trees from which the Forestry Service could claim a share of the stumpage fee. Existing 
plantations may generate some temporary revenue for the Forestry Service, but new plantations, if ever 
established, are likely to be private. The only viable solution seems to be a massive prontotion of 
dedicated forests (see sections 13.4 and 13.4.2). 
13.3 Interests of Local People 
Existing rights oflocal people to extract goods (NTFPs) from forest reserves date back to when forest 
reserves were established, so they have become increasingly difficult to verify. As the 1994 Forest and 
Wildlife Policy re-affirms the original rights af local people to draw subsistence benefits from forest 
reserves, it would seem appropriate to grant communities, who own the land on which forest reserves 
are situated, extensive rights to extract goods from the reserves. 
Granting extensive rights of managing NTFPs to the Forest Service, including the right to collect fees 
from NTFPs, would not be in the spirit of the 1994 Policy on Forests and Wildlife which requires 
incorporation of traditional methods of resource management where appropriate. In order to avoid 
162

--- Page 187 ---

over-exploitation of certain goods, the new Forest Service should be obliged to develop participatory 
plans for managing NTFPs on-reserves. Land-owning communities' rights to a share of the financial 
benefits from timber utilisation have been re-affirmed in the Timber Resources Management Regulations 
(1998; 21 (2)). How big this share is going to be will be decided by the Minister for Lands and Forestry 
in consultation with the Forestry Commission, the Forestry Department, and the Administrator of Stool 
Lands. The latter institution is presumably supposed to represent the interests of the land owners. Yet, 
the arrangement appears undemocratic and more concerned with securing a legal basis for funding the 
Forestry Department (or a future Forest Service) than securing a minimum share of the financial benefits 
from timber harvest to the land owners. What if the land-owning communities are not satisfied with the 
share of stumpage fees they are awarded? According to which legislation can they file a case? 
The Timber Resources Management Act (1997) and the Timber Resources Management Regulations 
(1998) have institutionalised consultative and participatory mechanisms for identification of timber 
utilisation contract areas. Enhancement of farmers' rights to land and trees is limited to veto felling of 
trees on land they cultivate, but it is even doubtful ifthis right can, in practice, be exercised. In fact, it 
appears that the 1994 Forest and Wildlife Policy is promising more than it can keep in terms of granting 
farmers more rights to land and trees, since the Constitution (1992; 267) clearly vests all stool lands in 
the stools and stipulates stool land revenue to be shared between stool, the traditional authority and the 
District Assembly. In addition, the Timber Resources Management Act (1997; 22) has maintained the 
vestiture of naturally occurring trees off-reserves in the President. 
Support with respect to management and legal protection of natural resources, which local communities 
decide to protect and manage for domestic and economic purposes, is still to be institutionalised and 
legally backed. Areas which local communities wish to set aside for protection of flora and fauna or 
culturaVreligious reasons should be relatively straightforward to legally back if this does not involve large 
areas which would otherwise be deemed suitable for an off-reserve timber utilisation contract. 
Areas which local communities wish to set aside for their own economic purposes are likely to become 
a more thorny issue, at least as far as timber production is concerned. Nevertheless, it appears crucial 
that solutions which are attractive for the land owners are found. Otherwise, the supply of timber could 
soon be too low to meet both the domestic and export driven demand. This would undermine foreign 
exchange earnings from wood exports and make it difficult to keep the on-reserve harvest within the 
AAC. In addition, the source of revenue that off-reserve timber harvest has represented to stools, 
traditional authorities, District Assemblies and other official bodies like the Forestry Department will dry 
out. 
13.4 Management of Forest and Timber Resources 
It will be difficult for the Government to establish more forest reserves, as this would require substantial 
compensation payments to the land owners (cf. section 12.1.1). Enhancing nature and timber 
conservation is rather going to be pursued through dedicated forests, a new concept introduced by the 
Forest and Wildlife Policy (1994; 5 (3)). Dedicated forests and other nature areas are to be set aside 
by the land-owning communities for protection of natural resources and cultural heritages like sacred 
groves (FD, 1995a; 9). The establishment of such areas appears a flexible and cost effective way of 
enhancing the conservation of forests of cultural and religious importance off-reserves. 
163

--- Page 188 ---

13.4.1 Production of Timber in Dedicated Forests 
Dedicated forests are envisaged to be set aside by the land owners (notthe District Assemblies) and 
managed by them in accordance with a plan which the Forestry Department must endorse (FD, 1995a; 
20). The Forestry Department could also, subject to a reasonable fee, deliver technical assistance to 
prepare management plans for dedicated forests. Yet, as recommended by FD (1995a; 28), the right 
to naturally occurring trees on stool land is still vested in the President (Timber Resources Management 
Act, 1997; 22). The financial incentives of creating dedicated forests for timber production are, 
therefore, not clear. At least not yet. 
Guarantees against dedicated forests being included in timber utilisation contract areas must naturally 
be provided. In addition, it is obvious that land owners will require additional rights to trees in dedicated 
forest where long-term timber production is one of the management objectives. Otherwise it would be 
rather pointless for them to consider timber production. A full transfer to the land owners of ownership 
to all trees in dedicated forests would produce the best incentives for timber production, but this is likely 
to run against the constitutional provisions on how revenue from stool land must be shared between the 
land-owning stool, the traditional authority and the District Assembly (Constitution, 1992; 267 
(6)). 
Accordingly, the only way to promote a sustained timber yield from dedicated forests is through a 
flexible interpretation of the term "stool land revenue". In order to stimulate off-reserve timber 
production it is paramount that provisions are made for deducting a fee to the land owners for forest 
management from the gross revenue before the rest is distributed in accordance with the constitutional 
provisions. 
13.4.2 Off-Reserve Timber; The Key to Sustainable Forest Management 
It should be a top priority of the Ministry of Lands and Forests to ensure that adequate financial 
incentives are established for local communities to manage off-reserve forest and timber resources. In 
the medium-long term this is the only feasible way to secure a sustained flow of timber for export and 
domestic consumption. Four fundamental facts support this conclusion: 
• 
The AAC from forest reserves is currently slightly higher than the domestic demand, but this is 
likely to change in a few years; 
• 
existing plantations are not big enough to substitute the current off-reserve harvest, and new 
plantations, if ever established, need minimum 40 years before they will yield saw logs; 
• 
farmers have currently no incentives to tend medium-sized naturally occurring trees on land they 
cultivate; 
• 
The stock of Scarlet Star species off-reserves is likely to be logged out within the next 15 years 
if incentives to produce timber off-reserves are not significantly improved. 
These are the core problems of managing timber resources in the high forest zone and they should be 
carefully considered in relation to the likely demand for timber in the medium-long term. There are 
undoubtedly strong interests invested in maintaining the revenue from wood exports. 
164

--- Page 189 ---

Realistically this renders a drastic reduction of the current export harvest impossible43• At the same time 
domestic timber demands will undoubtedly be met officially or unofficially. 
Accordingly, the feasibility of implementing sustainable management of forest reserves relies heavily on 
the degree to which a sustained timber yield from off-reserve areas may be realised. Dedicated forests 
are the only realistic option. 
In the process towards sustainable forest and timber resources management, decision-makers may have 
acknowledged that there will be no timber without trees. Now they are well advised to look beyond this 
obvious fact and realise that there will be no trees for timber if there are no incentives to grow 
them. 
43 
In 1996 the export harvest was almost 1,000,000 m', of which a minimum of 
570,000 m' were Scarlet Star species. 
165

--- Page 190 ---

A 15% under-reporting of the harvest on-reserves is,

column "not specified" are allocated to on- and off-reserves 
by the ratios of 1992.

11 Poles are not Included as they consist of plantation Teak for which reliable data on growth the total volume harvested is not available 
Exports for the penod 1972-85 are probably on the low side as only logs and lumber are inCluded. 
Apparent domestic consumption IS a crude estlmale associated with some factors of uncertainty: 
_ Changes In stocks are not specified which means that stock increases are taken as apparent domestic consumption 
_ The assumed recovery rates, especially the one for 'AII others', are rough estimates 
_ ills assumed that 55% of residues are re-processed for domestic markets other then firewood and charcoal 
_ In addition to recorded extracllon some illegal harvest takes place of which most is likely to be consumed domestically 
Harvest of plantation wood, where Teak is the major speCIes, is not Included as reliable extraction figures are not available.
